# INTRODUCTION

The Kett  oabye ia aai most famous of all the private presses. Founded in 1891 as a 'typoraphical venture'orris  William Bowden,  Januar 1 [pL), the Press was the last major ndertaking by William Morris in his remarkable career as a designer and reviver of traditional crafts. By emphasising the fundamental importance of excellent ink and paper (the latter hand-made), well-designed typefaces (which he himse created), and handsome ornaments and illustrations, Morris reinvigorated the book arts and produced a series of fifty-two books that are still widely admired and collected. After his death in 1896, most of the books already in production were completed, but the Press finally closed in the spring of 1898.

This bibliography describes the published works of the Kelmscott Press, books that were contemplated but were not actually printed, and advertising circulars and other ephemera; a final section supplies the texts of eight contracts and memoranda for Kelmscott books. Since the history of the Kelmscott Press has been told so many times (though not always accurately), in this introduction I will touch only upo certai poit  spe tet,awn  muc as possible primary sources such as diaries and letters.

## I. SOURCES

The chiesourc o informatin about the Kelmscott Pres is Syney (later Sir Sydney) C. Cockerell, second Secretary of the Press. Cockerell wrote the brief official history of the Press, compiled a checklist of its publications, preserved many documents that might otherwise have been lost, and kept a detailed diary during the years in which the Press existed. Cockerell's 'A Short History and Description of the Kelmscott Press' and 'An Annotated List of All the Books Printed at the Kelmscott Press in the Order in Which They Were Issued', both of which appeared in the last volume (As3) produced at the Press, are the foundations on which many later writers have built. (That volume also contained 'A Note by William Morris on his Aims in Founding the Kelmscott Press'.)

Another history of the Press with special authority is The Kelmscot Prss and William Morris Master-Craftsman (192) by H. Halay

Sparling, who was (briefly) Morris's son-in-law and the first Secretary of the Press. Though ampler than Cockerell's treatment, Sparling's book does not carry the same intellectual weight and is weakened by the author's eagerness to demonstrate that he is an eager and adoring disciple' of Morris (as he phrases it in his preface).

A more obscure, but nevertheless significant, source is a lecture by Frank Colebrook, editor of the Printing Times and Lithographer, entitled William Morris: Master Printer (Tunbridge Wells, [?1897]), much of which is based on the recollections of William H. Bowden, overseer of Morris's printers, and others connected with the Kelmscott Press.

Among more recent treatments of the Press, the most important is William Morris and the Art of the Book (New York, 1976), which grew out of an exhibition at the Pierpont Morgan Library of the Morris collection of John M. Crawford, Jr. (The immense Crawford collection is now owned by the Morgan Library.) The volume, however, exceeds one's usual expectations of a catalogue: aside from other riches, it contains essays on Morris as a book collector by Paul Needham, on Mori  h  oDuap,Mi grapher by John Dreyfus. Another catalogue of value, with excellent notes by Ronald Briggs, is The Typographical Adventure of William Morris, which records an exhibition sponsored by the William Morris Society in 1958.

Harry Buxton Forman's The Books of William Morris (1897) offers, rather unsystematically, a good deal of miscellaneous information about the Kelmscott books written by Morris. William Morris in Private Press and Limited Editions: A Descriptive Bibliography of Books by and about William Morris, 18911981 (Phoenix, Ariz., 1983), which is a bibliographical catalogue rather than a true descriptive bibliography, records the excellent private collection of John J. Walsdorf, Jr. Two good short accounts of the Kelmscott Press are found in Colin Franklin, The Private Presses (1969), pp. 3549, and Roderick Cave, The Private Press, 2nd ed. (New York, 1983), pp. 10312.

Morris's typographical writings have been collected in The Ideal Book: Essays and Lectures on the Arts of the Book by William Morris, edited by William S. Peterson (Berkeley, 1982). An appendix reprints four iviews wih Morris about i rk s a piter. Thest volu otre-olu Morricorndece,di y Norman Kelvin, is scheduled to appear in 1984.

Joseph Dunlap, in an unpublished doctoral thesis (Columbia University, 1972), has examined The Road to Kelmscott: William Mor-and the Book Arts before the Founding  the Kelscott Press

His major conclusions have been published in an article in Victorian P 3 (FalWinter, 1975), 14157. One  the sgiicant aspect o MorrrKee ivi as hitt i cl: see the essay by Dunlap in the Morgan catalogue, cited above, and Alfred Fairbank, 'A Note on the Manuscript Work of William Morris' in The Story of Kormak the Son of Ogmund, translated by Morris and Eirikr Magnússon (1970).

In American Book Design and William Morris (New York, 1977) Susan O. Thompson has explored Morris's immense and previously unassessed transatlantic influence. Among other specialised studies one might cite Colin Franklin, 'On the Binding of Kelmscott Press Books', Journal of the William Morris Society, 2 (Summer, 1978), 2830, and John Dreyfus, 'New Light on the Design of Types for the Kelmscott and Doves Presses', The Library, 5th Ser., 29 (March 1974), 3641. On the subject of the Kelmscott Press Press types, Thomas Balston's The Cambridge University Collection of Private Press Tγpes (Cambridge, 1951), pp. 1019, is also instructive. Morris's correspondence with his papermaker is published in Sydney Ward, 'William Morris and His Papermaker, Joseph Batchelor', Philobiblon, 7 (1934), 17780, and in Henry Morris, A Visit to Hayle Mill (North Hills, Pa., 1970), pp. .

Burne-Jones's designs for the Chaucer have been reproduced and analysed by Duncan Robinson in the companion volume of the Basilisk Press facsimile (1974) of the Chaucer; Robinson's text, with some of the illustrations, is reprinted in William Morris, Edward Burne-Jones and the Kelmscott Chaucer (1982).

Three studies of figures associated with the Press are worth mentioning: Colin Franklin's Emery Walker: Some Light on His Theories of Printing and on His Relations with William Morris and Cobden-Sanderson ge 9Fc vidw hil riTpe Punchcutter (1967), and Wilfrid Blunt's Cockerell (New York, 1965).

May Morris's recollections of her father's activities at the Press are to be found in an article, 'Reminiscences about My Father', Philobiblon, 7 (1934), 16972, and in her introductions to his collected works, which have been separately reprinted (New York, 1973).

At his death Morris owned a large collection of incunabula and reference books about printing and typographical design: the fullest list of his books is in the Sotheby auction catalogue, 5-Io December 1898. Similarly, a catalogue of Cockerell's Kelmscott Press collection (Sotheby, 1o December 1956) provides an invaluable list of proofs, trial pages, and association copies of Kelmscott books that have since been widely dispersed.

Unpublished materials relating to the Press are abundant, again emanating mainly from Cockerell. One of the most important of such documents is Cockerell's diary, which he kept methodically all his adult life: for the sake of this bibliography, I have examined the years 1886 through 1900 (BL Add. MSS. 52623-37). Cockerell himself began copying out passages referring to Morris shortly after Morris's death and often quoted his diaries when later writing or lecturing about Morris. Thé British Library also owns a large body of correspondence addressed to Cockerell (Add. MSs. 5270871).

Morris's engagement diaries for 1893, 1895, and 1896 (BL Add. MSS. 45409-1I) are less informative than Cockerell's but often fix the dates when Morris worked on particular ornaments or engraved titles. The correspondence of Morris in the British Library (Add. MSS. 4533845), mostly to his wife and daughters, is also occasionally helpful.

Alarge collection of Burne-Jones's letters, largely typescript copies, is owned by the Library of the Fitzwilliam Museum.

A number of key documents associated with the Kelmscott Press have survived. A letterbook of the Press, covering the years 1894-6, is in the William Morris Gallery. The Morgan Library owns a ledger for 1892-6 and a list of customers to whom announcements were sent in 1897; an earlier account book for 1891 is in the private collection of Yoshiaki Morisawa. A volume labelled 'Specimens' (BL C.43.h.5) is an indexed collection of ephemera, title-pages, and colophons that was used at the Press. The Huntington Library owns a substantial collection of invoices from W. H. Hooper, C. E. Keates, and others addressed to the Press. A group of enlarged photographs of fifteenthcentury types used by Morris as an aid in designing his own types is in he . Brideii Lbrary. I the BodleiLbrary rh. is a volume of ornaments with this note by Cockerell: 'This is the book which William Morris used in order to settle the initials and ornaments that were to go on the various pages of Kelmscott Press books, the sheets beingfrst submitted to him with the space forii tials &c. left open.'

Collections of proofs and trial pages, mainly assembled by Cockerell and Robert Proctor, are too numerous to list here, though most o them ar cited latern this bibliography.Libraries with major ol-al B  B, Moan Lbrary,he Cark Lbray  Ane nd theSanford University Library; he private collection Sanford and Helen Berger is also noteworthy in this respect.

An albu roaheatecott re ments and engraved titles is in the Newberry Library; since the album belonged to C. E. Keates, these ornaments are probably the ones he engraved. A much larger collection of proofs of ornaments, engraved titles, and illustrations  some of them marked and annotated  that was owned by W. H. Hooper is now in the Department of Prints and Drawings, the Victoria and Albert Museum.

## 2. TYPES

Except for a few passages in Selwyn Image's Greek type in Atalanta (A25), Morris used only three typefaces in the Kelmscott Press books, all designed by himself: the Golden type (a roman English), the Troy type (a blackletter Great Primer), and the Chaucer type (a blackletter Pica). All three were engraved by Edward P. Prince and were cast at the Fann Street Foundry of Sir Charles Reed & Son.

The inspiration to design a roman face came to Morris during an illustrated lecture by Emery Walker at the Arts and Crafts Exhibition on I November 1888. His chief models were a type used by Jenson in Pliny's Historia Naturale (Venice, 1476) and a similar but somewhat heavier type used by Jacobus Rubeus in Leonardus Brunus Aretinus's Historia Florentina (Venice, 1476). Walker made a series of photographic enlargements of pages from these and other books, which Morris traced and adapted before producing his own freehand drawings. (The photographs, enlarged to a scale of five times the original, are now in the St. Bride Printing Library.) Morris called the resulting design 'the regenerate type or Jenson-Morris' and was pleased when Walter Crane complained that it was more gothic than roman (Mackail, ii, 262, 268). The Golden type was cut during 1890, and it was first used in a trial page for The Story of the Glittering Plain (A1), set on 31 January 1891. The lower-case g was recut almost immediately.

During the latter half of 1891 Prince engraved what Morris described as a semi-Gothic type designed . . . with special regard to legibility' (Morris to Bernard Quaritch, 1 June 1892 [BQ]). Though the Troy type was a freer adaptation of its models than was the Golden type, the St. Bride photographs show that Morris drew heavily upon a type used by Peter Schoeffer in his Bible of 1462 and a type used by Gunther Zainer in a book printed in Augsburg in 1473. Morris's capital I was found to be unsatisfactory and had to be recut. Although early specimens of the Troy type were passages from Chaucer and Froissart, the first book in which it appeared was The Recuyell of the Historyes of Troye (A8), issued in December 1892.

It was clear to Morris that for the edition of Chaucer he needed a smaller version of the Troy type. Walker therefore made for him a series of photographic reductions of passages from Chaucer and The Story of the Glittering Plain set in Troy type (BL C.1o2.h.18). The Chaucer type was engraved in early 1892 and was first used for the table of contents and glossary of The Recuyell of the Historyes of Troye. It first appeared as a text type in The Order of Chivalry (A13), issued in April 1893.

A specimen-chart of the three types (WMAB, Pl. LxxvIII) shows that for each of them Morris designed the following characters: twenty-six capitals, twenty-six lower-case letters, ten figures, seven ligatures, an ampersand, parentheses, seven marks of punctuation, and a hyphen (with an alternative, oblique hyphen supplied for the Troy and Chaucer types). Morris also designed several ornaments for each itals, boldface, brackets, superior figures, or dashes.

In November 1892 Morris designed a lower-case alphabet (WMAB, Pl. LxxvI1) based on the type used by Sweynheym and Pannartz  intermediate between blackletter and roman  in De civitate Dei (Subiaco, 1467), but he did not complete it.

After the Kelmscott Press closed, five lectures by Morris were printed at the Chiswick Press for Longmans in the Golden type, and there was a proposal from Longmans, never acted upon, for a uniform edition of Morris's works in the Golden type (Cockerell diary, 31 May 1900), though seven volumes in Golden type were published by Longmans early in this century. In 1926 Cockerell, as Morris's Trustee, transferred the Kelmscott types, matrices, and punches from the Chiswick Press to the Cambridge University Press (Cockerell to May Morris, 18 January 1926 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 18]). In 1940, again through Cockerell's intervention, the cUP purchased them (Thomas n [Cambridge, 1951], p. v).

## 3. PAPER AND VELLUM

Though Morris personally preferred paper copies of his books to vellum, he printed a few vellum copies of most of the Kelmscott Press paper editions. For The Story of the Glittering Plain (A1) Morris used vellum left over from his earlier calligraphic experiments; thereafter the suppliers of vellum to the Press were Henry Band of Brentford, Middlesex, and William J. Turney & Co. of Stourbridge, Worcestershire. William H. Bowden has put on record a detailed description of how the vellum copies were printed at the Kelmscott Press:

## 4. COPYTEXTS

Historians of printing and book-design have paid a good deal of attention to the aesthetic qualities of Kelmscott books, but remarkably little has been written about the ambitious publishing programme initiated by Morris in 1891. The books printed at the Kelmscott Press fell mainly into three categories: Morris's own works, mediaeval texts, and standard classics of English literature. Many of Morris's choices were also adopted by later private presses, thus testifying to yet another aspect of his influence.

Morris of course proofread his own works (though he probably did not read against copy) and frequently introduced minor revisions. The evidence of the surviving proofs suggests that in most cases he did not proofread the other books, except quite perfunctorily, though he always indicated on the proofs what initials and borders were to be used. Many of the books were edited by F. S. Ellis, who took his responsibilities very seriously and often consulted Morris about textual problems, especially in the mediaeval works. Despite Ellis's strenuous efforts, however, scholars (some of whom are quoted in the NOTEs sections) have generally concluded that the kP texts fall short of the more exacting modern editorial standards. This is hardly surprising, since Morris and Ellis were aiming at a readable text of broad literary appeal rather than literary accuracy. (They did not hesitate, for example, to improve on Caxton's punctuation, though they retained his spelling.) Nevertheless, the care with which they printed literary documents, some of them previously unpublished, is remarkable; the Kelmscott Press was, among other things, a pioneering attempt at intelligent popularisation of literary works that in some instances were drawn from obscure sources. Other publishers such as Quaritch and David Nutt were eager to print mediaeval texts because of their antiquarian appeal, but Morris, to whom the Middle Ages were an immediate reality, published such texts at the Kelmscott Press because of their vivid literary charm.

## S. INKS

The initial supplier of black ink to the Kelmscott Press was Shackell, Edwards & Co., Ltd. of London. At Emery Walker's suggestion, Morris briefly tried an ink manufactured by Gebrüder Jänecke of Hannover, which, though satisfactory in other respects, was found to be too stiff for the comfort of his printers. However, during 1895 yellow stains began to appear in the sheets of the Chaucer (A4o) then being printed. (See William S. Peterson, 'William Morris and the Damned Chemists": The Search for an Ideal Ink at the Kelmscott Press', Printing History, 3, n0. 2 [1981], 411.) With great difficulty Morris succeeded in eradicating the stains by bleaching the sheets in the sun, but the unhappy experience persuaded him that he would have to switch to the Jänecke ink. According to Walker, '. . . Morris read the riot act and said if they [his printers] wouldn't use the German ink he would close the Press. As his pressmen were very well paid they naturally did not want to lose their jobs and gave way. However, they took it out of Morris another way and the number of sheets turned out per day were extraordinarily small' (Walker to John Johnson, 20 April 1931, quoted in WMAB, p. 82; this letter is in JC). On 19 August 1895 Morris wrote to Walker, 'The Hanover ink was tried on Friday and worked well: I shall use no other now' (The Letters of William' Morris to His Family and Friends, ed. Philip Henderson [1950], p. 374).

ry   o application of ink to the formes: in the John Johnson Collection of teB Glittering Plain A, e marke'Cooion Rollerd te Litho. roller'.

Various experiments with red and blue inks are also extant. Morris y

## 6. ILLUSTRATIONS

The principal wood-engraver for the Kelmscott Press was William Harcourt Hooper, Morris's neighbour in Hammersmith, who came out of retirement to work for the Press. He frequently irritated Mor-h   W M HRC].

For the Chaucer (A4o) and some of the other Kelmscott books illustrated by Burne-Jones, Morris also found it necessary to enlist the talents of Robert Catterson-Smith, afterwards Headmaster of the Birmingham School of Art. In a letter to May Morris, 6 June 1922 (BL Add. ms. 45347, f. 176), Walker wrote out a detailed account of how the Chaucer illustrations were produced. Burne-Jones, he said, did his drawings in silver point or hard pencil, on approximately the same scale as the woodblocks were to be engraved. These drawings then had to be modified and translated in order to harmonize with the Chaucer type being used in the book. At first Charles Fairfax Murray undertook this translation, but, according to Walker (exaggerating perhaps), it was three months before he completed one drawing. Morris therefore turned to Catterson-Smith. Walker's processengraving firm photographed Burne-Jones's drawings and made two very pale photographic prints ("platinos') of each of them. Catterson-Smith mounted one of the prints on a piece of cardboard and covered it with a slight wash of hinese white, so that the drawing was just barely visible. He then drew over Burne-Jones's lines with a brush; before this revised drawing was transferred photographically to the surface of the woodblock and engraved by Hooper, Burne-Jones and Catterson-Smith discussed it at length. Walker argued, in fact, that the collaboration was similar to that in which earlier woodengravers had made alterations in a design; the only difference was that in this case a third person had stepped into the traditional role of the engraver. Walker said that Burne-Jones told him the cooperation between himself and Catterson-Smith, especially as the work was so perfect that increasingly Burne-Jones thought of his assistant as a tool in his hand.

Catterson-Smith has also left a record of his procedures: 'Emery Walker made a very pale print of a photograph (a platino) from Sir E.B.J's pencil drawing - the exact size of the drawing  I then stuck the print down on stout card board, and, in order to avoid the expansion of the paper I put the paste on the card board first and then applied the paper print very quickly, so quickly that it had no time to absorb moisture and so expand, then I immediately ran a hot smoothing-iron over it which at once dried the paste. Next I gave the print a thin even wash of chinese white with a little size in it. The result was to get rid of everything but the essential lines: Next I went over the pale lines with a very sharp pencil copying and translating them from the B-J. drawing which was in front of me. The lines of shading were put in pencil. These shadow lines were very difficult as they had to be translations of very grey pencil tones, and they often took a long time to fit into these spaces. When the pencil drawing was finished all trace of the photograph had disappeared. Next came the inking over which was done with a fine round sable brush and very black chinese ink which I bought in bottles. By putting a little size in the Chinese white, as above mentioned, the ink was not absorbed by the white and so remained jet black  otherwise it would have become grey -. To get a good brush for the purpose was not easy. I spread the hairs fan-wise and look[ed] with a magnifying glass to see were there any irregular hairs, if there were I cut them out with a razor and so got an even point. When difficulties arose in the treatment of passages I consulted B-J. Sometimes work by Albert Durer was consulted to see how he had dealt with passages. Some of the drawings were done over several times. I found the best result came from using the brush in almost a vertical position, this with a well charged brush gave a fat" even line. w.. made a distinction between a "fat"and acid" line. . . . Fi nally Waker made a photraph  the wood-block and Hooper cut it

on the Chaucer paper (PML).

In t J wood-engravings were printed separately because the artist was slow in producing his designs.

The illustrations were printed directly from the woodblocks, as

Drawings, the British Museum) prove. The supplier of at least some .

## 7. ORNAMENTS

The Kelscott res borders nd iitals, all dee by Mor, wre also engraved by Hooper, assisted by C. E. Keates and W. Spielmeyer. Cockerell recalled, in a letter to May Morris, 1o February 1936, how they were drawn: 'Your father had two little saucers in front of him. He roughed out his design in pencil  then drew it carefully with his black brush, correcting with white & then again with black as he went on, & using white over the black for various little details  but he only, I think, worked on solid black (as sometimes happened) he had to black out a largish part of a design because he was not satisfied with it' (BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 177).

All of the ornaments were printed from electrotypes after Walker persuaded Morris that a group of electrotyped initials was virtually indistinguishable from the same initials printed from the original blocks. Morris's normal procedure for designing a border was to draw it directly on a proof of the text or on a piece of paper that was then pasted down on a proof or trial page. All or part of the page was photographed onto the surface of a woodblock by Walker & Boutall, but of course only the border was engraved. The initials were frequently engraved in groups on a single woodblock and were not separated until they had been electrotyped.

Morris occasionally experimented with hand-coloured initials, but no initials were so coloured at the Press.

In 1897 the blocks of all the Kelmscott Press ornaments and illustrations were given to the British Museum.

## 8. CANCELS

The most difficult kind of cancel to detect is that in which an entire gathering, or a pair of conjugate leaves, has been reprinted (see Fredson Bowers, Principles of Bibliographical Description [Princeton, 1949], pp. 234-5). The cancels in the Kelmscott Press books are, with one notable exception (A17), of this type.

In the numerous collections of proofs and trial pages assembled by Cockerell, there is considerable evidence of corrections and improvements being made in the Kelmscott books as they passed through the press; and in many instances Cockerell has written 'Cancelled'on leaves that were rejected. Librarians, auction firms, and rare book dealers, enthusiastically following Cockerell's example, have labelled as 'cancels' Kelmscott Press trial pages, proofs, and retrees. Such confusion is not surprising, since in practice the several categories of discarded leaves and sheets appear to merge almost imperceptibly into one another.

At one end of the spectrum is the trial page, of which only a few copies were struck off, used in designing a book. Usually (but not always) a trial page was printed on one side only as a leaf. Once the printing began, Morris inspected the first copy of each sheet (which he regarded as a proof), and if he was dissatisfied, the printers made alterations before any more sheets were printed. An excellent example of this sort of stop-press correction is the different text of 'La Belle Dame sans Merci' which was substituted in The Poems of John Keats (A24). Next, there are the few instances in which we know that a substantial number of sheets (probably all of them) were printed before an error was discovered, and a cancellandum (always a full sheet or at least conjugate leaves) was produced. The reprinting of a pair of leaves (d4.5) in the Chaucer (A4o) is the most unambigious example of this type of cancellation. Finally, in one peculiar case (A17), individual cancel leaves were printed, but six weeks after the publication of the book, and I have so far found no copy in which the cancellantia do in fact appear.

When confronted with a leaf marked 'Cancelled' by Cockerell, one wonders whether it is a true cancel (i.e., was it reprinted after all the sheets of that gathering had gone through the press?) or merely a first sheet (i.e., did Morris find something objectionable in it and ininsist upon alterations before the rest of the sheets were printed?). Cockerell did not distinguish between these two categories; yet the first is a cancel, and the second is not. I have therefore chosen to interpret the evidence as conservatively as possible: in only four cases (A2, A17, A4o, and A54) did it seem to me beyond any reasonable doubt that cancellation had in fact taken place. In the following list I will provide a summary of the known instances of altered leaves and sheets in the Kelmscott books, but  it must be emphasised again  I have found incontrovertible evidence of actual cancellation in four instances alone.

1.The Story of the Glittering Plain (Al). A variant of the last gathering, 2b² (BL C.1o2.a.19), suggests that the colophon and the conclusion of the text were reset in order to achieve tighter word-spacing.

.Poms by the Way (A2). A variant of the last gathering, 04, with a

## 9. BINDINGS

Each Kelmscott book was bound in either quarter linen or full vellum by J. and J. Leighton of London. (The Chaucer [A4o] was also available in a special pigskin binding designed by Morris and executed at the Doves Bindery.) One peculiarity of the books may be summarized in this pronouncement by Cobden-Sanderson (whose ideas much influenced the Kelmscott binding style): 'The end-papers . . . should . . . not be pasted on; they also should be sewn with the rest of the book. And for this purpose additional sections of plain paper should be supplied to the binder by the publisher to be bound up at the beginning and end of the volume' ('Bookbinding: Lecture by T. J. Cobden-Sanderson', in Transactions of the National Association for the Advancement of Art and Its Application to Industry [189o], p. 467). Occasionally the first or last leaf of an ordinary gathering was made to serve as a pastedown.

The quarter linen style Morris regarded as a satisfactory temporary binding' (Morris to Bernard Quaritch, 25 July [1891] [βQ]), though in fact the majority of Kelmscott Press books have not been rebound.

On the spines of the vellum bindings Morris often used enlarged versions of the Golden and Troy types. ". . . Mr. Morris has come to the conclusion that the black-letter capitals do not look so well as those of the "Golden" type, & he will not use them for lettering on the covers in future,' Cockerell wrote to Fred J. Hall on 22 October 1894 (LB).

The silk ties used on nearly all of the limp vellum bindings were supplied by Morris & Co. (They are, incidentally, the most fragile part of the Kelmscott books and are broken on many copies.) The colour of the ties is not specified in this bibliography, since a single colour was not adopted for all copies of a book. Dull green, reddish brown, and gold were the most common colours.

## IO. PRESSES

Morris's original intention was to do composition only in Hammersmith and then to send the formes to Emery Walker's office in Clifford's Inn for printing (Sparling, p. 9). In time Morris concluded that he must control the presswork as well as the typesetting, and early in 1891 he acquired a 'Demy Albion Press, complete with Cylinder Inking Table' for 25 (HEH P-Kelmscott-X). According to Sparling (p. 82), 'A second press was bought in November [1891], as work on the Golden Legend [A7] was dragging along, and books were beginning to get in one another's way.' A surviving invoice (BL C.1o2.h.18) reveals that on 29 November 1891 Morris purchased 'One Super Royal Genuine Albion Press' from Hopkinson & Cope for 42 10s.

Altogether Morris acquired three full-size Albions and a proofing press (Sparling, p. 68); of these, the present locations of three presses are known. (1) Á large Albion, reinforced with iron bands, is owned by J. Ben Lieberman of New Rochelle, N.Y. Manufactured in 1891, the press was purchased by Morris at the end of 1894 from Hopkinson & Cope for s2 1os. and was used for printing the Chaucer (A40). It as later wned by C.R.Ashbee, F. W. Goudy, and Melbert ary (WMAB, p. 139). (2) A Demy Albion press, manufactured between 1830 and 1838, is owned by the William Morris Society and is presently kept in the basement of Kelmscott House. It was owned successively by Ashbee, the Shakespeare Head Press, and Sir Basil Blackwell, who gave it to the Society. (3) A Demy Folio Albion, used at the Kelmscott Press for printing leaflets and proofing, is owned by the HuntnLry It  cu  Mo a eas. Later it was purchased in New York by Theodore Lilienthal, who sold it to the Library in 1953.

For descriptions of the three presses, see J. Ben Lieberman, 'The Lbry Bel  Kelt ouy  Blact u William Morri intingess  Willia Morr Scey ,  Tho Lial Wil Mo Goes West (privately printed, Berkeley, Calif., 1961).

![](images/801cdca371b15bcaa518fce9c0f9d3c23d6811c94fb169a7296741dab64b5836.jpg)  
4 F. W. Goudy, the American printer and type-designer, with the largest of Morris's presses, which was used for the printing of the Chaucer (A4o). (Private collection of J. Ben Lieberman.)

## II. STAFF

The size of Morris's printing staff fluctuated according to how many books were in production; it probably reached its maximum in 1895 and early 1896 when the Chaucer was approaching completion. A photograph taken at the Wayzgoose in September 1895 shows eleven printers (Fig. 5). The first one hired was William Bowden, a retired master-printer, who was soon joined by his daughter Mrs Pine and his son William H. Bowden, who became the overseer (Sparling, p. 73). In addition there were Sparling (the first Secretary of the Press, 1891 4), Cockerell (the second Secretary, 1894-8), Mrs Sarah A. Peddie (Cockerell's secretary), and Emery Walker (unofficial adviser).

For a time Morris hired a professional printer's reader (Sparling, p. 85). But Cockerell turned out to be an excellent proofreader, and he, Sparling, and F. S. Ellis seem to have carried most of the burden of proofing. One anecdote by Cockerell is worth recording: 'When I read the proofs for him [Morris] I often used to point out things, which he altered (as a rule) as a matter of course. On one occasion I remember an outburst & a grinding of teeth, & then the alteration after a few minutes, & a confession at Gatti's [restaurant] the same evening by way of amends to myself. But this was over a sentence that he had designed on the first page of the Chaucer, & his irritation was natural as some words had to be taken out & designed again' (Cockerell to May Morris, 19 January 1913 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 14]).

Acording to Colebrook (p. 73), all of the Caxton books published at the Kelmscott Press except for The Golden Legend (A7) were transcribed by Mrs Peddie on a typewriter at the British Museum; howevr, I nfo  a sk  e 188os or 1890s.

## 12. BUILDINGS

The first location of the Kelmscott Press was 16 Upper Mall, Hammersmith  just a few doors away from Kelmscott House  rented by Moi f  .Sparn f inteor O upsairs room  th cotte was fited wi cks cases, imposing-stone,etc.; another housed the single press, bought scondhand; the rooms on the ground-floor were utilized for stores.' This house was demolished some years ago.

In May   C Mal whic was art g   y Walke'. 6 ai the Press until it closed, though another small house, 21 Upper Mall, the Chaucer.

![](images/97c6d02126250d920c506f3d9586b6739e7ec61520158321d8c1496aaddc30b7.jpg)

It should also be borne in mind that Morris's personal and professional lives so mingled with each other that Kelmscott Press business constantly flowed into Morris's library at Kelmscott House, where sheets of books in production sometimes lay in stacks. Once Morris even jocularly suggested that the Press ought to be moved into the meadow next to Kelmscott Manor, his country house in Oxfordshire (Cockerell diary, 10 August 1892).

## I3. PUBLISHERS

Morris was at first content to allow either Reeves & Turner - 'the most unbusiness-like publishers but extremely honest', as Jane Morris described them in a letter to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, 2o April 1892 (Fm[1])  or Bernard Quaritch publish the Kelmscott Press books. However, a growing disenchantment with Quaritch's sharp business practices persuaded him to publish as well as print the books. (See NOTEs for A7 and A4o.) 'Mr. W. Morris wishes to have all the profits of the Kelmscott press,' Quaritch complained in a letter to his son, 8 February 1893;he will retail himself the ["]Godfrey of Bouloyne" [A15]  he will be the loser by it, but it will be a nuisance to me on account of the Chaucer' (Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 189). Thereafter Morris published most of the Kelmscott books himself: one consequence of this decision was that he was forced to subsidise the larger books by producing a considerable number of the more popular small ones. Thé Chaucer (A4o) in particular caused Morris a great deal of n on paper.

## 14. FINANCES

The Kelmscott Press was in effect an amusing diversion for Morris (though some very serious ideas lay behind its founding), and he seems to have been astonished that it did not cost him any more than it did. It is difficult to determine whether the Press realiseda pro, because Morris was not paid for his contributions, which were essential to the Press's success, and because, especially in the early years, the bookkeeping methods were haphazard. The situation improved slightly when Cockerell took over as Secretary in July 1894: 'In future, we may be able to keep a closer record of expenditures than e .J.R.n August 1894 (L B).

Bu Mot holity e cek     I out how much money I have got,' he complained (Mackail notebook J16], p.134). When e ent Walter Crane is sare f the o-G la promised another cheque as soon as he could 'get an account made out which I shall take care is not too much humbugged by the accountant, who (any of them) could easily make out that there was no proft at all. But I will see that it is all fair, as I really know what the pr effectallyriate prit by William aroJ 1975).

Hence any generalisations about the Press's finances must be made with extreme caution. Yet the evidence does suggest that on most of the books Morris made a modest profit, though again it must be emphasised that this profit was possible only because Morris contributed his priceless services gratis.

## I5. EXPLANATION OF THIS BIBLIOGRAPHY

The bibliography is based upon, and is intended to supersede, Sydney Cockerell's ´'An Annotated List of All the Books Printed at the Kelmscott Press in the Order in Which they Were Issued' (see A53). Certain pieces of information in Section A are taken over intact from the 'List': the numbers assigned to the borders by Cockerell, the number of copies printed, prices, and dates of publication. To do an independent analysis of the Kelmscott Press borders would be a major undertaking; I have made only a few spot checks on Cockerell's numbering system, and I have found him unfailingly accurate. The number of copies printed was frequently slightly higher than the 'official' figure given by Cockerell, but both this and the date of publication are, in a sense, arbitrary numbers. It seemed better to retain Cockerell's figures rather than to emend some of them very slightly.

For the sake of consistency, I have always treated the printed title rather than the engraved one as the 'real' title. Morris himself is inconsistent: sometimes he seems to regard the printed title as merely a half-title, and at other times he clearly intends it to be the main title. I have not described the engraved titles in each case because they are all fundamentally the same: the lettering is against a floral or vine background and is surrounded by a border.

In supplying collational formulas I have tried to follow the rules enunciated in Fredson Bowers's Principles of Bibliographical Description, though I have been reluctant to label certain leaves as cancels except in those cases where the evidence of cancellation is unmistakable (see above). The measurements given of pages and text areas are only approximate and will vary slightly from one copy of a book to another. The collations and measurements given are for paper rather than vellum copies. For each book I have examined a minimum of four copies: those in the Bodleian Library, the British Library, the Library of Congress, and the Library of Trinity College, Cambridge. For books with unusual features I have examined additional copies. Titles and half-titles of books are understood to be in the same type as the text unless otherwise indicated. I have recorded only the bindings in which the Kelmscott books were originally issued.

Because of typographical limitations of the machine on which this book is typeset, oblique rather than vertical rules are used to indicate lin-diviions. Since Mors never eployed the oblique rule s a mark of punctuation, this should cause no confusion.

In the notes section for each book I have drawn upon unpublished letters and diaries, published sources, trial pages, and proofs. I have not systematically searched for reviews of the Kelmscott books but have cited the few that I know about.

For the RELATED MATERIALS sections I have sent a form letter to major British and American libraries and have searched reference works such as Book Auction Records and American Book Prices Current; but o course I am aware that this scton can never b coplete, pr ticularly since much of the relevant material is probably in private collections. For items located in catalogues, I have given only the last reference found except when I was also able to cite the important catalogue of Cockerell's collection (Sotheby, 10 December 1956). Presentation copies - which are always from Morris unless otherwise indicated  are included in this section because they represent, from Morris's point of view, the reason for the Press's existence. 'Proofs' means a st of proofs that is within a haldozen pages of beig complee.Prooillutrations,ments, ndengrav tie,bu they are so numerous, are not listed unless they are marked or touched or are otherwise distinctive. I have recorded designs for ornaments only when they could be connected with a particular Kelmscott book. Retrees, which auction firms and librarians often mistakenly classify as cancels or proofs, are so abundant that I have not listed thém. Since some of the Kelmscott Press materials  once owned by Cockerell in te Morgan Lbrary, the Clark Lbrary and the Huntinon Lbray are not yet catalogued, I have identified them by using Cockerell's classification (.., 'Morrisiana . Similarly, because e of the collections of trial pages and proofs in the British Library are in multi-volume sets with only one shelf-mark, I have added Robert Proctor's private shelf-mark, which wil be found written in pencil on a fyleaf (e.g., BL C.43.c.26 [ΣQQ46]).

In compiling the RELATED MATERIALS sections I have encountered certain difficulties which should be mentioned. The most important Kelmscott Press collection to come on the market since Cockerell's in 1956 is the John A. Saks collection, which was sold at Christie's in New York on 20 May 1983. The principal buyer at the sale was Maggs Brothers, acting on behalf of a private collector, very well known in book-collecting circles, who declines to allow his name to appear in print. Hence the complete vellum set of Kelmscott books (which made \$66o,ooo at Christie's), many of them from Morris's library, must be identified here as the property of 'pcL' (i.e., private collection, London.) I have found it impossible to trace most of the other items from the Saks sale, but as this bibliography goes to press they are beginning to appear in the catalogues of various rare book dealers. I was also unable to gain access to another major collection in London, which consists of Emery Walker's set of the Kelmscott books (except for his copy of the Chaucer, which is now in the British Library), most of which are inscribed by Morris.

In Sections C and D I have used the 'Specimens' volume in the British Library as a standard of reference.

# William Moris, The Story  the Glittering Plain THE STORY OF THE GLITTERING / PLAIN. WHICH HAS BEEN ALSO / CALLED THE LAND OF LIVING / MEN OR THE ACRE OF THE UN-/DYING. WRITTEN BY WILLIAM / MORRIS.

COLLATION: Small 4°: a² b2a4 2b². 96 leaves, pp. [4] [1] 2188.

PAPER & DIMENSIONs: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 199× 140 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 1; six- and ten-line initials.

COTeNTs: a1 blank a2 title a2 table f contents b12b2 text 2b2v colophon.

BINDING: Stiff vellum with washleather tis. Stamped in old on spine: 'THE / STORY / OF/ THE /GLIT-/TER-/ING /PLAIN BY / WILL-/IAM / MORRIS / 1891.' [Golden type].

PUBLICATION: 200 paper copies (2 gns.); 6 vellum copies (12 and 15 gns.). Issued 8 May 1891.

NOTEs: The Golden Legend (A7) was originally intended to be the initial publication of the kp. However, when Batchelor supplied the first delivery of paper to the Press, Morris reported to Ellis that it was 'not sized quite hard enough. . . . But it is only a little lot (9 reams), therefore I intend printing a little edition of the Glittering Plain on it' (Mackail, ii, 268). Morris approached Walter Crane about illustrating it, 'but,' as Crane late recalled, 'he was so eager to get his first book out that he could not wait for the pictures, and so the Glittering Plain first appeared simply with his own initials and ornaments' (Crane, An Artist's Reminiscences [1907], p. 328). Hence in 1894 Morris produced another edition of the book (see A2z)  the only kP title to be printed twice  with illustrations by Crane.

The Glittering Plain was set, with minor alterations, from the English

Illustrated Magazine (June—September 189o), where it had been first printed, and the proofs (see j below) show that it was read for the press with unusual care by Morris. Hé had finished the border, which was engraved by Hooper, by 22 January 1891 (Cockerell diary), and on 31 January the earliest trial pages were pulled, even though the Golden type fount was not yet complete. The first sheet was printed on 2 March (see j below), and work progressed during the following month despite Morris's 'gouty illness'. Cockerell happened to visit the Press for the first time on 4 April, the day the colophon was being printed: 'Emery Walker took me to see the Kelmscott Press, in which the Glittering Plain, the first book to be issued from it, is now being printed from Morris' new type. Saw the colophon being set up, with the date April 4th 1891. Exceedingly interesting. The house a little old fashioned one the single hand press at the top of a winding corner stair, the compositor being in a little room leading out of that in which the press is. Printed sheets, one on vellum, lying about  all most beautiful, especially the first page with its elaborately designed border' (Cockerell diary).

Cobden-Sanderson noted in his journal on the same day: '. . . I have just received from Morris the "dummy"; it is a dummy binding by Leighton, to show how he proposes to bind The Glittering Plain' (The Journals of Thomas James obden-Sanderson [New York, 1926], i, 304). The volume was the only kP book with washleather ties. 'Morris had four of the vellum copies bound in green vellum, three of which he gave to friends' ('List'; see y below).

Wilfrid Scawen Blunt had lunch with the Morrises on 7 May 1891, the day the first copy of the book was delivered: 'He [Morris] is full as ever of his work and took me to see his printing press a few doors off, and sold me across the dining table the first copy of the first volume printed at the Kelmscott Press, a very wonderful production. . . . While we were sitting talking this copy was brought in to him by the printer, the first that had been bound and made up, and I asked him "what do you mean to do with these books?" He said "to sell them of course""Will you ell his copy to me?"Certainly"o much?A pound". So I paid my pound and took it away, having made him write his name thus:"I sold this book to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt May 7th 1891. William Morris" (Blunt diary).

## A2 William Morris, Poems by the Way

POEMS BY THE WAY. WRITTEN / BY WILLIAM MORRIS.

COLOPHON: HERE endeth Poems by the Way, written / by William Morris, and printed by him at the / Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, / in thé County of Middlesex; and finished on / the 24th day of September of the year 1891. / [paragraph] Sold By Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand, London. / [printer's mark 1]

COLATION: Small 4°: [a]² b8(±) c8n8 04. 102 leaves, pp. [4] [1] 2-197 [198] [2] [=200].

PAPER & DIMeNsIONS: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 205 × 143 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 1; six- and ten-line initials; shoulder titles and some lines in red.

[] []an [][a] ble  [] printer's mark  b102 text 03 colophon 03v4 blank.

BINDING: Stiff vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: POEMS/BY/THE/WAY/BY/WILL-/IAM/MORRIS/1891. [Golden type].

PUBLICATIN: 300 paper copies (2 gns.); 13 vellum copis (about 2 gns.). Issued 20 October 1891.

NOTEs: In June 1891 Morris wrote to Reeves & Turner: 'The title of my new book will be Poems by the Way; the format the same as the Glittering Plain. It will be printed in red and black. The poems will include some recently written and some written many years ago. Some have appeared in magazines, but with two exceptions of a little piece out of the Jason and one out of the Ogier, they will none of them have been printed in any book of mine (Mackail, ii, 273). The p b, who had preserved a number of unpublished poems of early date . . and who also had kept a record of those that had appeared separately in periodicals from time to time' (Introductions, i, 70). One of the poems, 'Goldilocks and Goldilocks', was written on 2o May 1891 in order to increase the length of the book. According to Cockerell, Morris first thought of entitling the volume Flores Atramenti ("List').

Printing began in July. When Wilfrid Scawen Blunt dined at Kelmscott House on 6 July, he noticed 'some sheets of his new volume of poems which is to be printed uniform with the volume he is printing for me. Some of the lines are in red, which has a capital effect' (Blunt diary). On 14 July, from Folkestone, Morris wrote to Walker: 'Yes, I think it would be best to print that sheet again; a misprint of that kind, though it does not otherwise matter, is such defacement of the book,  and 'tis a 2 guinea book let us remember.' Walker later annotated this letter by adding that the misprint was, he thought, in Poems by the Way, and that he had ordered it reprinted while Morris was t (.The misrint was probbly hose' h 1. 13 (see j below). We catch another glimpse of the book in production in Blunt's diary on 7 August 1891: 'Lunched at Kelmscott House where Mrs. Morris took me to see the printing. Morris's own poems were being struck off, most beautiful they are with their rubrics. The sheet I saw being printed contained the Ballad of John a Wood.'

A rejected variant of the last gathering (see n below) shows that Morris rearranged the colophon at the last moment in order to obtain more even word-spacing. He was apparently so pleased with his new printer's mark that he used it twice: at the end of the table of contents and on the colophon page. 'They are printing the colophon sheet today, which is exciting,' Morris wrote his daughter Jenny on 23 September 1891 (Mackail, ii, 279). Cockerell, calling at Kelmscott House on 5 November, observed 'several copies of Poems by the Way on the dining room table with covers stained red, yellow, blue & green' (Cockerell diary).

Roberts Brothers of Boston, Mass., issued an unauthorised photographic facsimile of Poems by the Way in 1891. An authorised inexpensive edition was published by Reeves & Turner in December of that year.

W BunTh ven Proteus

THE LOVE-LYRICS & SONGS OF / PROTEUS BY WIL-FRID SCAWEN / BLUNT WITH THE LOVE-SON-/NETS OF PROTEUS BY THE SAME / AUTHOR NOW RE-PRINTED IN / THEIR FULL TEXT WITH MANY / SON-NETS OMITTED FROM THE / EARLIER EDITIONS. / [in red:] LONDON MDCCCXCII.

COLOPHON: HERE end the Love-Lyrics and Songs of Pro-/teus, Written by Wilfrid Scawen Blunt; with / the Love-Sonnets of Proteus by the same Au-/thor. Printed by William Morris at the Kelm-/scott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex, and finished on the 26th / day of January of the year 1892. / [paragraph] Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196 Strand, London. / [printer's mark 1]

TImall  [q p [=12] [1] 2-251 [252]."

PAPER & DImensIONs: Flower(I) paper. Leaf: 205 × 142 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 1; six- and ten-line initials; shoulder titles, initials, stanza and sonnet numbers, and some lines in red.

conTenTs: π1-π1 blank π2r title π2v blank [a]1r-[a]4v table of contents b1-r5 text r6 colophon r6v blank.

BINDING: Stiff vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: 'THE / LOVE / LYRICS / AND / SONGS / OF / PROTEUS / BY / W. S. /BLUNT / 1892' [Golden tγpe].

PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (2 gns.); no vellum copies. Issued 27 February 1892.

NoTEs: 'I have put off writing to have more news first of my typefounding adventure,' Morris wrote to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt on 19 November 189o. 'I think now that I shall be ready to set to work in about 3 months perhaps earlier. I shall of course be very pleased to print your book, if I may have my own way about the get up  which, I také it is what you want.' (This and other letters from Morris to Blunt quoted below are in v & A[1] Box II1.86.kk.) In fact, Blunt did dictate one element of the design; the decorated initials were printed in red at his request, and when the book was in progress Morris commented: 'The are now on Mr. Blunt's book[.] I have one sheet and it looks very gay & pretty with its red letters, but I think I prefer mine in style of printing' (Morris to Jenny Morris, 22 October 1891 [BL Add. MS. 45340, f. 73]).

Morris reported to Blunt on 23 February 1891 that his type had now arrived and he could begin printing Blunt's poems. The volume was planned mainly as a reprint of Blunt's Sonnets and Songs by Proteus (1875) and The Love Sonnets of Proteus (188o). (In a preface to the latter, Blunt had thus explained his pseudonym: "The author of these sonnets, styling himself Proteus, acknowledges thereby a natural mood of change.' A copy of the book, with dates of composition supplied by Cockerell at the bottom of each page, is in the Department of Manuscripts and Printed Books, the Fitzwilliam Museum.) However, he also intended to include a new poem, 'Natalia's Resurrection', which he decided to place first in the  volume at Mrs. Morris's uing.

Typesetting began at the end of February, though it progressed very slowly because both The Golden Legend (Å7) and Poems by the Way (A) were in the press. The first proofs were not available to Blunt until the following August. But a major problem arose during the next month. On 3o September Blunt recorded in his diary: 'I have been away for two nights to Kelmscott [Manor] to see Morris about the printing and to wish Mrs. Morris good bye for the winter. . . . We have arranged that Natalia is to be suppressed and instead of it that the old Proteus Sonnets shall be reprinted as the second part of the Kelmscott volume with certain new sonnets and some of those omitted from the earlier editions. The suppression of Natalia was decided on in consequence of a wild letter from Margaret who imagined that the poem described the relations between herself and her husband and me. And, although the poem had really nothing to do with her, she was so urgent that I could not refuse. It put Morrs to a great inconvenice, or Natalia was already in print, and I was oblied to explain the circumstances to him, when he at once, and most good naturedly consented to the change.' The circumstances were that Blunt, who was notorious for his sexual escapades, had just concluded an affair with Margaret Talbot, wife of the Military Secretary at the British Embassy in Paris (Elizabeth Longford, A Pilgrim of Passion: The Le of Wilrid Scawen Blunt [1980], pp. 814). The pem does indeed seem to allude to the episode.

The change involved cancelling eighteen pages of type that had already been set (see a below). Morris's cheerful acquiescence is curious in view of the fact that his wife had also had a prolonged affair with Blunt. (For an account of the relationship, see Peter Faulkner, Wild Scawen Blunt and the Morrises [1981]. Throuhout the printin of the book, Janey Morris sent detailed criticisms of the poems to Blunt and proofed the entire volume. (She is not known to have proofread any other kp book.) Lady Gregory also corrected the proofs Edith Finch, WilfridScawen Blunt, 18409 [1938], .).

On 12 March 1892 Morris sent to Blunt a summing-up of the accounts: 'The 2zo copies offered for sale have all been sold, and Reeves has handed me 0 on account for you. . . I am charging yo for the printing which was a good deal more than I thought it would cost: but the book finally was much longer than I had estimated and the red printing, there being a great deal of it nearly doubled the cost of the presswork. . . . The binding is extra and will cost something over £3o. . . . I am glad that you will make something out of the book; for when Reeves pays up the whole there will be a good balance in your favour, without reckoning the copies you keep. So I think we may congratulate ourselves on a success.'

As for it critical reception, Morris reported (in a letter to Blunt, 3 April 1892) that the only unfavourable review was by Joseph Pennell in the Star, a 'cur by profession'. Ellis, however, took a dim view of Blunt's poetical pretensions: 'The fact is Morris is so good natured that he does not like to say "no" but is printing this book surely against the grain' (Ellis to Francis Jenkinson, 14 March 1892 [cUL Add. 6463, no. 2018]).

:Th x o aintancean ardent dmirer  Ruskin. 1 June 1891:He s far as Hammersmith with Morris & Walkér. Suggested that Morris should print decent editions of Omar Khayyam and of "On the Nature of Gothic" from the Stones of Venice. Morris seemed to think that he would do so, as The Golden Legend is such a big book that he will have to do some little ones after it is finished. Spoke of "The Nature of Gothic" and begged him to get Morris to print it with his new type.' 10 September 1891: 'Morris ready ready to print the Nature of Gothic if Allen will give him a free hand.' 1 September 1891: 'Called on Mr G Allen at Bell Yard, and told him that Morris was ready to begin printing the Nature of Gothic very shortly. It is admirable.' In fact, Cockerell later secured the ms of Morris's preface from Sparling in exchange for two copies of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 17 April 1936 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 181]).

This chapter of The Stones of Venice (1853) had previously been reprinted as a 4d. pamphlet by F. J. Furnivall on the occasion of the opening of the Working Men's College in 1853. Cook and Wedderburn, in their Library Edition of Ruskin's Works, add the following bibliographical note: 'The Kelmscott edition was set up from the 1886 edition of the complete work, and some misprints which crept into that issue are repeated. . . . Two misprints peculiar to the Kelmscott may also be noted. On p. 26, lines I and 12, the last letters are wrongly spc and on .6, last line, "God" is printe with a small , lxx). In 1899 George Allen published another separate edition of the chapter which reprinted Morris's preface.

The compositors of the KP edition were Collins and Howes (see a below).

PUBLIcatIoN: 50o paper copies (5 gns.); no vellum copies. Issued 3 November 1892.

NoTEs: The Golden Legend, a mediaeval collection of saints' lives, held great appeal for Morris. As Ellis succinctly phrased it in his 'Memoranda': 'Among the books which serve to illustrate the religious life and mode of thought that prevailed in the middle ages, none holds a more important place than the "Legend Aurea." Compiled and put into form in the latter half of the 13th century, the welcome with which it was received by the clergy & the lettered world is attested by the great number of manuscripts of it which are still extant, while its ready reception by the people generally is evidenced by the fact that within a short time of its first appearance it was translated into the vulgar tongue of most of the nations of Europe, frequently with the hagiological preferences of the people of the country for which it was designed' iii, 1282).

It was intended to be thc earliest book produced at the kp, but becaus o its length and some problems with the rst delivery of paper from Batchelor (see A1), Morris was forced to issue several shorter volumes before it was completed. On 21 July 189o, while the Golden type was still being cut, Morris purchased (from Sotheran, for $\mathcal { E } \mathfrak { s o } )$ (Sotheran to Cockerell, 30 November 1897 [BL C.102.h.18]) Wynkyn de Worde's I527 reprint of Caxton's translation (now in NL); when Cockerell visited Kelmscott House on 14 August of that year, Morris read passages of it aloud to him, and Cockerell noted that 'he thinks t] hn s  pe  s iarBu

Morris was uncertain whether to proceed with The Golden Legend or Cxton HistoryTr ntil Bear Quarch nthim cp e lat h  h y book of the two; ohe wrote Eis on  August 189o  I ccet your k y  h  il I early September Elis, Morris, and Quaritch were negotiating a contract (see E1). Quaritch was eager to publish the book, because, he said,rt I think every man of culture should be acquainte with the alleged lives of the Saints, and second I am aware of the philological value of all books Caxton' (Quaritch to Eis, 8 September 1890 [BC]).

Quaritch agreed to print 250 copies (a number almost immediately doubled) at his own expense, and Morris and Ellis were to contribute their services gratis in return for twelve copies each. But the contract, which is dated  September, stipulated that Morris was 'to have absolute and sole control over choice of paper, choice of type, size of the reprint and selection of the printer'Quaritch assumed that he would supply the paper, an idea that was abandoned after Morris met Joseph Batchelor later in the year. Quaritch was startled to discover that the only other modern reprint of The Golden Legend was an incomplete facsimile published by the Holbein Society in 1878 (which Morris owned [Library, no. 484]); however, the Early English Text Society had published several other Caxton titles during the 187os and 188os, and David Nutt, the London publisher, was issuing a limited edition of Caxton's Le Morte Darthur between 1889 and 1891. (For an account of the earlier English editions of The Golden Legend, see Pierce Butler, Legenda Aurea -Légende Dorée  Golden Legend: A Study of Caxton's Golden Legend . . . [Baltimore, 1899].)

Since Morris did not own the Caxton edition (1483) of The Golden Legend (though he possessed at least ten other early editions by the time of his death), Ellis arranged to borrow it from the Cambridge University Library; his daughter Phyllis (afterwards Mrs. Payne) then transcribed it so that the kP printers would not have to handle the book itself; and later Ellis read proofs against the book. (W. H. Bowden reported that not one word of the transcription was illegible [Colebrook, p. 37]; and the surviving scraps of it [see a below] confirm this claim.) As usual, Morris and Ellis disagreed about how much editorial apparatus was needed: Ellis persuaded Edward Gordon Duff, the bibliographer, to prepare a list of English-language editions of The Golden Legend, but in the end Morris declined to include it. 'The author-printer kicks much against introductions, and there is some practical difficulty as to bibliographical description in that there are but two sets of type[:] caps. for headings & that used for the text,' Ellis explained to Duff on 20 March 1892 (HEH DF 268).

Meanwhile, Morris wrestled with problems of design and typography: his initial view was that the book would have to be 'some kind of folio', and he argued that Caxton's joined letters and contractions should not be reproduced, 'since our book is to be a reprint, not a facsimile' (Morris to Ellis, 14 September 189o [Mackail, ii, 2634]). (Morris also extensively altered Caxton's punctuation.) On 31 December he wrote to Ellis that the book could not be double column, since roman type is wider than blackletter (Mackail, ii, 2656). By March 1891 Morris was experimenting with trial pages (see k, l, and m below), on which he made endless small adjustments in order to secure the correct proportions of margins. Writing to Quaritch on 25 July 1891, Morris predicted that printing and binding would cost between £1200 and £1400, and added: 'Mr. Burne Jones has promised to design me a frontispiece for the book and for my part I feel sure there will be a moderate public who will like it very much' (BQ).

Morris designed the border for page I and several of the initials while convalescing at Folkestone in April 1891 (Mackail, ii, 270). The engraved title was cut by Hooper (invoice: HEH HM 36654), as were most of the initials and borders, though a few of the earliest initials were cut by George Campfield. The first sheet was printed on 11 May 1891; the first volume, except for prelims and illustrations, was completed by October 1891. In September 1891 Burne-Jones saw some proofs and reported that he 'went crazy with pleasure' (Memorials, ii, 217). 'The two illustrations and the title (which was the first woodcut title designed by Morris) were not engraved until June and August, 1892, when the third volume was approaching completion' ('List'). Both illustrations 'were touched up for the wood-engraver by Mr. Fairfax Murray in a photographic copy' (Mackail, ii, 293). In Morris's design for the engraved title (see d below), the vine ornament behind the lettering is much lighter than in the final version, and the last line reads 'newly done' rather than 'done anew'. Cockerell reports that about halfa dozen'vellum proofs of the illustrations were printed ('List'; see also q, r, and t below). Morris abandoned his plan to design a printer's mark incorporating a drawing of Kelmscott Manor (Mackail, ii, 293).

The books were delivered to Quarich from the binder by 4 Octo u  A od, fl. 179]. Three days later Morris wrote to Quaritch:I must needs eo pushed it through so promptly. It is just 2 years ago that Ellis & I began talking about doing it in this very house [Kelmscott Manor] where I am writing' (BQ). A receipt of the same date (Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 436, fol. 184) reveals that the total cost of printing and binding was 1350  within Morris's original estimate. The book was very favour-T (1892), 3467.

On 28 October 1892, at the Blue Posts Restaurant, Cork Street, there was a dinner to celebrate the publication of The Golden Legend (menu and seating plan in MCL). The guests, in addition to Morris and Ellis, were Dr. F. J. Furnivall, Edward Gordon Duff, Joseph Knight, Dr. Richard Garnett, William Blaikie, William Michael Rossetti, Emery Walker, and C. F. Ellis. However, the publication of the book was marred by the first of several misunderstandings between Morris and Quaritch. In a letter to Walter T. Spencer, 14 November 1894, Cockerell later summarized the episode: 'The facts of the case are as follows: the Golden Legend was published not by us, but by Mr. Quaritch, who on the day of publication raised the prices from £5.5.- to 6.6, and then by degrees to 1o.1o.-. The trade, being dissatisfied with the terms which Mr. Q. offered to supply them, did not push the book at the time, & the result was that a good many copies remained on Mr. Q's hands. These he is now attempting to sell at the original price 5.5. to the public: with a discount of 15% to the trade' (LB).

Ellis prepared a modern-spelling, seven-volume edition of The Golden Legend for J. M. Dent' Temple Classics' in 190.

## A Raoul Leevre, The Recuell f the Historyes  Tre (2 vols.)

## [Vol. i only] THE RECUYELL OF THE HISTORYES / OF TROYE.

ENGRAVED TITLE: the / Recuyell/ of the / Historyes / of / Troye [gothic lettering designed by Morris].

coLOPHoN: HERE ends this new edition of William Caxton's / Recuyell of the Historyes of Troy, done after the / First Edition; corrected for the press by H. Halliday / Sparling, and printed by me William Morris at the / Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex, & finished on the fourteenth / day of / [printer's mark 1]

COLLATION: Large 4°: [Vol. i] a-t8 u4. 156 leaves, pp. [i-ij] ij-v [x []2 [6] []-k² +m3a 3²+I: 214 leaves, pp. [297] 298507 [508] [2] [509] 510718 [4] [=428].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287205 mm. Tex: 33 lines × 130 mm.

YP:Text i Troy type;tablcontent n glossry inaue type.

ORNAENTs: Borders a, 5, and 8; half borders; side and corner borders; six-and eight-line initials; chapter titles, and poem on $3 2 7 ^ { \mathbf { r } }$ and explicits of Books 1 and 11 in red.

CTENs: [Vol  aie  blank Caxtn's clhn $\mathsf { a } 2 ^ { \mathrm { v } } \mathsf { - a } 4 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ prologues (by Caxton and Lefevre) $\mathsf { a } 4 ^ { \mathrm { v } } \mathrm { - } \mathsf { a } 8 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ table of contents a8v engraved title b1u4 text of Book  u4 blank. [Vol. ] x12lr text of Book 112l2v-XIv blank 2m1-3a6v text of Book 111 $3 ^ { \mathrm { a } } 7 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ poem $3 { } ^ { 2 7 } { } ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ bblank $3 \mathrm { a } 8 ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } 3 \mathrm { b r } ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ glossary 3b1V colophon $2 \chi \mathrm { I } ^ { \Gamma - 2 } \lambda ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ blank.

BGLi vel  S  ol : TROYE/I [I enaged Gold].

PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (9 gns.); velum copies $( \mathcal { L } 8 0 )$ Issued 24 November 1892.

NOTEs: In the summer of 189o Morris was trying to decide which Caxton title to reprint in order to launch his new Press. '. . . now I have borrowed a Recueil of the Histories of Troy (the Wynkyn de Worde of course) from Quaritch,' he wrote to Ellis on 29 August, and have no doubt that the L.L. [Golden Legend] is by far the most important book of the two' (Mackail, i, 262). Nevertheless, he evenand also the first in which thé Chaucer type was employed (for prelims and end matter) - in February 1892. In a memorandum to Quaritch on I June 1892, Morris outlined his plans for the book: 'Reprinted from the first edition of Caxton's Translation  will make a quarto about 7oo p.p. which will if possible be bound in one vol: the whole edition wil be delivered to the publisher bound in vellum or marocco [si] to my satisfaction, so as to harmonize with the typography.

'The book will be ornamented very richly with woodcut ornaments entirely designed by myself; no labour or expense will be spared on them and there will be much variety in them. The semi-G r ill - pear in this book for the first time' (BQ).

For Morris's contract with Quaritch, see E2. Morris supplied the following note on the book for Quaritch's catalogue: 'As to the matter of the the book, it makes a thoroughly amusing story, instinct with mediæval thought and manners. For though written at the end of the Middle Ages and dealing with classical mythology, it has in it no token of the coming "Renaissance" but is purely mediæval. It is the last issue of that story of Troy which had such a hold on men's imaginations; the story built up from a rumour of the Cyclic Poets, of the heroic City of Troy, defended by Priam, with his gallant sons, led by Hector the preux Chevalier, beset by the violent & brutal Greeks, who were looked on as the necessary machinery for bringing about the undeniable tragedy of the fall of the city. Surely this is well worth reading, if only as a piece of undiluted Mediævalism' (see a below).

There are several references to the book's progress in Cockerell's diary. 21 April 1892: 'Took a sheet of the blackletter Recuyell to send to [John Ruskin at] Brantwood.' 2 June 1892: 'Morris had several new side borders for the Recuyell etc.'28 July 1892: 'Morris had pages of Recuyell experimentally illuminated by [Edmond] Reuter.' 6 September 1892: 'Saw . . . pages of the Recuyell (68o now printed).'

The block for the engraved title was cut by C. E. Keates (Keates's bill, 3 October 1892: HEH HM 36661). Altogether Quaritch paid Morris 1o12 10s. for the printing of the volume (receipts of 25 November 1892 and 28 February 1893 [BQ]).

The Star (London), 21 November 1892, p. 2, offered a vivid account of a pre-publication celebration at Kelmscott House: 'Mr. William Morris .  showed to a few friends on Tuesday last an advance copy of his reprint of Caxton's "Recueil of the Historyes of Troye." On looking through it, Dr. Furnivall said enthusiastically, "It's the most beautiful book I ever saw; it's the most beautiful book ever printed!" Mr. F. S. Ellis . . . cordially agreed in this opinion, and so did Mr. Emery Walker . . . and Mr. Theodore Watts. . .

Though there had been a number of earlier editions of the Recuyell, the kP version was the first to go back directly to Caxton's text. Nevertheless, H. Oskar Sommer, in an edition of the Recuyell published by David Nutt in 1894, made a strong attack upon the textual reliability of the KP edition: 'In our own days . . . Mr. William Morris has produced . . . an edition of "The Recuyell," which, as regards get ting up and artistic execution, is all that can be desired; but from my point of view, that of the critic and philologist, it is absolutely without any value. I have often wondered how it is that Mr. Morris, who is so rich in resources and designs, cannot find the means for the production of a beautiful book which at the same time meets all the requirements of modern scholarship. If Mr. Morris was determined to make the Trojan War the subject of one of his beautiful books, why did he not select Lydgate's Troy-Book, so infinitely superior to "The Recuyell," which from a purely literary point of view, has no value whatever?' Of the Recuyell, Reynard the Foxe (A1o), and Godefrey of Boloyne (A1s), Sparling says: '. .  Morris wished thém to be regarded as Kelmscott Press editions, and therefore to be amended where this was desirable. Caxton's text was to be taken as a basis, but not looked upon as archaeologically sacrosanct. It was to be collated with Caxton's originals, and corrected where need was, mistranslations being put right and omissions flled in, care being taken to preserve the style and flavour of Caxton in doing this' (p. 84).

RELATED MATERIALs: (a) Pres. copy to Cockerell, with design for engraved title; Ms in Morris's hand of note for Quaritch's catalogue; a slip torn from Morris's diary recording the beginning of the printing of the book; and calculations by Morris of the book's length (Cockerell sale, no. 10; now PML 769o3-4). (b) Designs for two initials (su). (c) Trial page (su). (d) Inc. proofs (Sotheby cat., 18 December 1944, no. 188. ) Inc. proofs (c red portfolio.) The Kelmscott editon was to have been illustrated with approximately two hundred wood-engravings designed by Burne-Jones, of which twenty-five designs were completed. These  as redrawn by Robert Catterson-Smith  were published in The Beginning o the WorlTwenty-FivePicures Eward Bue-Jones 9

## A William Caxton (trans.), The History of Reynard the Foxe

THE HISTORY OF REYNARD THE FOXE / BY WILLIAM CAXTON.

ENGRAVED TITLE: the / History of Reynard / the / Foxe [gothic lettering designed by Morris]

COLOPHON: HERE ends the History of Reynard the Foxe, done / into English out of Dutch by William Caxton / and now reprinted by me William Morris, at the / Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith in the / County of Middlesex. This book was corrected for / the press by Henry Halliday Sparling, and finished / on the 15th day of December 1892. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by Bernard Quaritch, 15, Piccadilly, London.

ar°   ] [=8] [] 2-162 [163-4].

PAPER & DIMeNSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287 × 205 mm. Tex: 33 lines × 130 mm.

TYP:Text in Troy type; glossary in Chaucer type.

ORNAMENTs: Borders 5a and 7; side, corner, and three-quarter borders; six-and eight-line initials; chapter title and explicit in red.

CeT: [a]-[a] lank [a]2r title [a]2v blank [a]3r-[a]r tabl co  it ex  an - m2 colophon m2 blank.

PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (3 gns.); 10 vellum copies (15gns.).   
Issued 25 January 1893.

NOTEs: For Quaritch's catalogue Morris wrote: 'This translation of Caxton's is one of the very best of his works as to style; and being translated from a kindred tongue is delightful as to mere language. In its rude joviality, and simple and direct delineation of character, it is a thoroughly good representative of the famous Beast Epic.' Caxton's translation from the Dutch (1481; 2nd ed., 1488 or 1489) had been frequently reprinted (for versions owned by Morris, see Library, nos. 8812), including several editions in the nineteenth century; for a full bibliography, see the Early English Text Society edition of Reynard (197o), edited by N. F. Blake, who says of the kKP edition that its text is unreliable and the punctuation in it has been modernised' (p. lxiii). In 1894 David Nutt published a metrical version, based on Caxton's text, by Ellis with illustrations by Walter Crane.

The book was anounc as in the press in the  lt of July 19. Printing actually began in late August or early September 1892. Two proofs of the engraved title  which was cut by Keates (Keates's bill, 3 January 1893: HEH H 3666)  are printe in red ink, possibly as an experiment (see b below).

For Morris's financial arrangements with Quaritch, see E4.

FOREwORD: AFTER a lapse of about three hun-/dred years Shakespeare's Poems are / in this edition printed with the origi-/nal spelling. That the orthography / of an author of the 16th or 17th cen-/tury cannot without detriment be / translated into that of the 19th, seems a proposition / too evident to leave room for controversy. The fact / of being able to study these poems in the language / in which they were writte, ought we think, to add / materially to the pleasure of the reader.

## A12 William Morris, News from Nowhere

[ornament 2] NEWS FROM NOWHERE: OR, / AN EPOCH REST, BEING SOME / CHAPTERS FROM A UTOPIAN RO-/MANCE, BY WILLIAM MORRIS.

COLopHon: This book, News from Nowhere or an Epoch / of Rest, was written by William Morris, and / printed by him at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex, and finished o the nd day  November,18. Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand, / London. / [printer's mark ]

O6 [ =312].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 205 × 140 mm. Text: 3o lines × 91 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Borders 9a and 4; six- and ten-line initials; shoulder notes and a few lines in red; wood-engraved frontispiece designed by C. M. Gere, with caption in Golden type: '[ornament I] THIS IS THE PICTURE OF THE OLD / HOUSE BY THE THAMES TO WHICH / THE PEOPLE OF THIS STORY WENT [ornament 3] / HEREAFTER FOLLOWS THE BOOK IT-/SELF WHICH IS CALLED NEWS FROM / NO-WHERE OR AN EPOCH OF REST &/ IS WRITTEN BY WILLIAM MORRIS [two ornaments 3]'.

COTents: [a]r-[a] blank [a]2 title [a]2 blank [a]3-[a] table of contents [a]4r blank [a]4 frontispiece b1-x1 text x1V colophon x2rx4v blank.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: 'NEWS / FROM / NOWHERE / BY / WILLIAM / MORRIS' [Golden type].

PUBLICATION: 300 paper copies (2 gns.); 10 vellum copies (10 gns.).   
Issued 24 March 1893.

NoTEs: New from Nowhere was printed in Commonweal, 11 January-4 October 189o. The first book edition was published by Roberts Brothers of Boston in 189o, and the first English edition was issued by Reeves & Turner in March 1891. The KP version 'was set up from a copy of one of Reeves & Turner's editions, and in reading it for the press the author made a few slight corrections' ('List').

The book was one of the earliest planned by Morris for his Press (Jane Morris to Wilfrid Scawen Blunt, 4 December [1890] [FM(I)]). On 21 June 1892 Morris wrote to his daughter Jenny: 'We are printing off News from Nowhere at present, as the Compositors were overrunning the press men. It looks very well' (BL Add. MS. 4534o, f. 85). The volume was delayed, however, by its frontispiece of Kelmscott Manor.

The genesis and progress of Gere's frontispiece can be traced in three entries in Cockerell's diary. 17 October 1892: 'I suggested that a picture of the house should be given as frontispiece to News from Nowhere and w.M. seemed to like the idea.' 1o November 1892: 'Saw proofs of some drawings of Kelmscott made by Gere, who is to make an illustration for News from Nowhere.' 24 January 1893: 'w.M. went to Kelmscott to meet Gere, who is drawing the house for "News from Nowhere".'

Printing of the frontispiece  which was engraved by Hooper (HEH HM 3665s)  was completed on 7 March 1893 (Morris diary)."

News fomNowere was the last book, except or Epistola  Contemptu Mundi (A31), to use paragraph symbols.
