## A13 [Ramón Lull], The Order of Chivalry, and L'Ordene de Chevalerie with a translation by Morris

THE ORDER OF CHIVALRY.

EXTRACT FROM ELLIS's 'MEMORANDA': [l6v] THE "Order of Chivayat xt o Fc whic themany r cript icep strange confusion has been made by various writers & bibliographers been this reatise nd a charmig it Frenc poem 3th y entit dne ChevaleiThis was frst pri Paris, 1808, from the text found in the MS. volume 25462, fo. 149157, i  Natal Libar  rs. [] Th   hee re from the text given in the "Fabliaux et Contes," 1808. . . . As the poem is ascribed to the 13th century, and the learned Director of the French National Library attributes the prose work to the 14th century, it might very well be that the author of the Ordre de Chevalerie was acquainted with the earlier work & might have been in some measure inspired by it. . . . [18r] The interest that it ['The Order of Chivalry'] has now as an historical document is considerable, and the wonder is that it has not been reprinted before this time in our own days.

NOTEs: There are frequent allusions to The Order of Chivalry in Cockerell's diary. 12 November 1892: 'Saw the sheets of Caxton's Order of Chivalry the printing of which is just complete. It is the prettiest book yet done.'3 December 1892: 'w.M. began the translation of a French poem from which Caxton seems to have derived the idea of the Order of Chivalry. It was wonderful to watch the words slipping into their places.' 27 January 1893: 'w.M. translating L'ordene de Chivalerie.' 31 January 1893: 'w.M. experimenting with lettering for the Order of Chivalry cut.' 13 February 1893: 'w.M. doing a border for the "Order of Chivalry" illustration.'2 March 1893: Morris showed Cockerell'proofs of the illustrations for the Order of Chivalry'.

The inclusion of L'Ordene was an afterthought; hence the book's peculiar format. (The first part of the volume represents the final use of the smaller Flower paper.) L'Ordene was initially translated into prose by Ellis before Morris decided to render it in verse (Mackail, ii, 295).

'It was the first book printed in Chaucer type. The reprint . . . was finished while News from Nowhere [A12] was in the press, and before Shakespeare's Poems and Sonnets [A11] was begun' ('List'). On 14 February 1893 Morris delivered the border to Hooper (Morris diary), who also engraved the frontispiece (Hooper's bill, 27 February 1893: HEH HM 36654). Some of the three-line initials were designed for The Well at the World's End (A39) but were used here for the first time.

For a more recent scholarly edition, see The Book of the Ordre of Cal A. .Byl arEh Text Scy, .

## A14George Cavendish, The Life of Cardinal Wolsey

THE LIFE OF THOMAS WOLSEY, / CARDINAL ARCH-BISHOP OF YORK / [OrnAMeNt 1] WRITTEN BY GEORGE CAVEN-/DISH.

COLOPHoN: Transcribed after the autograph manuscript of the / author, now in the British Museum, by F. S. Ellis, / and finished on the 25th day of December, in the year / 1892, in the Parish of Cockington in the County / of Devon. And printed by me William Morris, / at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, Hammer-/smith in the County of Middlesex, and finished / on the 3oth day of March, 1893. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by Reeves & Turner, 196, Strand.

FOREwoRD: THE author of this life of Cardinal / Wolsey held the Office of gentil-/man usher in his household from / 1526 till 1530. The book was writ-/ten about 1557. It was not printed / till nearly a hundred years later, but / was well known through the many manuscript / copies of it which were handed about, all of which / however were more or less garbled & incomplete. / From one of these imperfect copies an edition was / printed in 1641, & several other editions, all more / or less incomplete followed from time to time. At / the beginning of the present century Mrs. S. W. / Singer had the good fortune to light upon the au-/tograph MS. of the author, from which he printed / an edition prefaced by an elaborate introduction / and supplemented by very useful notes and ap-/pendices. He thought well however to modernize / the author's spelling, and occasionally to endeav-/our to clear or improve the style by slight altera-/tions. In a few instances the author's words were / inadvertently changed. The book is therefore now / for the first time put before the reader in the exact / form in which it was left by the author. One spe-/cial point of interest about it is, that it presents us / with the first separate biography in the English / language. But for it, we should have but a very imperfect idea of one of the most remarkable / figures in English history. Chroniclers, historians, / and dramatists have alike been indebted to it in / writing of Wolsey.

COLATION:80 [a]4 b-t8. 148 leaves, pp. [4] [i] ii-iv [=8] [1] 2-287 [288].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 205× 140 mm. Text: 30 lines × 91 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 1; six- and ten-line initials.

CONTENTs: [a]1r-[a]2r blank [a]2v foreword (by Ellis?) [a]3r title [a]3y-[a]4y prologue (by Cavendish) br-t7 text t8r colophon t8v blank.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: LIFE / OF / WOLSEY. / CAVENDISH' [Golden type; last ine reduced].

PUBLICATION: 250 paper copies (2 gns.); 6 vellum copies (10 gns.). Issued 3 May 1893.

NOTEs: The Ms which Singer used as the basis of his edition (2 vols., 1825) is BL Egerton 2402, which was acquired by the British Museum in 1876. As the colophon indicates, Ellis used the Ms rather than Singer's edition, but the editor of a modern scholarly edition (Richard S. Sylvester, ed., The Life and Death of Cardinal Wolsey by George Cavendish [Early English Text Society, 1959]) describes the κP version as 'shot through with errors' (p. xii).

There are no references to the book in Cockerell's diary. Morris, to hand'.

Halliday Sparling, / and printed by me, William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex, & finished on the 27th day / of April, 1893. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.

CATION: argπ []ave p 50 12.

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287 × 205 mm. Text: 32-3 lines × 126 mm.

TYPE: Text in Troy type; table of contents and glossary in Chaucer type.

ORNAMENTs: Borders 5a and 6; side, corner, half, and three-quarter borders; six-and eight-line initials; chapter titles in red.

conTeNTs: π1 title π1 blank π2π5V foreword (by Caxton) π5v [] able  contents [] blank [] ngraved titl b1"-2 ext 2giv Caxton's colophon 2g2r glossary 2g2v colophon.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: GODEFREY / OF / BOLOYNE' [Enlarged Troy type].

PUBLICATIoN: 300 paper copies (6 gns.); 6 vellum copies (20 gns.).   
Issued 24 May 1893.

Theollow oti thecmet ) Gofy was writte by Morris 'This book must from a liteary point f viw be considered one of Caxton's most important works. It is not a "ro-l  u serious piece of history, the original of which is William of Tyre's Historia Rerum in partibus transmarinis Gestarum; from the French translation of which Caxton made his English version, bringing it to an end just after the death of Godfrey, whereas the Archbishop of Tyre carries on the history for many years after. The style of Caxton's book is vigorous and agreeable, and often reads like the most dramatic of all historic works, Lord Berner's translation of Froissart, and altogether it is a delightful book to read.

![](images/3c57156aee50886cf1f240cfdb8ee0953bd76990077b3d3c9ac4fef5ed571500.jpg)  
Morris.

'There are many new ornaments in this the latest issue from the Kelmscott Press; for the chapters being very short and the scheme of ornament being very similar to that of the last two quartos, the Reynard and the Recuyell, the book is very decorative.'

The book does indeed contain a profusion of ornaments, many of which were engraved by Keates (see h below). The larger printer's mark was also used for the first time. In the proofs (see f below) there are numerous notes from Morris to his printers about the ornaments: e..1/ border o [ if it will not come in  border 9. but get it in if you can' (p. 341) and 1/2 border 5  but take care it does not print heavy' (p. 243).

Quaritch was very annoyed by Morris's decision to publish this, the last of the five Caxton reprints, himself: 'Mr. W. Morris wishes to have all the profits of the Kelmscott press; he will retail himself the [']Godfrey of Bouloyne"  he will be a loser by it, but it will be a nuisance to me on account of the Chaucer' (Quaritch to B. Alfred Quaritch, 8 February 1893 [Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 189]). Quaritch's prediction was accurate, for Godefrey did not sell well, and in 1897 Mrs. Morris donated a number of copies to public libraries.

Morris showed the first copy of Godefrey to Cockerell and others on the evening of 11 May 1893 (Cockerell diary).

PUBLICATION:300 paper copis (30s.); 8 vellum copies (0 gns.). Issued 8 September 1893.

NOTEs: The suggestion to print More's Utopia apparently originated with Cockerell on 29 February 1892 (Cockerell diary). The book was printed during July and early August 1893, and the cost of production was255 14.9. (Ledger). Morris wrote the foreword on2 July 1893 (Morris to Jenny Morris, 13 July 1893 [BL Add. MS. 45340, f. 122]).

The first English edition of Utopia was published in 1551 and the second edition (which Morris owned [Library, no. $6 4 7 ] ) \textrm { - }$ newlie pe n c n owiv o he mented'  in 1556. The printer of both editions was Abraham Vele. In a presentation copy of the KP volume, Ellis wrote: 'I cannot help feeling vexed at my name appearing as Editor opposite a palpable misprint $[ \mathrm { } ^ { 6 6 } \mathrm { W e a l e } ^ { 3 } ]$ of the first printer's name. I did correct it, but my proof was lost and the misprint left' (see o below). Robinson's translation had been reprinted several times during the nineteenth century.

MoretumaUtoa - peared in the first Latin edition of the work (see  below).

Aymer Vallance (William Morris [1897], p. 400) records an amusing anecdote: 'Of the 300 copies issued, 40 had been ordered in advance by an Eton master, with the intention of distributing them as prizes among the boys of the college, but when the work appeared with a compromisingly Socialistic introduction by Morris, the order, from motives of pruderce, had to be cancelled. However the copies were all disposed of before a year was out, so Morris did not suffer any loss.'

NOTES: Morris owned at least four volumes of Tennyson's poetry while an Oxford undergraduate: Poems, 1oth ed. $\left( \Im \ S \ - s \right)$ , In Memoriam, 6th ed. $\left( { \tt I 8 5 5 } \right)$ , Maud, and Other Poems $\left( { \tt I 8 5 5 } \right)$ , and The Princess, 6th ed. $\left( 1 8 5 4 \right)$ (all now at KM). After the publication of the KP edition of Maud, Morris confessed to Blunt: 'I would sooner . . . have printed them [Macmillan & Co.] Tennyson's first volume, which is all I ever cared for in his poems' (Blunt diary, 6 October 1893). On a later occasion Morris told Blunt that Tennyson 'never forgave him and Burne-Jones for having disapproved of his bowdlerization of the Morte d'Arthur in the "Idylls of the $\mathrm { K i n g } ^ { \prime \prime }$ (Blunt diary, 29 May 1896).

Maud, which was commissioned by Macmillan $\& \ \subset \mathbf { 0 } .$ , made a handsome profit for Morris: his production cost (including vellum copies) was $\mathcal { f } _ { 2 0 2 } \ \mathbf { I } _ { 4 } s .$ ered to Macmillan he charged £400 (see ES).

'The borders were specially designed for this book. They were both used again in the Keats [Å24], and one of them appears in The Sundering Flood $\left[ \mathrm { A } \varsigma \mathrm { I } \right] ^ { 3 } \left( ^ { \circ } \mathrm { L i s t } ^ { 3 } \right)$

On 14 November 1893 Bertram Dobell, the bookseller, wrote to Macmillan that a customer had returned his copy of Maud complaining of typographical errors on pp. 19, 26, and 69. Two days later Morris replied to Macmillan: 'I am sorry to say that as to the places quoted in Mr. Dobel's note, the facts are as stated; but having been very carefully over the book I can see no other errors. I will go over it again and when I have found out what the worst is, we will consider what is best to be done' (HEH HM 36673). As a result, four cancellans leaves were printed $\left( \mathrm { p p . ~ I 5 - I 6 , ~ I 9 - 2 0 , ~ } 2 . 5 \mathrm { - } 6 , 6 \mathrm { - } 7 0 \right)$ and were perhaps inserted in at least a few copies of Maud. The errors corrected were as p.16,1ehoul e 'hedp.19, 1.ohoule 'fro'; p.26, 1.18, 'where'should be 'were'; p.69, 1. I, 'and war should be 'and the war'.

## A18 William Morris, Gothic Architecture

## GOTHIC ARCHITECTURE: / A LECTURE FOR THE ARTS / AND CRAFTS EXHIBITION/ SOCIETY [OrHaMEnt 2] BY WILLIAM / MORRIS.

PUBLICArION: 1500 paper copies (2s. 6d.); 45 vellum copies (10s. during the Exhibition and 15s. afterwards). Printed during October and November 1893. First copies issued 1 October 1893.

NOTEs: Work on the book began in late August 1893 (Ledger). During October and November a press was moved to the New Gallery for the annual Arts and Crafts Exhibition, and Gothic Architecture 'was printed in public, under the eyes of an interested and constantly renewed crowd, whose presence imposed a severe strain upon the pressman Collins's Celtic modesty' (Sparling, p. 87). There were three impressions, presumably of soo copies each (though Cockerell, in a letter to Charles F. Carter of 8 February 1895, sets the total of paper copies at I54o [LB]): in the first impression 'Van Eyck' (p. 45, 1. I) was misspelled 'Van Eyk', and guilds' (p. 41, 1. 16) was misspelled gilds'. The second-state readings are present in both the second and third impressions and the vellum copies.

This was the first book printed in 16° at the Kp. At least one trial s oneyewT u tials used in it appear here for the first time' ('List ').

Cost of production was 165 18s. 5d. (Ledger).

## A19 William Meinhold, Sidonia the Sorceress

SIDONIA THE SORCERESS [ornament ] BY WILLIAM / MEINHOLD TRANSLATED BY FRANCESCA / SPE-RANZA LADY WILDE.

COLoPHoN: Here ends the story of Sidonia the Sorceress, translated from the / German of William Meinhold, by Francesca Speranza, Lady / Wilde, and now reprinted by me, William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex. Finished on the 15th day of September, 1893. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.

CLar°a]vpp =16] [] 2-455 [456].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 287205 mm. Tex: 41 lines × 126 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

EBore;nte;hate ers' names, and side notes in red.

]    ] by Meinhold) a pitlefrom Theodore Plonnies to Bogislaff the Fuh b1-2g4" text 2g4 colophon.

BING: Limell witk  Stan oln spi 'SI-DONIA / THE / SORCERESS' TrO tpe]

PUBICA .

TE of Sidonia: 'sIDONIA THE SORCERESS, the translation of which is now on the point of being issued by the Kelmscott Press, is an Historical Romance, based more or less on fact, concerning the Witch Fever that afflicted Northern Europe during the latter half of the 15th & first half of the 16th centuries. It was written by William Meinhold, a Lutheran minister, dwelling in the island of Rugen, off the shore of Pomerania, a man so steeped in the history and social life of his country during the period above-mentioned, that he might almost be said to have been living in it, rather than in his own, the early part of the present century. The result of his life and literary genius was the production of two books: "The Amber Witch," & "Sidonia the Sorceress," both of which, but, in my judgment, especially "Sidonia," are almost faultless reproductions of the life of the past; not mere antiquarian studies, but presentations of events, often tragic, the actors in which are really alive, though under conditions so different from those of the present day. In short, "Sidonia" is a masterpiece of its kind, and without a rival of its kind. It must be added that it was a great favourite with the more literary part of the pre-Raphaelite artists in the earlier days of that movement. The two beautiful water-colour pictures of Sidonia and of Clara von Dewitz, by Mr. Burne-Jones, which the general public had the opportunity of seeing at the exhibition of his pictures this spring, caused many questions to be asked about this, which the present edition of the book will answer satisfactorily. Lady Wilde's translation, which was the one through which we made acquaintance with Meinhold's genius, is a good, simple, and sympathetic one.'

Lady Wilde's translation of Sidonia the Sorceress, which was published in the Parlour Library in 1849, was well known to Morris's circle: Rossetti in particular was an enthusiastic admirer of it, and Burne-Jones produced two water-colours based on the story in 1860. Oscar Wilde's mother doubtless appreciated Morris's cheque for 25 (see d below), for she was living in extreme poverty in the 1890s.

Through the intervention of Aymer Vallance, Aubrey Beardsley prepared a drawing of Sidonia intended as a frontispiece, which he showed to Morris in the spring or early summer of 1892. Morris's comments, though he praised Beardsley's "'feeling for draperies', were so discouraging that the young artist abandoned the attempt, and thereafter Beardsley expressed great hostility towards Morris and the KP (Vallance, 'The Invention of Aubrey Beardsley', Magazine of Art, May 1898, pp. 363-4; Vallance, 'List of Drawings by Aubrey Beardsley', in Robert Ross, Aubrey Beardsley [1921], p. 71).

Work began on Sidonia in the first half of 1893 (Ledger); on 13 April

1893 Morris recorded in his diary: 'I have now got 2/2 sections of Sidonia printed.' Cost of production was 584 18s. (Ledger). The book did not sell briskly: on 0 October 1894 Cockerell instructed Leighton to bind thirty copies 'in half holland uniform with the Golden Legend' (LB), and these were later donated to British and American libraries.

After Sidonia's publication, F. S. Elis wrote to Morris on 6 January 1894 listing the printer's errors he had found in it: 'I have just finished reading aloud "Sidonia" & am more impressed than ever with its excellence. . . . To come to the point there are certainly more misprints than there ought to be. Pray dont think that I consider myself perfect as a press-reader, far from it & it is a consciousness of the difficulty of one man getting a thing perfect that makes me propose that you should send me a proof simultaneously with your other reader, simply on the principle that four eyes are better than two. In case you have not had your attention calleéd to what I mean I will be the "damned goatu fiend[] . (. ), i"for g"3 4I5. 1. 27 "navitate" for "nativitate" p. 419. 1. I5 "coach" for "couch" rter1. r The re but a few that I have notd, or when I had no pencl t hand I didn't mark 'em. The same (in a less degree) is the case with GoreyPerhaps I amne of the few people who have read all the books you have printed' (BL Add. MS.715, f.11).

## A2o Dante Gabriel Rossetti, Ballads and Narrative Poems

BALLADS AND NARRATIVE POEMS / BY DANTE GA-BRIEL ROSSETTI.

ENGRAVED TITLE: BALLADS / AND / NARRATIVE / POEMS BY / DANTE / GABRIEL / ROSSETTI [roman lettering designed by Morris]

coLoPHoN: [ornament 1] Here ends the Book of Ballads and Narrative / Poems, written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, and / printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott / Press, 14 Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the / County of Middlesex; finished on the 14th day / of October, of the year 1893. / [printer's mark 1] / Published by Ellis & Elvey, 29 New Bond Street.

COLATION: 80°: [a]4 b-p8 q4. 120 leaves, pp. [8] [1]2227 [228] [4] =232].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 205 × 140 mm. Text: 2731 lines × 92 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Borders 4a and 4; six- and ten-line initials; some lines and shoulder titles in red.

CONTENTS: $\ [ \mathsf { a } ] _ { } \mathbf { I } ^ { \mathsf { r } } - \bigl [ \mathsf { a } \bigr ] \mathsf { 2 ^ { v } }$ blank [a]3 title [a]3 table of contents [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title b1r-q1V text q2r colophon q2v−q4 blank.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: 'BALLADS /ÁND / POEMS / BY / D. G. / ROSSETTI' [reduced Golden type].

PUBLICATION: 310 paper copies (2 gns.); 6 vellum copies (10 gns.).   
Issued November 1893.

NOTEs: In the Ellis papers at UCLA there are frequent references to this and its companion volume, Sonnets and Lyrical Poems bγ Dante Gabriel Rossetti (A2oa). Early in July 1893 Morris agreed to print Rossetti's poems for Ellis & Elvey, recommending that they be divided into two volumes and that his charge per copy (for each volume) would be 21s. (see E6). By 12 July Ellis & Elvey reported to William Michael Rossetti, who held his brother's literary rights, that the first volume which Morris suggested calling Ballads and Tales in Verse  would be ready by the end of November. The title was slightly altered at the request of W. M. Rossetti, who read proofs and carefully supervised the book.

One issue that arose during the printing of Sonnets and Lyrical Poems was whether the sonnet 'Nuptial Sleep' should be restoréd to The House of Life. D. G. Rossetti had deleted the sonnet in 1872 after it was brutally attacked by Robert Buchanan in his notorious essay 'The Fleshly School of Poetry', and William Michael, in a preface to his brother's Collected Works (1886), explained that 'his own decision commands mine' (I, xxxiii). W. M. Rossetti again declined to allow the sonnet to appear in the KP edition; he restored it to the text of The House of Life only in 1904.

W. M. Rossetti submitted the table of contents for the second volume in early November 1893, and on 27 December Ells & Elvey sent him proofs of the first sheet. The KP Ledger is not entirely clear in distributing costs between the two volumes, but it appears that the production cost of Sonnets and Lyrical Poems was £202 s. On 6 July 1894 Ellis & Elvey wrote to W. M. Rossetti: 'The Kelmscott Press Edi-. 264 copies of vol 1 & 265 of vol 11 have been sold altogether.'

Meanwhile, in America, Copeland & Day had early in 1894 produced an edition of The House of Life, done in close imitation of the Kelmscott sye, which troubled both Morris and Els & Elvey. 'However much we may disapprove of this publication, & the bad e    e Elvy dclar Rossettn February 9opeand&Day a sent a copy of the book to Ellis & Elvey.)

Burne-Jones to Sir Sydney Cockerell asking for a copy of the Poems' (Cockerell sale, no. 23). (c) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 17 December 1893 (PL). (d) Pres. copy to Philip Webb, 14 December 1893 (Tc). (e) Pres. copy to Jenny Morris (FM[1]). (f) Harry Buxton Forman's copy (BC). E: The source of this and two other κ books  Of the Friendhip of Amis and Amile (A23) and The Tale of the Emperor Coustans and of Over Sea (A26)  was Nouvelles françaises en prose du xiième siècle by L. Moland and C. D'Hericault (Paris, 1856). Morris's translations were later collected in a single volume entitled Old French Romances Done into English bγ William Morris, published by George Allen in 1896 with an introduction by Joseph Jacobs, who described the tales 'as a sort of outhouse to that goodly fabric so appropriately known to us all as The Earthly Paradise' (p. xxv).

Allen, incidentally, wished to reprint the kP engraved titles, but Morris declined permission (Cockerell to Allen, 23 November 1895 [LB]). Hooper's bills for engraving the titles of King Florus (24 November 1893) and Amis and Amil ( March 1894) ar n HEH HM 3655.

Cost of production was £71 16s. 1d. (Ledger).

Seventy-six sets of sheets of King Florus were distributed by Tregaskis to the world's leading binders, and the results were exhibited in London between 27 June and 7 July 1894. (See the Studio, 3 [July 1894], 1256, and 'A Memorable Exhibit of Bookbinding', British Printer, 7 [July-August 1894], 13.) These copies are now in the John Rylands Library, Manchester.

## A22 William Morris, The Story of the Glittering Plain

NOTEs: This has the odd distinction of being the only title printed twice at the kP. Morris was so eager to get the first edition (Å1) into print that he would not wait for Crane's illustrations; but later (probably in the autumn of 1892) Morris again approached Crane about illustrating a new edition (see F3), with the profits from the book to be split between them. Crane earned at least iso for his contribution to the book (Cockerell to A. J. R. Gooderson, 27 September 1894 [LB]). Crane's designs were cut on wood by A. Leverett. 'I think your woodcuts look delightful,' Morris wrote to Crane on 17 February 1894 (letter privately printed by William P. Barlow, Jr., 1975). Crane himself, however, doubted if I was ever quite Gothic enough in feeling t is r asTe WrkWal ane  Jul, Easter Annual, 1898, p. 10).

Philip Webb, in a letter to Morris, 18 February 1894, commented: The bok is quiteup  heTha ark  l ut he pi, and these, if one is careful in looking, have their value: but Crane will not (or can-not) take pains. A lot of work in these blocks is good really good  but the inaptitudes are abundant. . . . However, do not mind  the whole book looks delightful; and, for the pictures . .. why, they are more than helped out by the borders, and the heavyline engraving of the subjects. But the features; why, they are killéd by Crane's no-notion of surface, & of black[,] white and grey. Also, costume is before the figure it clothes. I cannot see the difficulty with a knowledge of old time  of inventing costume. Alack  but still, it don't much matter; but, again: do not be so rash in trusting the picture makers in future. How can they know?' (v & A[1] 86.TT.3). Cockerell a thi not  Welet'o was o s than Webb with Crane'ilustratons to his Gitterig Painhht this volume his one Kelmscott Press failure.'

Morris designed the borders in late 1892 and early 1893 (Cockerell diary, 1 November 1892; Morris diary, 18 March 1893). 'Neither the borders in this book nor six out of the seven frames round the illustrations appear in any other book. The seventh is used round the second picture in Love is Enough [A52]'List'. The engraved title was cut by Hooper (Hooper's bill, 1s December 1893: HEH HM 36655).

The copytext was not the earlier kP edition but the edition printed in 1891 at the Chiswick Press (see c below). The names of the following compositors appear in the marked text: H. H.[owes], Binning, Tippett, and Collins.

The cost of production was £485 18s. 8d. (Ledger). The book sold slowly, and on 11 October 1894 Cockerell instructed Leighton to 'bind u G iiolla

BNNG: Lim vell with i s.Stape i old n ne: THE POEMS / OF / JOHN KEATS.' [Golden type].

:0 p cp . p 9s.. Issued 8 May 1894.

NOTES: On 25 May 1893 Ellis wrote to Harry Buxton Forman: 'Morris is printing at the Kelmscott Press a small edition of certain Poems of    th which e e wort pg & I   i through the press for him.

'Would you mind telling me if any of Keats'Poems are at present copyright, as he would of course omit them.

It will simply [be] a hanomey rint vole wthut ot any kind' (BC).

Sheet y had to be reprinted because the wrong version of Keats's 'La Belle Dame sans Merci' was used. A slightly garbled text of the poem had appeared in Leigh Hunt's Indicator in 1820 and was occasionally reprinted thereafter, and it was this inferior version which had been set up by the kP compositors. Cockerell records Morris's reaction: 'In February 1894 the last sheets of the Kelmscott Press Keats . . . were being printed. A specimen of each sheet of every book was brought in to Morris as soon as it came off the press. I was with him when he happened to open the sheet on which La Belle Dame sans

Merci was printed. He began to read it and was suddenly aware of unfamiliar words. . . . Great was his indignation. He swiftly altered the words and then read the poem to me, remarking that it was the germ from which all the poetry of his group had sprung  The sheet was reprinted and the earlier and better version restored  I still have the cancelled sheet with his corrections' (Sidney Colvin, John Keats: His Life and Poetry, His Friends, Critics and After-Fame [1918], p. 470).

As an undergraduate Morris owned the 1854 edition of Keats's Poetical Works (now at km), and his own poetry, as he admitted, shows indebtedness to Keats (see Clarice Short, 'William Morris and Keats', PMLA, 59 [June, 1944], 51323).

Cockerell saw the first page of the KP Keats on 8 October 1893 (diary). Cost of production was £372 8s. 1od. (Ledger). The book was popular and went out of print very quickly.

RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Inc. corrected proofs, including rejected version of 'La Belle Dame sans Merci' (BL C.43.c.26 [ΣQ26]). (b) Inc. proofs (Sotheby cat., 22 December 1952, no. 87). He read out the poem to me and added, "That was the beginning of all our school'"(Cockerell's note) (Pm 'Morrisiana 11'). COLOPHON: HERE ends Atalanta in Calydon, a Tragedy, made / by Algernon Charles Swinburne, and printed by / William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middlesex; / finished on the 4th day of May, 1894. Macmillan & Co., who have kindly / allowed them to be used here. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.

CLAT: Large 4°: [a]4 b-f8 g4. 6 leave, pp.[8] [1] 281 [824].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 289 × 210 mm. Text: 31 lines × 130 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Borders a and ; side and three-quarter borders; three six, egh and eleven-lineinitials; row aments on [a]v and speakers' names (usually in margin) in red.

ConTenTs: [a]1r title and Greek epigraph [a]v-[a]2v dedication to Walter Savage Landor and poem in Greek [a]3 dramatis personx and quotation from AEschylus [a]3 argument [a]4° blank [a]4 engraved title b1r-g1 text g1V colophon g2r-g4 blank.

PUBLICATIoN: 250 paper copies (2 gns.); 8 vellum copies (12 gns.).   
Issued 24 July 1894.

NoTEs: Swinburne was a warm admirer of the kP books, and he and

Morris maintained a friendly but not intimate relationship. Wilfrid Scawen Blunt recorded in his diary on 26 May 1896: 'Swinburne's new poem was reviewed yesterday in all the papers. Morris thinks it poor stuff and not worth doing as the story Balin and Balan was quite perfect in its prose form in the Morte d'Arthur. "It will not do however" he said "for Swinburne to hear me saying this, for he would never forgive me". Swinburne, it appears[,] is the most sensitive and jealous of men and cannot bear the smallest criticism, but he and Morris have not met for years though Mrs. Morris goes now and then to see Swinburne.' When Atalanta (which was first published in 1865) was printed by Morris, Swinburne wrote to him that it was 'certainly one of the loveliest examples of even your incomparable press' (John Y. LeBourgeois, 'Some Unpublished letters of Swinburne', Notes and Queries, NS 19 [July 1972], 262). Morris's own comment, in a letter to Theodore Watts, 9 August 1894, was 'I like the look of the volume very much' (BCNY).

This is the only kP book in which Morris used a type not designed by himself. During 1892 Selwyn Image, with the technical assistance of Emery Walker, designed a Greek type based 'on a certain tenth century Ms in the B. Museum, and on a Spanish printed book there of the fifteenth or early sixteenth century, which was itself founded on a MS of the Ioth century' (Seluγn Image Letters, ed. A. H. Macmurdo [1932], p. 75). A series of letters from Image to Macmillan & Co. about the designing of the type is in BL Add. Ms. 5526o. The type was first used in Archer Hind's edition of the Phædo (1894). On 27 April 1894 Morris wrote to Macmillan: '. . . I venture to ask you to be kind enough to lend me some of your new Greek type for the quotations & dedicatory poems. In the event of your not wishing the type to leave your hands, the passages might perhaps be set up by you & stereotyped  in which case I may mention that I propose to have them in shrhout, iut leadi  iA. ., In he Milla uppc

In the vellm copies of Atalanta, the colophonappears n .

T out within a few weeks of publication.

## A27 William Morris, The Wood beyond the World

THE WOOD BEYOND THE WORLD. / BY WILLIAM MORRIS.

COLOPHON: HERE ends the tale of the Wood beyond / the World, made by William Morri, and/printed by him at he Kelscot ress, / Upper Mall, Hammersmith. Finished the / 3oth day of May, 1894. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by William Morris, at the Kelmscott / Press.

CLATION: 80: [a]4 b-r8 s4. 136 leaves pp. [8] [1] 2-261 [262] [2] [=264].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207 × 140 mm. Text: 27 lines × 80 mm.

TYPE: Chaucer type.

ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Borders 13a and 13; side, half, and three-quarter borders; three, six-, and ten-line initials; chapter titles and shoulder notes in red; wood-engraved frontispiece designed by Burne-Jones.

CET: [a]1-[a]2 blank [a]3 title [a]3v-[a]4 blank [a]4 frontispiece b1r-2 text s3 colophon s3v-s4v blank.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: THE WOOD / BEYOND / THE WORLD / BY / WILLIAM / MORRIS' [Golden type].

PUBLICATIoN: 350 paper copies (2 gns.); 8 vellum copies (0 ns.).   
Issued 16 October 1894.

NOTEs: The Wood Beyond the World was written by Morris specifically for the kP: hence in the ms (see a below) he indicated the positions of decorated initials and leaf ornaments. The book was printed during the spring of 1894 but was not issued until the following autumn because of a delay in completing the frontispiece (Ledger; Sparling, p. 79).

![](images/bcbcfd51afbf5bf378bd2278847475ce07cbd2ce639231ee46d5718e15a414f9.jpg)  
The fonpe euc The Wo beyn the Wrld Adese y Eward Burne-Jones, with the border designed by Morris. The design is in the Beinecke Library, Yale University.

![](images/cfc113b87c74901ef9abf9e66f9453f459ec2b5b0627762c67f5e6a45a482f84.jpg)

The wood beyondthe world Chapter I. Of Golden Walter and his father

![](images/b757ba52939d8d053e8ed2e261965e1fea3df0a4d93a1bd67fb7ced121efd944.jpg)

UHILE HGO THERE WAS AHUNGMAN DELLINGIN AGREATAND goodly city by the sea which had to name Langton on Holm. He was but of five and

twenty winters, a fair faced man, yellow haired, tall and strong; rather wiser than foolisher than young men are mostly wont; a valiant youth, & a kind; not of many words but courteous of speech; noroisterer, noughtmasterful, but peaceable and knowing how to forbear: in a fray a perilous foe, & a trusty warsfellow. His father, with whom he was dwelling when this tale begins, was a great merchant, richer than a baron of the land, a head man of the greatest of the Lineages of Langton, and a captain of the Porte; he was of the Lineage of the Goldings, therefore was he called

in red.

The borders in this book, as well as the ten half-borders, are here used for the first time' ("List'). The quarter sheet d2.7 (pp. 356, 456) had to be reprinted because the phrase (on p. 46) 'somewhat uneasy going' was mistakenly printed as "somewhat easy going' (see e below). Burne-Jones was paid 2o for designing the frontispiece; the total cost of production was £330 18s. 5d. (Ledger).

By the date of publication - which Cockerell gives in a letter (to Miss E. M. Evans, 19 October 1894 [LB]) as 18 rather than 16 October - all but a few copies had been sold."On 21 February 1895 Morris arranged for a cheap edition to be issued by Lawrence & Bullen: his royalties from that edition in 1895 were 69 (Cockerell diary, 21 February 1895 and 20 January 1896).

PUBLICATIoN: 250 paper copies (2 gns.); no vellum copies. Issued 29 October 1894.

NOTEs: On 8 March 1894 Bernard Quaritch wrote to Morris: 'Would you allow me to print at the Kelmscott Press a small volume: The Book of Wisdom and Lies[,] a Georgian storybook translated by Wardrop[.] The edition to be 25o, and you to undertake the supervision (not the proof-reading) to supply paper, reproduce a portrait etc.

'I have the translators Manuscript' (HEH HM 36666).

Two days later Quaritch added: 'I have sent the Ms. of the Georgian Tales to your address by parcel post.

There is no necessity of giving a portrait and the Arms of Georgia.

'If you accept the printing of the Ms. it shall be with the understanding that neither I nor the translator interfere with you' (HEH HM 36667).

On 17 March Morris sent Quaritch three sets of two specimen pas (see Di.6) with this note: 'I shall be glad to print it for you, and will do 25o copies at 1.5.0 per copy delivered to you bound in wrapping vellum with silk strings in the manner of my Kelmscott Press boboks (Q).

Quaritch was pleased with the specimen pages and accepted Morris's terms on 19 March. 'I think the volume will form an original and respectable link in the series of Kelmscott press publications,' he declared (HEH HM 36668).

Morris completed the design for the engraved title on 22 August 14 The Letters f William Morrs to His Family and Friends, ed. Philip Henderson [1950], p. 360). He purchased ten copies of the book for his own use (Cockerell to Quaritch, 10 October 1894 [LB]).

Cost of production was £277 2s. 8d. (Ledger).

N: Elis wrote to Morris n 6 January 1894: 'I quite agree with you as to the doubt whether so big a book as Shelley ought to make.

'I esteem all his work good except his juvenilia (written before Q.[ueen] Mab.). To make a volume of his shorter poems & lyrics would seem to me mean & unfair[.] But I doubt extremely if 3 fattish volumes would pay & I think perhaps therefore it is better to let him alone' (BL Add.ms. 52715, f. 11). Ellis promised to consult Swinburne about the matter. On 17 January Cockerell recorded the decision in his diary: 'w.. is going to print a Shelley.

The usual silk ties were not used in the Shelley.'The ties were oited from the Shelley as an experiment, as some people don't care for them,' Cockerell wrote to Stopford Brooke on 14 December 1894, 'but except in the 2 other volumes of this work, they will appear in all future books that are bound in vellum' (LB). In a letter to the publishers Dodd, Mead & Co., 29 March 1895, Cockerell said, 'I have had no other complaints of the binding of the Shelley, though, as the volu  thick, Istand thejen. It is  re bind the 3 vols. uniformly' (LB).

The vellum copies, however, do have ties.

'Some of the half-borders designed for The Wood beyond the World [A27] reappear before the longer poems' ('List ).

Cost of production for the three volumes was 832 4s. (Ledger).

On 22 March 1895 Cockerell sent to Leighton a list of those who were to receive complimentary copies of volume ii: Burne-Jones, Georgiana Burne-Jones, Philip Webb, Emery Walker, Miss Burden, Theodore Watts, Swinburne, Miss Kate Faulkner, May Sparling, F. Smith (Morris & Co.), and R. Smith (Morris & Co.) (LB).

## A30 Psalmi Penitentiales

## PSALMI PENITENTIALES.

COLOpHoN: THUS ends this rhymed version of the / Penitential Psalms [ornament 1] Found in a Manu-/script of Horæ Beatæ Mariæ Virginis, wri-tent Glouceter about the year 1440, and/now transcribed and edited by F. S. Ellis. / Printed by William Morris at the Kelms-/cott Press, 14, Upper Mall, Hammersmith. / Finished on the 15th day of November, 1894. / [printer's mark 1]

COLLATION: $8 ^ { \mathrm { o } } : [ { \mathrm { a } } ] ^ { 4 } \ { \mathrm { b } } - { \mathrm { e } } ^ { 8 } .$ 36eave pp. [] [63 [].

A & NSN: Fr( pape. L2070 . ex: 26 lines (including blank lines)88m.

TYPE: Chaucer type.

ORNAMeNrs: Three-quarter borders; three- and six-line initials;   
Latin texts in red.

CONTenTs: [a]1r-[a]4r blank [a]4y title b1r-e6r text e6 colophon $\mathrm { e } 7 ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } \mathrm { e } 8 ^ { \mathrm { r } }$ glossary e8v blank.

e printed in black on front cover.

:0 p cp   p  s.   
Issued 1o December 1894.

NoTEs: '. . . the Rev. Professor Skeat has pointed out that the scribe must have copied them from an older manuscript, as they are in the Kentish dialect of about a century earlier. The half-border on p. 34 appears for the first time in this book' ('List ).

Ellis made considerable alterations in the text and consulted Morris and F. J. Furnivall while editing the volume (see b below). As usual, he and Morris disagreed about the editorial apparatus. On 2 November 1894 Morris wrote to him: 'Tis clear that I dont take the same view of a glossary as you do; I dont think we want a series of synonyms, but in each case the exact modern equivalent of the word, and the new word should be the nearest possible, e.g. not "rigid" for stef", but stiff. In short all we want is some /2 dozen words like ord (Iclandic oddi) point, or thorns. Otherwise we have something which a reader of old English doesn't want, and which is not enough for a non-reader, who would want every word respelt or p[a]raphrased. Please excuse me; but you told me to knock the glossary about; now I have done so' (PML).

Cost of production was £71 13s. (Ledger). By 4 October 1894 the entire edition was subscribed for (Cockerell to Messrs. Cornish Bros. [LB]); hence it was not included in the κp list of  December 1894 C28).

The book evoked one of Mrs. Morris's rare comments (in a letter to Blunt, 1 December [1894]): 'Some small books are being printed at [the] same time [as the Chaucer], one is quite lovely, the penitential psalms in mediaeval verse  with a great deal of red  I fear it is all sold, those small things go off at once before one knows about them often' (FM[I]).

RELATED MATERIALS: (a) MS in Ellis's hand of glossary, with revisions in Morris's hand (PML). (b) Inc. corrected proofs and revises (PML). (d) Cockérell's copy, with a letter from Skeat, 8 January 1895, 'concerning the Ms.' (Cockerell sale, no. 33; now [book only] BC). (e) Ellis's copy, with a letter from Skeat in which he describes the translation as 'quite a nugget' (Hodgson cat., 3 July 1922, no. 77). (f) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 9 December 1894 (Hodgson cat., 9 December 1937, no. 27). (g) Pres. copy to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 9 December 1894 (wMG). (h) Pres. copy from Jane H. Hooper.Tutt nat aiisnati  GugelMois. /agaph mbo iueta izie prvat at /eele'editore, sono state tirate / cento cinquanta copie in carta, & sei in / pergamena. / [x81] [paragraph symbol] Impresso in London per Guglielmo / Morris alla Stamperia Kelmscott. / Adi ultimo di novembre Mdccclxxxxiv. / [printer's mark 1]

COLLATION: 8°: χ8. 8 leaves, pp. [1-3] 4-15 [16].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 207 × 140 mm. Text: 27 lines × 79 mm.

TYPE: Chaucer type.

ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATION: Border I; six-line initial; conjeCturally restored words and printer's mark in red; wood-engraved illusraia r.

blank.

BNDG:Quartolland (bluepapen boards). Notit  ot cover.

12 December 1894.

NOTEs: Charles Fairfax Murray began working for Morris & Co. in the 87 s n artst ncpyt (Intis, i, 6; fr may yrs he maintained a close relationship with Morris, Ruskin, Rossetti, and Burne-Jones, and in the latter part of his life he became celebrated as a connoisseur and collector. (See Carole Cable, 'Charles Fairfax Murray, Assistant to Dante Gabriel Rossetti', Library Chronicle of the University of Texas, NS 10 [1978], 819.)

'The little Psalmi Penitentiales, said Morris, 'has not only an archalogical buta veryhigh tay valueIdal Book,..

Since the text is in Italian, there is an unusually large number of typographical errors in the proofs (see d below). Cost of production was 8 4s. 7d. (Ledger).

On 28 December [1894] Ellis wrote to Murray, 'By no means the smallest value of the book is your lovely design on the title. If I do not entirely give credence to demoniacal possession I shall hereafter be a believer in angelical possession for the spirit of Fra Angelico or one of his friends must have taken possession of your fingers when you drew that exquisite design, instinct with the feeling of the I5th century' (PM L). Murray's wood-engraving was a close adaption of a woodcut in Epistola di Frate Hieronymo da Ferrara dellordine de frati predicatori a uno Ámico (Florence, n.d. [late sixteenth century]). Murray also drew a few details from another woodcut portrait of Savonarola at his writing desk which appears, with slight variations, in a number of works by Savonarola that were printed in Florence and Venice in the early sixteenth century.

The book was privately printed for Murray and was of course not for sale at the kp. Hence it is probably the only k P book that was ever inscribed to Morris (see k below).

RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Preliminary drawing for illustration (CL 'red portfolio'). (b) Three sets of proofs, the first two corrected (Cockerell sale, no.). According to May Morris, 'When finished the tale turned out to be so much longer than intended for a small book that, to meet the additional expense of production, the issue was increased to more than double the usual number. But for an edition of six hundred my father thought the price (fifteen shilings) so high that, to add to the attraction, he gave notice that he undertook not to eprint it in anycheape editi(Intrductions, i, ).

In an interview in 1895, Morris said, 'When you come to look at it, and see that you get a book like this . . for seven and sixpence avol ume, it would be impossible to refuse buying them. I love my books, lohh most charming thing ued from y pre (Ideal Book, .1).

## A36 Dante Gabriel Rossetti, Hand and Soul

## HAND AND SOUL. BY DANTE / GABRIEL ROSSETTI.

ENGRAVED TITLE: HAND & / SOUL / BY / DANTE / GA-BRIEL / ROSSETTI [roman lettering designed by Morris]

COLOPHON (English edition): [ornament 2] HERE ends Hand and Soul, / written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, / and reprinted from The Germ for / Messrs. Way and Williams of / Chicago, by William Morris, at / the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith [ornament

1] Finished the / 24th day of October, 1895. / [paragraph] [ornament 2] Sold by William Morris at / the Kelmscott Press.

COLOPHON (American edition): [ornament 2] HERE ends Hand and Soul, / written by Dante Gabriel Rossetti, / and reprinted from The Germ by / William Morris, at the Kelms-/cott Press, Upper Mall, Hammer-/smith, England [ornament 1] Finished the / 24th day of October, 1895. / [paragraph] [ornament 1] Sold by Way and Williams, / Chicago.

CLATI 16°: [a]4 b-83 leav pp.[8] [1]2-56 [2] =58.

PAPER & DIMENsIONs: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 142 × 102 mm. Text: 19 lines × 63 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTs: Borders I5a and 15; four- and six-line initials; epigraph, title on b1r, shoulder notes, footnote on er, and first paragraph of colophon in red.

CoTents: [a]1-[a]2v blank [a]3r title [a]3y epigraph [a]4 blank [a]4 engraved title b1-e4° text e4y colophon.

BINDING: Stiff vellum. Stamped in gold on spine: 'HAND AND SOUL' [reduced Golden tγpe].

PUBLICATIon: In England, 225 paper copies (10s.); 10 vellum copies (3os.). In America, 300 paper copies (price not known); 11 vellum copies (\$3.50). Issued 12 December 1895.

NOTEs: Rossetti wrote this story in December 1849 for the first number of the Germ. It was set in type for the 1869 edition of Rossetti's Poems, but the author decided not to include it and instead had a few copies printed for private distribution to his friends. The text was reprinted with minor alterations in the Fortnightly Review, 8 (December 1870), 692702.

On 29 April 1895 W. Irving Way, the Chicago publisher, called on Sydney Cockerell at the κp:  . . I ventured to remark that a new departure in a business way had made a trip to England necessary in order to establish some business connections there. My first thought was that Mr. Morris would consent to print a book for us, something that oaChiC off the press, and"Chaucer" was progressing satisfactorily, there was a short interim in which Mr. Cockerell thought something might be reprint of Rossettis "Hand and Soul" among the possibilities.'Cockerell took Way over to Kelmscott House to meet Morris, who agreed to the joint venture. (Way, 'A Visit to William Morris', Modern Art [Boston], 4 [Summer, 1896], 798o0.)

On 26 June 1895 Cockerell sent Way & Williams an estimate (see the KP would charge five shillings per paper copy and fifteen hi  ll yI   ta u, Cockerellexplained that the prices quoted did not include carriage to the USA. 'On the copies sold through our agency in London, Mr. Morris would expect a discount of 25% to cover discount to trade, ent  i  o each paper copy sold. . . .

Printing began on 12 September 1895 (Cockerell diary), and on 14 December the American copies were shipped on the s.s. Untria (Cockerell to U.S. Express Company, 17 December 1895 [LB]). Cockerell wrote to Way & Williams on 17 December, 's20 paper copies were printed, leaving 19o for us to sell after sending s to the Libraries, & deducting 25 for Mr. Morris' use. . . . I am sorry that we only had 6 vellum copies left when your letter asking for 12 reached us. . . . we find that the price you are paying us for the books will only just cover the epenss ( t d that), he os  indng havig be e than was anticipated' (LB).

In January 1896 Morris had twenty more copies bound up from overs, of which he sent eight to America. 'This makes the total number bound s4o i.e., IS more than the number announced,' Cockerell said in a letter to Way & Williams on I4 January, 'But there are always a few overs, & this is not an extravagant number proportion' (LB).

Way & Williams proposed another title, but Morris declined, being 'full of plans of his own' (Cockerell to Way & Williams, 2 February 1896 [LB]).

Cost of production was 105 3s. 9d. (Ledger).

Hand and Soul was the only kp book published in America and the only 16° book printed on vellum.

RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Corrected (by Morris and Cockerell) proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 69; now Asu). (b) Inc. proofs (Saks sale, no. 157). (c) 'Sheet e of both issues, showing the different colophons' (Cockérell's note) [pML 'Morrisiana II'). (d) Cockerell's copy, with design for one initial and '17 leaves (" overs") PRINTED ON VELLUM, showing the two different colophons' (Cockerell sale, no. 39; Blackwell cat. 655, no. 384). (e) Pres. copy to Burne-Jones, 15 December 1895 (DC). (f) Pres. copy to Theodore Watts, I5 January 1896 (mc). (g) Pres. copy to A. C. COLLATION: 80 a4 b-f8 [g]8 h². 54 leaves, pp. [4] [i] ii [2] [=8] [] 2100.

PAPER & DIMeNsIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 206 × I4I mm. Text: 29-31 lines × 93 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTS: Borders 13a and 13; three-quarter borders; six- and tenline initials; shoulder titles, note on $\mathrm { f } _ { 4 } \mathrm { r }$ , and side notes and a few lines in 'The Rime of the Ancient Mariner' in red.

CONTENTS:a $\cdot \mathbf { r } - \mathbf { a } 2 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ blank  tie  table  contents 4blank a4v engraved title brh2 text h2v colophon.

NOTEs: On 6 January 1894 Ellis wrote to Morris, 'What do you think of Coleridge? his poems, excluding of course the dramas, would make a volume which would answer Mr. Wemmicks desideratum [possibly 'for it's a principle with me, if you have an idea, carry it out and keep it up' - Great Expectations, chap. xxv], and I should think there would be every chance of selling 250 or 300' (BL Add. MS. 52715, f. 11). In an undated letter, printed by Mackail (ii, 325-6), Morris replied: 'As to the Coleridge-Keats question, you don't quite understand the position I think. Keats was a great poet who sometimes nodded: we don't want to make a selection of his works. Coleridge was a muddle-brained metaphysician, who by some strange freak of fortune turned out a few real poems amongst the dreary flood of inanity which was his wont. It is these real poems only that must be selected, or we burden the world with another useless book. Christabel only just comes in because the detail is fine; but nothing a hair's breadth worse must be admitted. There is absolutely no difficulty in choosing, because the difference between his poetry and his drivel is so striking.

'I have been through the poems, and find that the only ones that have any interest for me are  1. Ancient Mariner, 2. Christabel, 3. Kubla Khan, 4. the poem called Love. This would make a very little book, about 6o pages. There is one other which at least has some character, though rather tainted with Wordsworthianism; it is called The Three Graves, and is about as cheerful as the influenza. But then it is crght;and at the bes it wouldrather watrdownthe ood s.

TYPE: Chaucer type.

I16) ['THE CHAMBER / OF LOVE / IN THE / WILDERNÉSS'], t7v (p. 286) ['FRIENDS / IN NEED MEET / IN THE / WILDWOOD'], and 2aSV (p. 362) ['THE LAST / TIME OF THE / LONG CHAMPION'].

[]-[] an [] ]-[a] []- tion b1-2i8r text 2i8r colophon 2i8 printer's mark 2.

BINDING: Limp vellum with silk ties. Stamped in gold on spine: THE WELL / AT THE / WORLD'S END / BY / WILLIÁM / MORRIS' [Golden type].

PUBLICATION: 350 paper copies (5 gns.); 8 vellum copies (20 gns.).   
Issued 4 June 1896.

NotEs: The early history of The Well at the World's End  which was longer in production than any other title printed at the kp - is recorded in Cockerell's diary. 21 April 1892:'Saw . . . some proofs of the Well at the World's End. is September 1892: 'Saw . . . trial pages of the Well at the World's End etc.' (One of these was set in a single column ['List'].) 17 October 1982: 'Morris busy on the 2nd of the four borders which he intends to have in "The Well at the World's End. ..  19 October 1892: . read proofs of the first 200 pages of the Well at the World's End  which is now at length approaching completion.'

Printing began on 16 December 1892 ('List'). The book was announced in the kP list of December 1892 (C3) 'with 4 woodcuts, designed by C. F. Murray'. In the spring of 1893 Morris decided to allow Arthur J. Gaskin to illustrate it (Cockerell diary, 23 April 1893). Writing to his daughter Jenny on 13 July 1893, Morris said, 'I found [here] 2 designs from Gaskin, small ones this time: I am sorry to say that I did not think they would quite do' (BL Add. MS. 4534o, f. 122). The drawings were revised, and in the end Gaskin contributed nineteen designs (see Gaskin, pp. 44-7), some of which were engraved by Hooper in October and November 1893 (HEH HM 36655). A number of pages , z, and aa below). On 20 February 1895, however, Morris sought the advice of Philip Webb, who agreed with Morris's unfavourable judgement of Gaskin's work; on 26 February Morris wrote to Gaskin declining to use his illustrations (Morris diary). Though Gaskin asked to be allowed to do four new illustrations (Morris diary, 27 February and March 18), Morris tured instead  Burne-Jones, whose degs were engraved as usual by Hooper (HEH HM 36655).

The text was t from the sheets of the Longmans edition, printed Chiwic ess, which was sutaneusy  producin e b below). According to Sparling, . the last sheet [of the  i-le, y

![](images/81023b008a4e8dd424a1921b1660b972340edc6b048b3a2e8c6ffd340cb71534.jpg)

A rejected wood-engraving (reduced) designed by Arthur J. Gaskin for The Well at the World's End (A39). The design is in a private collection; proofs of this and other illustrations designed by Gaskin for The Well are scattered about in a number of libraries. None of them was used in the published book, which was instead illustrated by four engravings designed by Edward Burne-Jones.

occied upon the Chaucer [A40] and a third upon the Earthly Paradise $\left[ { \tt A } _ { 4 } \mathrm { I } \right] ^ { \prime } \left( \mathrm { p } . \mathrm { \ 7 9 } \right)$

The" eight borders and the six different ornaments between the columns, appear here for the first time, but are used again in The Water of the Wondrous Isles [A45], with the exception of two borders' ("List).

Cost of production was 1383 14s. 4d. (Ledger).

"48 copies were bound in white pigskin at the Doves Bindery from a design by Morris. Of these, three were vellum copies; two are now in public collections (BL and Harvard), and the third is in a private collection in London. (Cockerell ['List'] mentions the existence of only two such copies.)

PUBLICAT1ON: 425 paper copies (£20); 13 vellum copies (120 gns.). Issued 26 June 1896.

NOTEs: Chaucer had been one of the great discoveries of Morris and Burne-Jones at Oxford in the 185os, and from the beginning Morris envisioned an edition of his works as one of the likely publications of the KP. Cockerell recorded in his diary on 11 June 1891 that Morris thinks of printing a Chaucer from a blackletter fount which he hopes to design'. In a proof (BL C.102.h.18) of the first list of the KP, May 1892, among the titles described as in preparation is 'THE POEMS OF GEOFFREY CHAUCER. Black Letter. Large 4to'. In the published version of the list (C1) this title was deleted, but in the list of December 1892 (C3) the Chaucer was finally announced: 'Chaucer's Works. With about 6o designs by E. Burne-Jones.' Thereafter the letters and diaries of the period are filled with references to the progress (or lack thereof) of the Chaucer, which was recognised by all concerned, even before its publication, as the most ambitious and remarkable of the KP books.

Before entering into a discussion of several specific aspects of its production, it may be useful to outline the chronology of the book. In January 1892, when the Troy type was delivered, Morris had two trial pages set, but it was immediately obvious that a smaller type was needed. Writing to Frances Jenkinson on 14 March 1892, Ellis commented: . . . I enclose you a specimen of the new type in which as you see Caxton's "Recuyel" is to be & Chaucer is to follow but in a size smaller type but same pattern  and in double cols. To print Chaucer in this size wd. be delightful but I fear buyers would turn resentful[?] at the size which the book would necessarily make' (cul Add. 6463, no. 2o18). By July 1892 another trial page, in the newly cut Chaucer type, was printed and was adopted as the basis for the book: two columns of fifty-eight lines each.'The idea of the Chaucer as it now exists, with illustrations by Sir Edward Burne-Jones, then took definite shape' ('List ).

r. Mo be  t bor Feb. 1,,

![](images/de7a9ee9c94cc29299f426bf1186561ffc28d84b5cc69fac26ab9fe2c5a46316.jpg)

![](images/b9d1bee1ba3e5bd5577ccf16601da1d1de63d056dd18097c675472325b2a4086.jpg)

AS OLDE StORIES tELlEN US, a duc that bigbte Theseus; Of A tthenes he was lord and governour, And in his tyme swich a conquerour, That gretter was ther noon under the sonne.

ful many a riche contree badde be wonne; That with his wysdom and his chivalrye He conquered al the regne of femenye, That whilom was ycleped Scithia; And weddede the queene Ypolita, And broghte hire hoom with bym in his contree With muchel glorie and greet solempnytee, And eek bir faire suster Emelye. And thus with victorie and with melodye Lete I this noble duc to Atthenes ryde, And al his hoost, in armes bym bisyde. And certes, if it nere to long to heere, I wolde have toold yow fully the manere, How wonnen was the regne of femenye By Theseus, and by his chivalrye; And of the grete bataille for the nones Bitwixen Attbenes and Amazones; And how asseged was Ypolita, The faire hardy queene of Scithia; And of the feste that was at hir weddynge, And of the tempest at bir boom comynge; But al that thyng I moot as now forbere. I have, God woot, a large feeld to ere, And wayke been the oren in my plough.

![](images/00acebdad8fbd24e0d40b4dfb02a1fe71cbba9fc8022b72158ae0f87f5718fa7.jpg)

18 The Works of Geoffrey Chaucer (A4o), p. 9 (reduced). The design for the illustration (by Edward Burne-Jones) is in the Fitzwilliam Museum. A proof of an earlier state of the wood-engraving is in the Clark Library.

but was dissatisfied with the design and did not finish it. Three days later he began the vine border for the first page, and finished it in about a week, together with the initial word"Whan," the two lines of heading, and the frame for the first picture, and W. H. Hooper engraved the whole of these on one block. The first picture was engraved at about the same time' ("List'). In an announcement of 14 November 1894 (C27) the number of illustrations was altered to 'upwards of 7o' and the number of copies to be printed was increased from 325 to 425  the latter decision being a result of Morris's growing financial anxiety about the book.

Printing of the Chaucer began on 8 August 1894 (Cockerell diary). At the beginning of the next year another press was set up in Sunderland Cottage, 21 Upper Mall, to hasten the progress of the book. Burne-Jones finished the illustrations in December 1895. On 10 December 1895 Morris began designing the special pigskin binding (Cockerell diary), and on 19 February 1896 he started working on the engraved title (Morris diary). The first two copies of the book were delivered to Morris and Burne-Jones on 2 June 1896. Douglas Cockerell, on 24 June, brought to Morris in Folkestone the first pigskinbound copy. 'Very satisfactory,' Morris noted in his diary.

Morris's first task in planning the book was to find a suitable text for the Chaucer. Ellis, just before travelling to Cambridge, wrote to Frances Jenkinson on 17 April 1892, 'I shal also take the opportunity of calling on Mr. Skeat if he is in Cambridge to hear what he says as to the text of the projected Kelmscott Press folio Chaucer' (cul Add. 6463, no. 2046). Walter W. Skeat, Professor of Anglo-Saxon at Cambridge University, was in fact at that moment preparing his monumental edition of Chaucer, which the Clarendon Press was to begin publishing in 1894, and it must have been immediately obvious to Ellis and Morris that this was the text they were searching for. But securing permission to reprint it from the Delegates of the Oxford University Press proved to be unusually difficult. When the Delegates first considered the request in November 1892, they gave a negative response (C. E. Doble to Skeat, 29 November 1892 [OUA: DUP/PUB/- 1/56]). Skeat suggested to Morris, as an alternative, that he might reprint the first edition of Chaucer (Doble to Skeat, 6 December 1892 [OUA: DUP/PUB/1/56]).

For the e be Mor  heatteriene, hou El continued t sudy Chaucer's text i reparation or the  editn. At a meeting of the Delegates on 26 May 1894 the request was renewed, wu efin eesL e

Revd. Prof. W. W. Skeat proposing that the Delegates should grant permission to Mr. Wm. Morris to reprint the Oxford text of "Chaucer" amongst his Kelmscott Editions on condition of a contribution being made by him to the proposed English Lectureship Funds at Cambridge. Resolved that the Delegates think it would be injurious to the Clarendon Press edition to accede to Mr. Morris' proposal.' On August 8 Morris sent  h Secretary  theClarendon Press a letter of clarification:

'May I ask you to be so kind as to lay before the Delegates the request to allow me to make use of Professor Skeat's Chaucer under the following circumstances. I am publishing a Chaucer, which is being printed at the Kelmscott Press, & will be ornamented with picture[s] designed by Sir Edward Burne-Jones, and borders &c by myself. This book I hope to make a specially beautiful one as to typography & decoration, and I naturally wish to make the text as good as possible. I ask therefore to be allowed to avail myself of the corrections of errors which Professor Skeat's learning & acumen are making public, though we (Mr. F. S. Ellis, the editor, & myself) by no means intend to produce a literal reprint of his text. We should be pleased to make full acknowledgment of our obligations to Professor Skeat in the book itself.

'I should add that it is impossible that the book could come into competition with the text now being published at the University Press, as only 325 copies will be issued, at a high price (£2o), & without notes or commentary. It is intended to be essentially a work of art' (OUA: DUP/PUB/II/23).

In the meantime, Morris had begun printing the Canterbury Tales, for which he used the text of the Ellesmere manuscript. The Delegates, at their meeting of 19 October 1894, at last gave Morris permission to reproduce 'a limited number of readings from Prof. Skeat's Text of “"Chaucer" . . . , provided due acknowledgment of the sanction of the Editor and the Delegates be made'. In fact, as the marked copy of Skeat's edition at Yale (see a below) shows, Morris adopted the Skeat text almost verbatim with only a few minor emendations. The initials and names of Morris's printers in this copytext also reveal that the following compositors, in descending order of frequency, set the bulk of the Chaucer (excluding the Canterbury Tales): F. Collins, T. Binning, J. Tippett, Barks, H. Howes, and L. Tasker.

Another major problem with which Morris had to wrestle while producing the Chaucer was his business relationship with Bernard Quaritch, who had published three of the early kP books (A7, A8, and A1o) and had hoped to publish the Chaucer as well. Morris was annoyed when Quaritch raised the price of The Golden Legend on the day of publication (see NoTEs for A7) and eventually decided to publish future kp books himself; hence their negotiations over the Chaucer were often tense and suspicious in tone. The first serious misunderstanding arose in August 1894 when Quaritch in effect undersold Morris by discounting at a higher rate to dealers. 'Since as I understand now, your circular has already been sent out, I am driven to offer the Chaucer to the trade at 2o per cent discount,' Morris wrote to Quaritch on 16 August 1894. Furthermore, Morris declined to supply him with 'any more copies, beyond the 63 already booked, at a higher discount than is per cent until all my trade customers have been supplied by me' (BQ). Quaritch grudgingly accepted these new terms, because he had no choice but to do so, but he complained to his son on 2o August, 'Mr. Wm Morris will call on me tomorrow; he is laying down the law to me about the Chaucer, - and I must submit.

'This has cooled my ardour to obtain the transfer of his publications from Reeves & Turner. Mr. Morris's success has turned him into a despot' (Bod. ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 194).

The following day Morris 'did not dare to show his face' but instead sent Cockerell, whom Quaritch found to be 'a very sharp fellow'. 'My friendship for Morris is at an end,' Quaritch lamented (Quaritch to B. Alfred Quaritch, 21 August 1894 [Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 195]). He agreed to buy sixty-three copies at a twenty-five per cent discount and fifty-three at a fifteen per cent discount. When Morris decided to print an additional hundred copies, Quaritch at once leaped forward with a proposal that he buy fifty of them  but at the original discount of twenty-five per cent (Quaritch to Morris, 20 November 1894 [HEH HM 36669]). 'Mr. W. Morris & I are on the road of being reconciled, he sees now how hastily he acted,' Quaritch wrote to his son on 2 November (Bod. Ms. Eng. lett. c. 435, f. 197).

The quarrel with Quaritch was, in short, patched over in the end. Another key figure in the history of the Chaucer, but one with whom Morris almost never quarreled, was Edward Burne-Jones, the illustrator. Burne-Jones worked on the Chaucer designs only on Sundays, when Morris came to talk as he drew: on one such occasion, Burne-Jones boasted, 'we never got to speak of anyone later than the ninth century' (Burne-Jones to Frances Horner, n.d. (FM[1]). The elaborate procedure used to translate his designs into wood-engravings, employing the skill of Robert Catterson-Smith, has been described earlier in this bibliography (see Introduction, above). To one of his correspondents Burne-Jones explained his pleasure in the book as it progressed: 'And I have been calculating that . . . the time I have an hour he ime I hould ave spent   o Saury il Mon day at "houses," if I had been amiable and sociable. . .  And yet I know quite well not ten people in the land will care twopence about it. Yet it will be a very nice book  but it should have come out when Morris and I were at Oxford in our first term, and we should have lost our senses with bliss. . . . I have fourteen more designs to make and then my share is done, and I may permit myself the luxury of one smile. I have always been happy over it: it has never tired me but refreshed me always' (Burne-Jones to Frances Horner, 9 August 1895 (FM[1]).

Burne-Jones's studio assistant, T. M. Rooke, records in his diary that on  March 1896 Morris, very ghostlike, feeble & old looking, appeared at Burne-Jones's door. Burne-Jones said that the Chaucer 'was the finest book ever printed  if w.m. had done nothing else it would be enough'. Morris replied: 'I must own I'm complacent about it  must try though not to be too conceited. And there's one thing that's not t beforgotten, that you backed me up wellin iold ap. If you'd been at all slack over it and hadn't been as much excited about it as I was, we should never have got through with it.'

'Besides the eighty-seven woodcut illustrations designed by Sir Edward Burne-Jones and engraved by W. H. Hooper, the Chaucer contains a woodcut title, fourteen large borders, eighteen different frames round the illustrations, and twenty-six initial words designed for the book by William Morris. Many of these were engraved by C. E. Keates, and others by W. H. Hooper and W. Spielmeyer' (" List').

An announcement issued on 26 February 1896 (C40) indicated that Morris would design four special bindings for the Chaucer: a full and a half pigskin executed at the Doves Bindery, and a full and a half pigskin executed by Leighton. Cockerell states that only the Doves full pigskin design was completed, and it of course was carried out on forty-eight copies; however, in Butler Library, Columbia University, there is adummy Chaucer in a full pigskin bindingexecuted by Leighton and evidently designed by Morris.

The cost of production was £7217 11d. (Ledger).

A discriminating and enthusiastic review in the Athenaeum (3 Octoe896, pp. 444 began This great eiton oChaucer is certainly the crowning achievement of the Kelmscott Press.'

NOTEs: Morris designed the first border in June 1895. 'I have been working hard at a border all day; but it has not come out right,' he wrote to Jenny on 18 June. . . . never mind about the border. You see I am trying something new & can't expect to succeed at first go off (BL Add. MS. 45340, f. 176).

Non  th  borr  Teart p months are also not used elsewhere'List.

The Earthly Paradise was frst published in 186870; Morris during the 186os laid plans, never realised, for an edition to be elaborately illustrated by Burne-Jones (see Joseph R. Dunlap, The Book That Never Was [New York, 1971] and A. R. Dufty's introduction to Morris's The Story ofCupid and Psyche [1974]. A single-volume, revised edition was issued by Reeves & Turner in 189o. For a discussion of the alterations in the  edition, ee Inructins, i, 15.

This was the first kp book in which the Apple paper was used. 'The sizeaccording to theist  June 1896 ,wll be a new e, n termediate between the large 4to and the 8vo books hitherto issued.' To an interviewer in 1895 Morris commented: 'I am rather exercised as to its format but Cockerell is infavour of this new size  a sort o mild quarto, and yet looking like an octavo (Ideal Book, p. 11).

Cost of production was 855 18s. 7d. (Ledger).

## A42 Laudes Beatæ Mariæ Virginis

## LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS.

coLopHoN: These poems are taken from a Psalter written by an / English scribe, most likely in one of the Midland coun-/ties, early in the 13th century. / [paragraph] Printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press, / Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex, and finished on the 7th day of July, 1896. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

INSERTED NOTE (14S × 210 mm, Chaucer type; 'Specimens', nO. 128:

o Perch'; distributed to subscribers after the book's publication and in most copies of the book): LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS. / The Reverend E. . Dewick has pointed out that these poems / were printed in 1579, in a 16mo volume, with the title Psalterium / Diva Virginis Maria, rhythmice conscriptum a Reverendissimo / domino Stephano, olim Archiepiscopo Cantuariensi, ex Biblio-/theca Tegrinseensi depromptum, Cantandum sub melodia, Pa-/tris sapientia. Cum gratia & privilegio Cæsareo. Excusum Te-/gernsee. Anno MDLXXIX. This Tegernsee edition contains a / Conclusio of four verses in the same metre as the Aves, but the / text ioherwise inferior  that printed by William Mors. The tion of the authorship to Stephen Langton is doubly in-/teresting, as the manuscript transcribed r e Kelscott r was probably written before his death in 1288. / Dec. 28th, 1896.

NOTEs: Morris purchased an early thirteenth-century English psalter, which he called the Nottingham Psalter, on 25 May 1893 for 115 (WMA B, p. 35). One section of the Psalter, a Laudes beatae Mariae virginis, which Morris printed, was attributed either to Stephen Langton John eckham, Archbishop  Canterbury. Other anuscipts o the Laudes exist. In the Nottingham Psalter version, the opening words of each psalm are in red, followed by a quatrain which applies them to the Virgin. (For a description of the Psalter, which is now in the Moran Lbr hMonae,

T (see , c, and d below) reveal more than usual variation: two, for example, are set in the Golden and Chaucer types, and several are in black and red only.

Cost of production was 86 11s. 11d. (Ledger).

## A45 William Morris, The Water of the Wondrous Isles

: Mors 'began [his] new ranc in verse'n  Fry 1895, but on 8 February 'after seeing B[urne]-J[ones] w.M. decided to write the new romance in prose & verse, & began to rewrite it' Cockerell diary). Eventually Morris wrote it in prose alone.The verse fragment is in BL Add. mss. 453223, 45325.)

'It wsf    e prs in he  Jue 1, 18 were not printed until about a month later' ("List').

In Cockerel's diary we catch glimpses of the dying Morris attempting in vain to complete the design of the initial word 'Empty'. 4 September 1896: 'He sat up & was going to design "Empty" for the 3rd part of The Water of the Wondrous Isles  but only just managed to black in the spaces, & just rough in the word.' 6 September 1896: 'w.M. rather tired, but decidedly no worse. He had done a little more to Empty".' But, as the colophon indicates, Catterson-Smith had to finish the design.

May Morris believed that her father had seen only the first two parts of the book through proofs (Introductions, ii, 575), though Cockerell was convinced that Morris had read more proofs than that (Cockerell to May Morris, 14 September 1912 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 12]). May helped in proofing the rest of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 19 January 1913 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 14]).

Cost of production was 158 10s. 3d. (Ledger).

A less expensive edition was published by Longmans in October 1897.

## A46 Froissart's Chronicles (specimen pages)

No title-page.

CoLopHoN: [ornament I] These two trial pages of the projected / edition of Lord Berners' translation of / Froissart were printed at the Kelmscott / Press in September, 1897, to preserve the / designs made for the work by William Morris [ornament 1] In the border are the arms of / France, the Empire, and England; on the / second page are those of Reginald Lord / Cobham, Sir Walter Manny, and Sir John / Chandos.

COLLATION: 2°: [a]². 2 leaves, pp. [].

veLLUM & DImensions: Vellum. Leaf: 424 × 283 mm. Text: two colun  m  .

TText inhau type;titf rologue n roype.

ORNAMENTs: Border 32 (which includes a twenty-one-line initial shoulder notes in red.

COTeTs: [a] colophon [a]1v-[a] text [a]2 blank.

BINDiNG: Not bound.

PUICATIO

NTE: In 1895 Morris described his projected Froissart in these words Y t extha e. It's fine old English and would take a lot of beating. The edition of the text I am using is the two volumes quarto published by Rivington l  y Cobden-Sanderson. . .no book that I could do would give me half p t. . I press delights me more than I can say. I am taking great pains with it, and doing al I can to realise what I have long wished Ideal Book, pp. 11112).

Berners'translation f Froissart (Morris's copy is ow at ) had b type was delivered from the foundry, he had a single-column trial page set up in January 1892 (see b below); early in 1893 double-column trial pages in the Chaucer type were produced ("List'). In March of that year Morris was showing initials designed for the Froissart to his friends (Cockerell diary, 1 March 1893). On 8 October 1893 Cockerell noted in his diary: 'E[dward] B[urne]-J[ones] had that morning offere  o a bi.George  sme hiel  rs or h Fros, which will put the book on the shelf for a while. Jason to be printed instead.' The subject of the frontispiece of volume i was to be St. George, that of volume ii Fame; it was understood that Emery Walker r be the editor (Sparling, p. 111).

Though it was several times announced as in the press, the Froissart continued to be held in abeyance while work on the Chaucer progressed. It was definitely abandoned only after Morris's death. In November 1896 'about thirty-four pages were in type, but no sheet had been printed' ("'List'). During the following month thirty-two copies of sixteen of those pages (see D1.8) were privately distributed to friends of Morris. 'This suggested the idea of printing two pages for wider distribution. The half-border had been engraved in April, 1894, by W. Spielmeyer, but the large border only existed as a drawing. It was engraved with great skill and spirit by C. E. Keates, and the two pages were printed by Stephen Mowlem, with the help of an apprentice, in a manner worthy of the designs' ('List').

According to a note by Cockerell, 'only about a half-dozen (perhaps not so many) copies' were printed with the colophon on the first rather than the last page, as I came to the conclusion it was a mistake' (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], n0. 202). This statement seems to be one of Cockerell's rare inaccuracies, because on all the copies I have examined the colophon appears on the first page.

A facsimile of these specimen pages was produced by Frederick Goudy, the American type-designer, in 1918.

When Swinburne received his copy of the sixteen pages, he wrote to Cockerell, 20 January 1897, 'A thousand thanks for your beautiful & precious gift. Why did not Morris take up & finish Froissart earlier  instead of the less appropriate work (Shelley, for one instance) on which the Kelmscott Press was occasionally wasted' (pML MA 3000 R-v).

Cost of production, including all expenses incurred for the abanonedFsart, a  .
