NOTEs: The text is based upon the Camden Society's Thornton Romances (see NoTEs for A33), in which James Halliwell wrote that 'Sir Degrevant . . . is, perhaps, more curious and valuable than the [other romances in the volume]. The descriptive notices of early costume and architecture, areof peculir interest; and it would perhaps be dif ficult to select a romance of the kind of more merit' (p. xxii). The story had long been a favourite in Morris's circle: in the summer of 186o Burne-Jones did a wall-painting of The Wedding Procession of Sire Degrevaunt in the drawing room of Red House, Morris's home.

The text follows closely the Halliwell version except that brackets around conjectural readings are omitted. A more recent editor of the romance has described the kP edition as "a pleasant book to read, but - vant [Early English Text Society, 1949], p. xv).

'The frontispiece was not printed until October, 1897, eighteen months after the text was finished' ('List'). On 18 October 1897 Cockerell wrote to Leighton, 'Please send for the sheets of Syr Degrevaunt & the blue papers for the covers, on Friday. You will then be able to go ahead & to issue Syr Isambras [A48] & Syr Degrevaunt together at the end of the month' (LB). A flimsy was inserted by Leighton between the fronispiece and the first page of the text, the only instance in which this was done in any kP book.

Cost of production was 48 3s. 7d. (Ledger).

## A49 Sydney C. Cockerell (ed.), Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century

## SOME GERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH / CENTURY

COLopHoN: [[e]6r] Here ends Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century, for / which the blocks (with one exception) were prepared by Walker / and Boutall under the direction of the late William Morris. Now / edited by S. C. Cockerell, and printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith. Finished on the 15th day of December, 1897. / [e]6v] [printer's mark 2] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

FOREwoRD: Of the 35 reproductions of German woodcuts here given, 29 are all / that were done of a series chosen by William Morris to appear in / the Catalogue of his library, which was to have been annotated by / him and printed at the Kelmscott Press. The other 6 were made or /his article in the h umber o Bibloraphica on thery Wood-/cut Books of Ulm & Augsburg, and thanks are due to Messrs. Ke-/gan Paul, Trench, Trübner & Co. for the loan of the blocks, as well / as for permission to quote the portions of the article which serve as / an introduction to this book. These quotations will show the nature / of the remarks that Mr. Morris intended to write for the Catalogue, / of which they would have been the most interesting feature. A minor / feature was to have been a statement of the number of different cuts, / and the number of cuts including repetitions, in the principal illus-/trated books. For this purely scientific side of the work I was made / responsible, and as the early printers usually repeated their cuts, and / no one has hitherto troubled to count them in this way, it has seemed / worth while to preserve these few statistics now that the original de-/sign of the Catalogue has fallen through. They are accordingly print-/ed as an appendix in a list of the more important woodcut books that / the library contained. For some particulars in this section I am / indebted to my friend Mr. Robert Proctor, who has kindly looked / over the proofsheets, and pointed out errors and omissions. / [paragraph] I have been tempted to add a list of the manuscripts, which, though / comparatively few in number (112) were the most notable part of the / library; but without a full description of each volume such a list would / be meaningless, and anything more than a list would be out of place. / S. C. COCKERELL.

6 January 1898.

e   oe Ccl diary nd  xt yar   hut alogued Morris' extensive collection of incunabula and mediaeval manuscripts. Out of this activity grew Morris's scheme of a heavily illustrated catalogue of his library, with descriptions by Cockerell and additional notes by himself. To an interviewer in 1895, Morris said, o te ook theelves.O course, thi is justa litt fancy r my own pleasure, and yet I have an idea that there ought to be many book-lovers and art-lovers who would find much in the volume to delight them. . . You see that I am sparing no trouble to give the fnest reproductions' (Ideal Book, p. 112).

Tw tial pag  the projec catague ave urvived (C.43. f.24 [∆b2] andC.102.h.1. The first is a descitio  a psalter N.E. France, c. 1260, on a leaf of Flower paper (281 × 204 mm), with a text of 33 lines × 127 mm. The other is a corrected proof of a description of Speculum Humanae Salvationis (1473), with a text of 35 lines × 132 mm. A number of manuscript notes by Morris for the catalogue  which he began on 21 May 1894 (Cockerell diary) - are extant, most plentifully in the Morgan Library.

Morris planned to charge about five guineas for the catalogue (Cockerell to Macmillan & Bowes, 24 December 1895 [LB]). After his death the book had to be abandoned (but not before 170 1s. 4d. had been expended on it [Ledger]), though for a time there was talk of printing it elsewhere (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 (BL Add. MS 52743, f. 238]). Instead Cockerell decided to combine the illustrations of woodcuts that had already been prepared (all but one by Walker & Boutall) with portions of an article by Morris, 'On the Artistic Qualities of the Woodcut Books of Ulm and Augsburg in the Fifteenth Century', Bibliographica, 1 (1895), 43755.

The book sold briskly before publication. 'There has been a great rush on the German woodcuts & they were all sold out last week,' Cockerell wrote to Leighton on 30 November 1897. '. . . A good many people have been left out in the cold, as orders keep coming in! I suppose it is the announcement that the Press is closing that has made people so eager' (Jc).

In another letter to Leighton, 14 December 1897, Cockerell said, 'At the last moment I have discovered a tiresome misprint in the colophon of 'Some German woodcuts' - which necessitates a cancel. This will be finished tomorrow & can be fetchedon Friday' (c).

## AWilliam Morris, The Story of Sigurd the Volsung

THE STORY OF SIGURD THE VOLSUNG AND THE / FALL OF THE NIBLUNGS [ornament I] BY WILLIAM MOR-RIS [Troy type]

CoLopHoN: Here ends The Story of Sigurd the Volsung and the Fall of the Niblungs, written/ by William Morrs. With two pictures desined by Edward Burne-Jones and / engraved by W. H. Hooper. It was printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith, and fe 9 y anary a Sold e Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

T mal °: 0[p]10eave pp.[8] []2207 10] [2] [=212].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 328× 231 mm. Txt: 48 lines × 169 mm.

ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATIONS: Borders 33a and 33; side and corneR shoulder titles in red; wood-engraved frontispiece and illustration on [p]1r (p. [209]) designed by Burne-Jones.

x ] ] [p]1r-[p]2v blank.

SIGURD' nlagd Golden pe.

PICT6 s.ec .   
Issued 2 February 1898.

NOTEs: Sigurd was first published in 1877. The earliest references to the KP edition are, as usual, in Cockerell's diary.  November 1891: Saw . .a trial page f Sigurd. October 89:Sigurd to lustrted (perhaps) by B.urne.[ones] but canot be thought oftill Chu  h ..ul posed new folio Sigurd with B-J illustrations.'

However, only with great reluctance did Burne-Jones agree to illustrate the book. 'To pacify and please Morris I will do what I can to adorn the Volsung Tale,' he wrote to Lady Leighton in late 1895 (Memorials, ii, 276). 'I shan't like doing it so much as Chaucer,' Burne-Jones commented to his studio assistant on 7 January 1896. 'The scenery for one thing isn't quite a painter's scenery - it's more a poet's' (Rooke diary). On 5 May 1896 he complained, 'It's quite wonderful how distasteful the task of making drawings of it is to me. . . . Terrible the tragedy of it is, and the fine things in it are so much fitter for literature than for my art, and I've no turn for the dramatic' (Rooke diary).

As a result Burne-Jones made slow progress on the illustrations (which were to number about twenty-five), and in any case Morris's death meant the abandonment of the plans for a fully illustrated folio. Thirty-two copies of two folio pages (see D1.9) were privately distributed to friends of Morris in January 1897. On the evening of 26 January 1897 Cockerell persuaded Burne-Jones to supply two illustrations for a more modest edition (Cockerell diary). Catterson-Smith redrew the designs (Cockerell diary, 6 October 1897).

'The two borders used in this book were almost the last that Mr. Morris designed. They were intended for an edition of The Hill of Venus, which was to have been written in prose by him and illustrated by Sir E. Burne-Jones. The foliage was suggested by the ornament in two Psalters of the last half of the thirteenth century in the library at Kelmscott House. The initial A at the beginning of the 3rd book was designed in March, 1893, for the Froissart [A40], and does not appear elsewhere' ('List').

(b) Four preliminary drawings by Burne-Jones probably ntended for Sigurd v & A[] 1.11).Original designs for ive] he ar fo Sur  VoluA n B pear for the only time on these proofs. The lower three were never engraved, as Morris's death on 3 Oct 1896 put an end to the project' (Cockerell's note) (BL C.102.l.22). (d) Trial pages (BL C.102.I.22). (e) Trial pages in Golden type (BC). (f) Trial pages and inc. proofs (Cockerell sale, no. 78). (g) Marked and touched proof of second illustration (v & A[2] EW 138). ## A51 William Morris, The Sundering Flood

## THE SUNDERING FLOOD WRITTEN BY / WILLIAM MORRIS [Chaucer type]

COLoPHoN: [ornament 1] Here ends the story of the Sundering / Flood, the last romance written by William / Morris [ornament 2] It was overseen by May Morris, / and printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith. Finished on the I5th / day of November, 1897. / [printer's mark 1] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William / Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

CLAtiON: 80: [a]4 b-2i82k6.258 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2-507 [508].

PAPR & DIMENSIONS: Flower(2) paper. Leaf: 140205 mm. Text: 27 lines × 80 mm.

TYPE: Chaucer type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 1o; half and three-quarter borders; three-, six-, an twelvene initils;chapter tites ndshoulder notes in red;ne block map (on front pastedown) drawn by H. Cribb.

] ] ] k colophon.

BINDING: Quarter holland (blue paper on boards). Printed in black on paper label on spine: 'THE / SUNDERING / FLOOD / BY / WILLIAM / MORRIS' [Golden typel; printed in black on front coVer: 'THE SUNDERING FLOOD. WRITTEN / BY WIL-LIAM MORRIS' Ch t.

:300 pape cp (s;velp( Issued 25 February 1898.

N: 'This was the last romance by William Morris. He began to on Sept. 8, 1896' ('List).

A trial page was printed on 19 May 1897 (Cockerell diary).

In a letter to N. L. McMinn, 13 February 1928 (kM), Emery Walker explained how his firm, Walker & Boutalí, produced the map on the front pastedown of The Sundering Flood and similar maps in the volumes of the Saga Library: either Morris or Magnússon, he said, would submit a rough sketch that included the names of places. An employee of Walker & Boutall  in this case H. Cribb  then drew a finished design, and a line block was made from it. The original plan was to place the map in the book proper, as Cockerell reported to Jenny Morris, 28 February 1898: .". . one or two find fault with the map being pasted inside the cover of the Sundering Flood. I knew this would provoke complaints, but it was done after much consideration, as the map was found not to harmonise in style, scale, or colour with the first page of the book, opposite to which it had been intended to put it' (BL Add. MS. S2739, f. 52).

Sheet b was reprinted because a border used on it was found to be unsatisfactory (see c below).

For a discusssion of the emendations made by May Morris in the KP text, see Introductions, ii, 6212.

Ale expensive editon of The Sundering Flood was published by Longmans in February 1898.

UNFINISHED BOOKS

This category includes books that were planned and announced but eventually brted and, i ther cass, books that were mentin by Morris but never really begun. The books that were more seriously contemplated by Morris are placed first in the list.

## B1 Froissart's Chronicles

See NoTes for A46 and D1.8.

## B2 Shakespeare's Plays

On the fyleaf of a copy of The Heimskringla (1893) is the following note in the hand of Frederick J. Furnivall, founder of the New Shakspere Society: 'F. J. Furnivall, from Wm. Morris, at his Kelmscott House, Upr. Mall, Hammersmith, on Sunday evg., 26 Feb. 1893, when he assented (provisionally) to F.J.F.'s proposal that he should print a grand Shakspere in 3 vols. folio, edited by P. A. Daniel, or F.J.F., after his Chaucer (ed. F. S. Ellis) was done' (JDC). On 23 April 1893 Cockerell recorded in his diary, "wM is to print a big Shakespeare for Longman.' However, in a letter to Frank E. Bliss, 23 July 1894, Cockerell observed that 'the Shakespeare will not be ready for some time' (LB).

In the British Library (C.102.1.22) there is a trial page on Perch paper of two columns of Golden type; shoulder titles, speakers' names, and stage directions are in red. Each column is 61 lines × 104 mm. The passage printed is Macbeth, Act I, Scene viii. (Two copies of a slightly different trial page are known to be extant: one is in the Berger collection and one appeared in the Saks sale, no. 146. A trial page similar to the one in the Berger collection is reproduced as the frontispiece in Books and Letters Collected bγ William Harris Arnold of New ork [Jamaica, N.Y., 1901].)

By 1894 it was decided to issue the Shakespeare in eight or ten 80 volumes at about 25/- or 3o/- per volume. On 5 February 1895 Cockerell wrote to F. E. Thompson: 'The Shakespeare is not yet in the press, as the Chaucer is absorbing all Mr. Morris' attention. It (the Sh.) will be a size between the Beowulf & the 8vo books unless present plans are altered' (LB).

It is not surprising that Morris never found time to complete the Shakespeare. He once admitted to Cockerell, 'Shakespeare did not much attract me, as I have not much sympathy with the dramatic form' (Introductions, ii, 7).

## B3 Vitas Patrum

F. S. Ellis, the editor of this projected volume, wrote in an announcement (C15) issued in about February 1894: 'This work, written by St. Jerome in the fourth century, was translated into English by William Caxton during the last years of his life, and is stated by his servant and successor to have been finished on the day of his death.

'It was printed at Wynkyn de Worde's press in 149s. . . .

'Wynken de Worde's edition, which has never been reprinted, has become so rare that a fine perfect copy of the book has been sold for as much as two hundred and fifty pounds, and can rarely be met with even at that price. It is a book of very great interest alike to the philologist, the hagiologist, and the student of folk-lore.

'It is proposed to proceed with the printing of a new edition uniform with that of Caxton's Golden Legend, issued from the Kelmscott Press in 1892, as soon as the name of one hundred and fifty subscribers have been received. In no case will more than two hundred copies be printed. It will make two large 4to volumes, and the price will be five guineas.'

Cockerell wrote to Charles F. Carter on 7 August 1895: 'Mr. Morris has abandoned the idea of printing "Vitas Patrum", as the number of subscriptions did not justify his going on with the book' (LB).

For a specimen page, see DI.S.

## B4 Theodore Watts, The Coming of Love

In a preface to the first and second editions of The Coming of Love (1898), Theodore Watts (afterwards Watts-Dunton) wrote: 'Had it not been for the intervention of matters of a peculiarly absorbing kind matters which caused me to delay the task of collecting these verses I should have been the most favoured man who ever brought out a volume of poems, for they would have been printed by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.' Parts of The Coming of Love had appeared in the Athenaeum and other periodicals, but some of the poems had been lent and lost; hence another reason for the delay in publication.

## A42 Laudes Beatæ Mariæ Virginis

## LAUDES BEATAE MARIAE VIRGINIS.

coLopHoN: These poems are taken from a Psalter written by an / English scribe, most likely in one of the Midland coun-/ties, early in the 13th century. / [paragraph] Printed by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press, / Upper Mall, Hammersmith, in the County of Middle-/sex, and finished on the 7th day of July, 1896. / [printer's mark 2] / Sold by William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

which he called the Nottingham Psalter, on 25 May 1893 for 115 (WMAB, p. 35). One section of the Psalter, a Laudes beatae Mariae virginis, which Morris printed, was attributed either to Stephen Langton or to John Peckham, Archbishop of Canterbury. Other manuscripts of the Laudes exist. In the Nottingham Psalter version, the opening words of each psalm are in red, followed by a quatrain which applies them to the Virgin. In Cockerell's diary we catch glimpses of the dying Morris attempting in vain to complete the design of the initial word 'Empty'. 4 September 1896: 'He sat up & was going to design "Empty" for the 3rd part of The Water of the Wondrous Isles  but only just managed to black in the spaces, & just rough in the word.' 6 September 1896: 'w.M. rather tired, but decidedly no worse. He had done a little more to Empty".' But, as the colophon indicates, Catterson-Smith had to finish the design.

May Morris believed that her father had seen only the first two parts of the book through proofs (Introductions, ii, 575), though Cockerell was convinced that Morris had read more proofs than that (Cockerell to May Morris, 14 September 1912 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 12]). May helped in proofing the rest of the book (Cockerell to May Morris, 19 January 1913 [BL Add. MS. 52740, f. 14]).

A less expensive edition was published by Longmans in October 1897.

## A46 Froissart's Chronicles (specimen pages)

No title-page.

CoLopHoN: [ornament 1] These two trial pages of the projected / edition of Lord Berners' translation of / Froissart were printed at the Kelmscott / Press in September, 1897, to preserve the / designs made for the work by William Morris [ornament 1] In the border are the arms of / France, the Empire, and England; on the / second page are those of Reginald Lord / Cobham, Sir Walter Manny, and Sir John / Chandos.

COLLATION: 20: [a]². 2 leaves, pp. [4].

veLLum & Dimensions: Vellum. Leaf: 424 × 283 mm. Text: two columns of 63 lines × 87 mm ([a]1v) or 94 mm ([a]2) each.

TYPE:Text in Chaucer type; title of Prologue in Troy type.

ORNAMENTs: Border 32 (which includes a twenty-one-line initial wohal bor;tre e-;ar d shoulder notes in red.

ConTenTs: [a]1 colophon [a]1v-[a]2r text [a]2v blank.

BINDING: Not bound.

PBLICArIo: o paper copies; 16 vellu cpies ( gn.). Issud October 1897.

NOTEs: In 1895 Morris described his projected Froissart in these words to an interviewer: 'You cannot have a better text than old Berners's. It's fine old English and would take a lot of beating. The edition of the text I am using is the two volumes quarto published by Rivington and others in 1812. My reprint is a full folio and will take up two volumes. I also intend to publish it in four parts. Those who may like a strong binding will be able to get one from me for both the haucer [] as well as he Froiart. It will be in white piskin  a euul l Cobden-Sanderson. . . . no book that I could do would give me half I'lk pres delights me more than I can say. I am taking reat pains w t, and doing all I can to realise what I have long wished (Ideal Book, pp. 111-12).

Berners' translation of Froissart (Morris's copy is now at km) had bee v' ei collys    Try type was delivered from the foundry, he had a single-column trial page set up in January 1892 (see b below); early in 1893 double-column trial pages in the Chaucer type were produced ("List'). In March of that year Morris was showing initials designed for the Froissart to his friends (Cockerell diary, 1 March 1893). On 8 October 1893 Cockerell noted in his diary:'E[dward] B[urne]-J[ones] had that morning offered to do a big St. George & some shields of arms for the Froissart, which will put the book on the shelf for a while. Jason to be printed instead.' The subject of the frontispiece of volume i was to be St. George, that of volume ii Fame; it was understood that Emery Walker be the editor (Sparling, p. 111).

Though it was several times announced as in the press, the Froissart continued to be held in abeyance while work on the Chaucer progressed. It was definitely abandoned only after Morris's death. In November 1896 'about thirty-four pages were in type, but no sheet had been printed' ('List'). During the following month thirty-two copies of sixteen of those pages (see D1.8) were privately distributed to friends of Morris. 'This suggested the idea of printing two pages for wider distribution. The half-border had been engraved in April, 1894, by W. Spielmeyer, but the large border only existed as a drawing. It was engraved with great skill and spirit by C. E. Keates, and the two pages were printed by Stephen Mowlem, with the help of an apprentice, in a manner worthy of the designs' ('List ).

According to a note by Cockerell, 'only about a half-dozen (perhaps not so many) copies' were printed with the colophon on the first rather than the last page, 'as I came to the conclusion it was a mistake' (Davis & Harvey cat. 892 [27 March 1903], n0. 202). This statement seems to be one of Cockerell's rare inaccuracies, because on all the copies I have examined the colophon appears on the first page.

A facsimile of these specimen pages was produced by Frederick Goudy, the American type-designer, in 1918.

When Swinburne received his copy of the sixteen pages, he wrote to Cockerell, 20 January 1897, 'A thousand thanks for your beautiful & precious gift. Why did not Morris take up & finish Froissart earlier - instead of the less appropriate work (Shelley, for one instance) on which the Kelmscott Press was occasionally wasted' (pML MA 3000 R-v).

Cost óf production, including all expenses incurred for the abandoned Froissart, was £108 5s. 7d. (Ledger).

RELATED MATERIALS: (a) Designs for eight initials (cL 'red portfolio'). (b) Trial pages, some with preliminary drawings of coats of arms, and inc. proofs (Bc). (c) Trial pages, designs for ornaments and initials, and a ´'list in Morris's autograph of the Coats-of-arms that would be needed, with notes by Sir Emery Walker' (Cockerell sale, no. 76). (d) Inc. proofs (su). (e) Inc. corrected proofs (Saks sale, no. 174). () Philip Webb's copy (rc). (g) Copy with Morris's bookplate and note by Cockerell: 'Sold to Henry Gamman, August 1897' (Perry collection).

CONTENTS: $ { \left[ { \mathrm { a } } \right] }  { \mathrm { I } } ^ { \mathrm { r } } -  { \left[ { \mathrm { a } } \right] } 2 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ blank ${ \big [ } { \mathfrak { a } } { \big ] } _ { 3 } \operatorname { r }$ title ${ \big [ } { \mathfrak { a } } { \big ] } _ { 3 } \mathrm { v } _ { - } { \big [ } { \mathfrak { a } } { \big ] } _ { 4 } \mathrm { r }$ blank $[ { \mathfrak { a } } ] 4 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ frontispiece b1-g1 text g1V colophon $\mathrm { g } 2 ^ { \mathrm { r } } { - } \mathrm { g } 4 ^ { \mathrm { v } }$ bblank.

NoTEs: The text is based upon the Camden Society's Thornton Romances (see NoTEs for A33), in which James Halliwell wrote that 'Sir Degrevant . . . is, perhaps, more curious and valuable than the [other romances in the volume]. The descriptive notices of early costume and architecture, areof peculiar interest; and it would perhaps be di ficult to select a romance of the kind of more merit' (p. xxii). The story had long been a favourite in Morris's circle: in the summer of 186o Burne-Jones did a wall-painting of The Wedding Procession of Sire Degrevaunt in the drawing room of Red House, Morris's home.

The text follows closely the Halliwell version except that brackets around conjectural readings are omitted. A more recent editor of the romance has described the kP edition as 'a pleasant book to read, but extluas TheRDvant [Early English Text Society, 1949], p. xv).

'The frontispiece was not printed until October, 1897, eighteen months after the text was finished' ('List'). On 18 October 1897 Cockerell wrote to Leighton, 'Please send for the sheets of Syr Degrevaunt & the blue papers for the covers, on Friday. You will then be able to go ahead & to issue Syr Isambras [A48] & Syr Degrevaunt together at the end of the month' (LB). A flimsy was inserted by Leighton between the frontispiece and the first page of the text, the only instance in which this was done in any kP book.

Cost of production was 48 3s. 7d. (Ledger).

## A49 Sydney C. Cockerell (ed.), Some German Woodcuts of the Fiteenth Cenury

## SOME GERMAN WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH CENTURY

CoLopHon: [[e]6r] Here ends Some German Woodcuts of the Fifteenth Century, for / which the blocks (with one exception) were prepared by Walker / and Boutall under the direction of the late William Morris. Now / edited by S. C. Cockerell, and printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper / Mall, Hammersmith. Finished on the 15th day of December, 1897. / [e]6] [printer's mark 2] / Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

FOREwoRD: Of the 35 reproductions of German woodcuts here given, 29 are all / that were done of a series chosen by William Morris to appear in / the Catalogue of his library, which was to have been annotated by / him and printed at the Kelmscott Press. The other 6 were made or/his article in theth number of Biblioraphica on the arly Wood-/cut Books of Ulm & Augsburg, and thanks are due to Messrs. Ke-/gan Paul, Trench, Trübner & Co. for the loan of the blocks, as well / as for permission to quote the portions of the article which serve as / an introduction to this book. These quotations will show the nature / of the remarks that Mr. Morris intended to write for the Catalogue, / of which they would have been the most interesting feature. A minor / feature was to have been a statement of the number of different cuts, / and the number of cuts including repetitions, in the principal illus-/trated books. For this purely scientific side of the work I was made / responsible, and as the early printers usually repeated their cuts, and / no one has hitherto troubled to count them in this way, it has seemed / worth while to preserve these few statistics now that the original de-/sign of the Catalogue has fallen through. They are accordingly print-/ed as an appendix in a list of the more important woodcut books that / the library contained. For some particulars in this section I am / indebted to my friend Mr. Robert Proctor, who has kindly looked / over the proofsheets, and pointed out errors and omissions. / [paragraph] I have been tempted to add a list of the manuscripts, which, though / comparatively few in number (112) were the most notable part of the / library; but without a full description of each volume such a list would / be meaningless, and anything more than a list would be out of place. / S. C. COCKERELL.

COLLATION: Large $4 ^ { 0 } \colon { \mathsf { a } } ^ { 4 } \ \left( { \mathsf { a } } { 4 } + { \pi } { 2 } \right) \ [ { \mathsf { b } } { - } { \mathsf { d } } ] ^ { { \nu } } \ [ { \mathsf { e } } ] ^ { { 6 } } .$ 36 leaves, pp. [i-ii] iiixi [xii] 1-23 (rectos not numbered) [23] 24-36 [37] [=60].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Perch paper. Leaf: 290 × 213 mm. Text: 42 lines × 127 mm.

TYPE: Golden type.

ORNAMENTS & ILLUSTRATIONS: Six-line initial; titles, marginal figure references, first paragraph of colophon, and some words in red; 35 line-block illustrations.

conTenTs: π1r title π1v blank a1 foreword (by Cockerell) a1v blank a2r-a4r introduction (by Morris) a4V-π2r A LIST OF THE WOODCUTS OF THE FIFTEENTH / CENTURY REPRO-DUCED IN THIS BOOK.' π2 blank [b]r-[d]7r illustrations [d]- 7 blank [d]8r-[e]5r '[ornament 3] A LIST OF THE PRINCIPAL BOOKS OF THE / FIFTEENTH CENTURY, CONTAIN-ING WOOD-/CUTS, IN THE LIBRARY OF THE LATE WILLIAM / MORRIS, ARRANGED ALPHABETICALLY ACCORD-/ING TO TOWNS, WITH THE NUMBER OF CUTS / IN EACH, AND REFERENCES TO HAIN'S RE-PER-/TORIUM BIBLIOGRAPHICUM.' PUBLICATIoN: 225 paper copies (30s.); 8 vellum copies (5 gns.). Issued 6 January 1898.

N: Morris asked Cockerell 'to help him to catalogue his library' on the evening of 19 October 1892 (Cockerell diary), and for the next few years Cockerell, in addition to his other duties, studied and catalogued Morris's extensive collection of incunabula and mediaeval manuscripts. Out of this activity grew Morris's scheme of a heavily illusrated cataloguef his lbrary, with desciptions byCockerell nd additional notes by himself. To an interviewer in 1895, Morris said, o te book theselves.O course, this is just  litt fncy r y own pleasure, and yet I have an idea that there ought to be many book-lovers and art-lovers who would find much in the volume to delight them. . . You see that I am sparing no trouble to give thefinest reproductions' (Ideal Book, p. 112).

Tial  e je aehav uvi C.. f.24 [∆b12] and C.102.h.18). The first is a decription of a psalter, N.E. France, c. 1260, on a leaf of Flower paper (281 × 204 mm), with a text of 33 lines × 127 mm. The other is a corrected proof of a description of Speculum Humanae Salvationis (1473), with a text of 35 lines × 132 mm. A number of manuscript notes by Morris for the catalogue  which he beganon 1 May 1894 (Cockerell diary are extant, most plentiully in the Morgan Library.

Morris planned to charge about five guineas for the catalogue (Cockerell to Macmillan & Bowes, 24 December 1895 [LB]). After his death the book had to be abandoned (but not before 170 1s. 4d. had been expended on it [Ledger]), though for a time there was talk of printing it elsewhere (Cockerell to Robert Proctor, 9 November 1896 (BL Add. MS 52743, f. 238]). Instead Cockerell decided to combine the illustrations of woodcuts that had already been prepared (all but one by Walker & Boutall) with portions of an article by Morris, 'On the Artistic Qualities of the Woodcut Books of Ulm and Augsburg in the Fifteenth Century', Bibliographica, 1 (1895), 43755.

The book sold briskly before publication. 'There has been a great rush on the German woodcuts & they were all sold out last week,' Cockerell wrote to Leighton on 30 November 1897. ". . . A good many people have been left out in the cold, as orders keep coming in! I suppose it is the announcement that the Press is closing that has made people so eager' (Jc).

In another letter to Leighton, 14 December 1897, Cockerell said, 'At the last moment I have discovered a tiresome misprint in the colophon of 'Some German woodcuts' - which necessitates a cancel. This will be finished tomorrow & can be fetched on Friday' (Jc).

RELATED MATeRIALs: (a) Inc. proofs (JC). (b) Proofs of process block illustrations (cuL Syn.1.89.97). (c) Proofs of process block illustrations (BL C.102.l.22). COLoPHoN: Here ends The Story of Sigurd the Volsung and the Fall of the Niblungs, written / by William Morris. With two pictures designed by Edward Burne-Jones and / engraved by W. H. Hooper. It was printed at the Kelmscott Press, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith, and finished on the 19th day of January, 1898. / [paragraph] Sold by the Trustees of the late William Morris at the Kelmscott Press.

CLATIOn: Small 20°: 4 b-08 [p]². 10 leaves, pp. [8] [1] 2207 [208 10] [2] [=212].

PAPER & DIMENSIONS: Apple paper. Leaf: 328 × 23I mm. Text: 48 lines × 169 mm.

NOTEs: Sigurd was first published in 1877. The earliest references to the KP edition are, as usual, in Cockerel's diary.  November 1891: Saw . .a trial pagef Sgurd.   October 89:Sigur to lustrated (perhaps) by B.[urne-]J.[ones] but cannot be thought of till Chaur e 8.ul posed new folio Sigurd with B-J illustrations.'

However, only with great reluctance did Burne-Jones agree to illustrate the book. 'To pacify and please Morris I will do what I can to adorn the Volsung Tale,' hé wrote to Lady Leighton in late 1895 (Memorials, ii, 276). 'I shan't like doing it so much as Chaucer,' Burne-Jones commented to his studio assistant on 7 January 1896. 'The scenery for one thing isn't quite a painter's scenery  it's more a poet's' (Rooke diary). On 5 May 1896 he complained, 'It's quite wonderful how distasteful the task of making drawings of it is to me. . . . Terrible the tragedy of it is, and the fine things in it are so much fitter for literature than for my art, and I've no turn for the dramatic' (Rooke diary).

As a result Burne-Jones made slow progress on the illustrations (which were to number about twenty-five), and in any case Morris's death meant the abandonment of the plans for a fully illustrated folio. Thirty-two copies of two folio pages (see D1.9) were privately distributed to friends of Morris in January 1897. On the evening of 26 January 1897 Cockerell persuaded Burne-Jones to supply two illustrations for a more modest edition (Cockerell diary). Catterson-Smith redrew the designs (Cockerell diary, 6 October 1897).

'The two borders used in this book were almost the last that Mr. Morris designed. They were intended for an edition of The Hill of Venus, which was to have been written in prose by him and illustrated by Sir E. Burne-Jones. The foliage was suggested by the ornament in two Psalters of the last half of the thirteenth century in the library at Kelmscott House. The initial A at the beginning of the 3rd book was designed in March, 1893, for the Froissart [A40], and does not appear elsewhere' ('List ).

A trial page was printed on 19 May 1897 (Cockerell diary).

In a letter to N. L. McMinn, 13 February 1928 (KM), Emery Walker explained how his firm, Walker & Boutall, produced the map on the front pastedown of The Sundering Flood and similar maps in the volumes of the Saga Library: either Morris or Magnússon, he said, would submit a rough sketch that included the names of places. An employee of Walker & Boutall  in this case H. Cribb  then drew a finished design, and a line block was made from it. The original plan was to place the map in the book proper, as Cockerell reported to Jenny Morris, 28 February 1898: ". . one or two find fault with the map being pasted inside the cover of the Sundering Flood. I knew this would provoke complaints, but it was done after much consideration, as the map was found not to harmonise in style, scale, or colour with the first page of the book, opposite to which it had been intended to put it' (BL Add. MS. 52739, f. 52).

Sheet b was reprinted because a border used on it was found to be unsatisfactory (see c below).

For a discussion of the emendations made by May Morris in the KP text, see Introductions, ii, 6212.

B.

UNFINISHED BOOKS

This category includes books that were planned and announced but entually brn, boo that w y Morris but never really begun. The books that were more serously contemplated by Morris are placed first in the list.

## B1 Froissart's Chronicles

## B Shakespeare's Plays

On the fyleaf of a copy of The Heimskringla (1893) is the following note in the hand of Frederick J. Furnivall, founder of the New Shakspere Society: 'F. J. Furnival, from Wm. Morris, at his Kelmscott House, Upr. Mall, Hammersmith, on Sunday evg., 26 Feb. 1893, when he assented (provisionally) to F.J.F.'s proposal that he should print a grand Shakspere in 3 vols. folio, edited by P. A. Daniel, or F.J.F., after his Chaucer (ed. F. S. Ellis) was done' (jDC). On 23 April 1893 Cockerell recorded in his diary, 'wM is to print a big Shakespeare for Longman.' However, in a letter to Frank E. Bliss, 23 July 1894, Cockerell observed that 'the Shakespeare will not be ready for some time' (LB).

In the Britih Library .102..)there s  trial pageon Perc p per of two columns of Golden type; shoulder titles, speakers' names, and stage directions are in red. Each column is 61 lines × 104 mm. The passage printed is Macbeth, Act I, Scene viii. (Two copies of a slightly different trial page are known to be extant: one is in the Berger collection and one appeared in the Saks sale, no. 146. A trial page similar to the one in the Berger collection is reproduced as the frontispiece in Books and Letters Collected bγ William Harris Arnold of New York [Jamaica, N.Y., 1901].)

By 1894 it was decided to issue the Shakespeare in eight or ten 80 volumes at about 25/- or 3o/- per volume. On  February 1895 Cockerell wrote to F. E. Thompson: 'The Shakespeare is not yet in the press, as the Chaucer is absorbing all Mr. Morris' attention. It (the Sh.) will be a size between the Beowulf & the 8vo books unless present plans are altered' (LB).

It is not surprising that Morris never found time to complete the Shakespeare. He nce admitted to Cockerel Shakespeare d not much attract me, as I have not much sympathy with the dramatic form' (Introductions, ii, 677).

## B3 Vitas Patrum

F. S. Ellis, the editor of this projected volume, wrote in an announcement (C15) issued in about February 1894: 'This work, written by St. Jerome in the fourth century, was translated into English by William Caxton during the last years o his lie, and is stated by his servant and successor to have been finished on the day of his death.

'It was printed at Wynkyn de Worde's press in 1495. . .

'Wynken de Worde's edition, which has never been reprinted, has become so rare that a fine perfect copy of the book has been sold for as much as two hundred and fifty pounds, and can rarely be met with l ogist, the hagiologist, and the student of folk-lore.

'It is proposed to proceed with the printing of a new edition uniform with that of Caxton's Golden Iegend, issued from the Kelmscott Press in 1892, as soon as the name of one hundred and fifty subscribers have been received. In no case will more than two hundred copies be printed. It will make two large 4to volumes, and the price will be five guineas.'

Cockerell wrote to Charles F. Carter on 7 August 1895: 'Mr. Morris has abandoned the idea of printing "Vitas Patrum", as the number of subscriptions did not justify his going on with the book' (LB).

For a specimen page, see DI.S5.

## B4 Theodore Watts, The Coming of Love

In a preface to the first and second editions of The Coming of Love (1898), Theodore Watts (afterwards Watts-Dunton) wrote: 'Had it not been for the intervention of matters of a peculiarly absorbing kind matters which caused me to delay the task of collecting these verses — I should have been the most favoured man who ever brought out a volume of poems, for they would have been printed by William Morris, at the Kelmscott Press.' Parts of The Coming of Love had appeared in the Athenaeum and other periodicals, but some of the poems had been lent and lost; hence another reason for the delay in publication.

The KP edition of 2oo copies was originally planned for publication in 1894 at two guineas, to appear simultaneously with John Lane's cheaper edition (Thomas Hake and Arthur Compton-Rickett, The Life and Letters of Theodore Watts-Dunton [1916], i, 276). Even after Morris's death Cockerell called on Watts and Swinburne to discuss the book on 19 October 1896 (Cockerell diary), and the following day Cockerell drafted a memorandum on the subject (see E8).

## Catalogue  Willia ori See NOTEs for A49.

## B6 Cupid and Psyche

According to Georgiana Burne-Jones, 'One of many plans now ended [with the death of Morris in 1896] had been to publish an illustrated book of The Hill of Venus. It was to have been rewritten and have had ornamental borders made for it by Morris; the pictures, redrawn, were those designed by Edward long ago for The Earthly Paradise. The question as to whether the story should be written in verse or prose was settled by Morris saying, "Well, you know, old chap, prose loos blackr he page nd   better   p (Memorials, ii, 290).

However, after calling on Burne-Jones on 8 December 1896, Cockerell recorded in his diary: 'EB-J talked about continuing the Kelmscott Press  & wanted his Cupid and Psyche drawings to be recut & printed.' The plan was that Catterson-Smith would, as usual, redraw the designs on photographic copies, and Hooper would cut the new blocks; the wood-engravings would be accompanied by the text of the 'Cupid and Psyche'section of The Earthly Paradise, set in Troy type. Seven specimen quarto pages were printed at the kP in January 1897, and it was arranged, according to a note by Cockerell, 'that the book should be printed after the close of the Kelmscott Press'. The text area was to be 159 × 105 mm (HRC: Emery Walker papers). A memorandum between Morris's trustees, Burne-Jones, and Longmans, Green (who were to publish the volume) is dated February 1897 (wMG K.774). The project was finally abandoned after Burne-Jones's death in 1898.

## B7 The Bible

In a letter to Blunt, 4 December 189o, discussing her husband's plans for printing, Jane Morris wrote, a nice bible is much needed, but the Universities only have the right to print that' (FM[1]). 'He [Morris] has thought of printing . . . the Bible,' Cockerell noted in his diary on 1o September 1891. Mackail, in a letter to Cockerell, 6 March 1899, recalled that Morris first thought of reprinting the Authorised Version but later favoured Wycliffe's translation (BL Add MS. 52734, f. 23).

Cockerell wrote to James G. Clarke on 3 October 1894, 'Mr. Morris has sometimes spoken of printing the Bible, but it would probably be in folio, & in any case it is too big an undertaking to be thought of until the Chaucer & Shakespeare are finished' (LB).

Several extant Burne-Jones drawings, illustrating episodes in the Bible and the Apocrypha, may have been intended for this project (WMAB, p. I1; Kenneth A. Lohf, 'The Burne-Jonesiness of Burne-Jones', Gazette of the Grolier Club, ns No. 17 [May 1972], pp. 4670).

## B8 Gesta Romanorum

'The black letter fount is almost ready, and he [Morris] thinks of making a beginning with John Ball. Chaucer to follow  also perhaps "Gesta Romanorum" (Cockerell diary, 22 October 1891).

Morris owned several editions of the work (Library, nos. 41516, 8889).

## B9 Some English Mediaeval Songs and Music

So listed by Cockerell List. The book of mediaeval English pty I remember myself discussing with him [Morris] (or rather, taking part in a discussion between him & Burne-Jones) as early as 1892. But it was one of the things that he repeatedly took up & laid aside again' (Mackail to Cockerell, 6 March 1899 [BL Add. MS. 52734, f. 23]).

## B10 John Bunyan, The Pilgrim's Progress

.alke  printig The igrirogress  Berar ha suggestion)' (Cockerell diary, 10 December 1894).

## B11 A Book of Ballads

'rF..El t K.[lt] H.use] Talk bou  Book Ballads to be printed at the Kelmscott Press' (Cockerell diary, 26 January 1894). 'He [Morris] was engaged on the selection of the Ballads, w illness' ("List).

## B12 The Douce Apocalypse

On 3 September (misdated August) 1894 Emery Walker wrote, on behal  Morri, .W. icoon, Lrai he Bodi Ly, requesting permission to photograph and publish in facsimile a thirteenth-century apocalypse (ms. Douce 18o) known as the Douce Apocalypse (Bod.: Applications to Facsimile, 1889-94). On 28 November 1894 Walker and Morris spent the day at the Bodleian examining the manuscript, which had been a favourite with Morris when he was an undergraduate (Bod., L.P. entry book; note by Walker on letter from Morris to Walker, [30 October 1894] [HRc]).

## B13 The Musical Compositions of Henry vI11

Amongst other editions which he (Morris) hoped to have printed," says The Saturday Review,was a volume of musical compositions by King Henry vI11. and the English composers contemporary with him. The project originated with Sir Edward Burne-Jones, who was to have designed the woodcuts; and the music, which was to have consisted of the contents of two Mss. in the British Museum, Add. MSS. 31,922 and s,665, was to have been edited by Mr. Arnold Dolmetsch. The project was carried so far that the photographing of the Ms. was actually commenced" (Aymer Vallance, William Morris [1897], p. 414).

## B14Edward FitGrald, The Rubaiyat f OmarKayyám

Cockerell's diary reveals that he suggested this title to Morris on at least three occasions. 11 June 1891: 'Suggested that Morris should print decent editions of Omar Khayyam and of " On the Nature of Gothic" from the Stones of Venice. Morris seemed to think that he would do so, as The Golden Legend is such a big book that he will have to do some little ones after it is finished.' 21 April 1892: "Suggested that wM should print a collection of his best loved poems including Omar Khayyam.1 November 1892: 'I begged him to print Omar Khayyam, and I think he will do it.'

In 1872 Morris, with the assistance of Burne-Jones and Fairfax Murray, had produced an illuminated ms of The Rubáiyát (now in BL Add. MS. 37832).

## B15 Scott's Novels

'He [Morris] has thought of printing Scott's novels as well as Chaucer and the Bible, after he has completed printing the Golden Legend and all his own books!!' (Cockerell diary, 10 September 1891).

## B16 Dickens' Novels

In a letter to Frances Horner, probably writen in November 1893, Burne-Jones said, Morris vows he will print Dickens (if he lives e Kelscott Press ([). Morris hinte t smehigike this to an interviewer in 1895 (Ideal Book, p. 107).

## B17 John Ruskin, Unto This Last

'Morris declared his willingness to print "Unto This Last" at the Kelmscott Press . . .' (Cockerell diary, 24 March 1892). However, in a letter to Morris of 20 January 1893, George Allen, Ruskin's publisher, rejected as too expensive Morris's terms of £1 per copy for an edition of 300 copies (HEH HM 36676).

## B18 William Morris, The House of the Wolfings

'C. M. Gere, another nice young Birmingham artist, called in the aftn. with some drawings, and w.M. asked him to have a shot at llustrating the Wolfings' Cockerell diary, 1 December 1892). During 1893 and 1894 Gere produced a series of drawings, which Morris criticised in some detail. (His letters to Gere are in the Huntington Library.) Trial pages in Chaucer and Troy types are extant (BC; BL C.102.h.18; BL C.43.f.24 [∆b12]), as is a design for a border (BL C.- 43.c.26 [ΣQQ46]). Gere's drawings, annotated by Morris, are in a private collection in England.

## B19 William Morris, The Roots of the Mountains

ov.  e April 1893). A trial page in Chaucer type was struck off (BL C.102.h.18; BL C.43.f.24 [∆b12]).

## B2o Poems by Ralph Waldo Emerson

Late in 1894 F. Holland Day, of the Boston publishing firm of Copeland & Day, approached Morris about printing a selection of Emerson's poems, which would be co-published by Copeland & Day in America. In a letter to Day of 11 January 1895, Cockerell outlined Morris's terms: 16/- per paper copy and 3 10s. per vellum copy for an edition of 3oo or 350 (LB). But in the meantime Morris had agreed to print Hand and Soul (A36) for another American publisher; hence on 27 June 1895 Cockerell wrote to Day again, saying, 'I fear that there is now very little chance of the Emerson being begun this year, other things having been taken in hand' (LB).

Malory's Morte Darthur.

ADVERTISING CIRCULARS

During the first year of its existence the kp did not advertise its books, probably because most of them were fully subscribed by the date of publication. As Cockerell explained later, 'We only send out speial announcements of books which are not nearly sold out when they go to the binder' (Cockerell to W. Glaisher, 24 October 1895 [LB]). The circulars proved to be effective: Cockerell noted in his diary on 1 October 1894, for example: 'Lots of K.P. orders owing to circular sent out on Friday.' The first of the lists to include titles 'in preparation', that of 1 December 1892 (C3), was prompted by an inaccurate report in a Lonn epper,t  y Mors, the matter by arational statement of what books were in preparation' (Morris to Quaritch, 1 December 1892 [BQ].

In the following entries the circulars are printed on leaves of the Flower paper of approximately 210 × 143 mm unless otherwise indicated. The Whatman paper used for a few of the earliest circulars was stock left over from the large-paper edition of The Roots of the Mountains (189o). Title numbers not accompanied by a letter are titles listed in Section A; italic numbers indicate titles announced for the first time. Unless otherwise noted, the circulars are printed in black ink only; 'two colours' means both black and red ink are used. The Morris & Co. lists were distributed directly by Morrs & Co. to their customers rather than by the KP.

(Specimens', no. 2.) Two leaves (191 × 161 mm), Whatman paper. Two colours. Golden type. Proof: sU. Corrected (by Morris) proof and revise: BL C.102.h.18. ALREADY PRINTED: 1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6. IN THE PRESS: 7, 12, 8.

No vellum copies are mentioned. In the proof the following titles ar also listed as in preparation: ,0, 19 .

## C3 List, December 1892

AL-READY PRINTED: I, 2, 3, 4, S, 6, 7, 8, 9, I2, IO. IN THE PRESS: 13, 15, 11, 39. IN PREPARATION: 22, 14, 40.

Again, the 'Specimens' copy is on different paper. Printer's mark I and the specimens of the three type faces ('This is the Golden type. / This is the Troy type. / This is the Chaucer type.') appear for the first time. There were two issues of this list: in the second, the number of copies of The Well at the World's End (A39) was increased from 30o to 350, the publisher of Shakespeare's Poems (A11)  Reeves and Turner was added, and misspellings of C. M. Gere's name and of L'Ordene were corrected.

This final circular was issued in several forms, at least one of which was printed on Perch paper (jc). In the earlier issues more books (especially vellum copies) were listed, and the announcement about the presses was not included; these issues were essentially proofs, probably sent to only a few booksellers. On July 894, for instance, Cockerell wrote to Larner Sugden: 'I regret that Mr. Morrs has decidd not to print any leaflets in future. He has done it once ortwce to oblige personal friends, but it was found to interfere too much with her wr(LB. An F.C. Een,   Sept 184,Cee eor that Mor et hat e s not e h printing the Creed, Lord's Prayer & the Commandments in the manner pr in your letterof the th inst. He ees that the pri press is essentially for the multiplication of copies, & that when a single copy only is required it is much better to get it written out or painted' (LB). On the same date Morris, again through Cockerell, decn pr abu .rth,  tha printed instead at the Chiswick Press (LB).

## D1 Specimen Pages

This category does not include 'trial pages' prepared at the kP as an aid in designing books; by 'specimen pages' is meant pages that were distributed to booksellers, customers and friends of the Press, or the publisher of the book in question. See also A46, which is included in Section A rather than here because it was in fact published.

1 The Recuello the Historyes Troye (A8), fur pages, including the explicit (p. 1), Caxton's prologue (pp. 24), and Morris's colophon and printer's mark 1 (p. 4). (Copy examined: Bod. Kelmscott Press d.4.) Troy type. Eight-line initials, and corner and side borders. Last two lines on first page in red. Two leaves of Flower paper, each 274 × 193 mm (trimmed). Text: 33 lines × 130 mm. An entry in Proctor's notebook indicates that it was a specimen printed for Quaritch [the publisher] 1892'. Quaritch ordered 2o0o copies of the specimen (Saks sale, no. 181[24]).

The History o Godefrey  Bolone (A1s), one page (the begining of chap. cc). (Specimens', no. 8.) Troy type. Eight-line initial and three-quarter border. Chapter title in red. Óne leaf of Flower paper, 287 × 204 mm. Text: 31 lines × 125 mm. Both this and the following specimen page accompanied the Godefrey announcement (Cs), which was probably issued in April 1893.

The History f Godefre of Bolone (A1s), p. 415 (beginnig 'must nes take it on hym . . .). (Specimens', no.7.) Troy type. Six-lineiitials and side border. One leaf of Flower paper, 283 × 208 mm. Text: 32 lines × 125 mm.

3. Sidonia the Sorceress (A19), p. 12. ('Specimens', no. 11.) Golden type. Six- and ten-line initials. Title and shoulder note in red. Text: 41 lines × 126 mm. Text: 30 lines × 88 mm. Morris sent three copies of these pages to Bernard Quaritch, the publisher of the book, on 17 March 1894 (BQ), and Quaritch replied on 19 March that he was 'delighted with the specimen leaves' (HEH HM 36668).

One leaf of Perch paper, 423 × 285 mm. Text (p. 2): two columns of 63 lines × 105 mm ech. The word 'SPECIMEN. is printed in red (Golden type) in the lower let corner of p. 1. The frst page differs slightly from that of the published version in that the left column contains seven rather than six lines. The specimen pages were printed in August 1894 even though all paper copies, and all but three vellum copies, of the Chaucer had already been sold. On 30 October 1894 Cockerell wrote to Allan F. Vigers:'I regret to inform you that . . the very few spec imen pages struck off have been distributed among the leading booksellers' (LB). When Quaritch later asked for twenty-five copies of the specimen (after Morris had decided to print an additional 1oo copies of the Chaucer), Cockerell replied on 19 November 1894: 'The few specimens of the first sheet of the Chaucer struck off were distributed l    e    il hv extra 25 done for you' (LB).

Cockerell's diary indicates that the pages were issued on 22 January 1897. The pages record the form in which Morris originally intended to print Sigurd; they were distributed to friends along with the Froissart specimen pages (above).

## D2 Press Stationery and Forms

The majority of these items are undatable, but many of them appear to have been printed after Sydney Cockerell became Secretary of the Press in July 1894. Before that date, for example, Sparling seems to have carried on the kP correspondence on his personal stationery. All of the following items are printed in the Golden type.

## D3 London County Council Scholarship Certificates

On 16 January 1894 H. P. Harris wrote to Morris: 'I have been asked, to ask you if the Kelmscott Press would undertake the printing of certificates for scholarship boys etc. for the Technical Instruction Committee of the London County Council. There is a feeling that to have such things printed in the best possible way would be a good thing all round. What do you say to it? If you are disposed to take it up, I will let the proper authorities know' (cL'Morrisiana 23'). By the following autumn the Press received the first printing order. Morris is said to have complained, 'I am not a jobbing printer', but he agreed to the request because of his friendship with Dr William Garnett of the Technical Education Board (Colebrook, p. 37). Cockerell wrote to Garnett on 26 October 1894: 'I beg to acknowledge, with thanks, your order of yesterday's date for looo copies of the certificate, which shall be put in hand as soon as possible. I regret that we are unable to supply envelopes' (LB). And on 20 November Cockerell reported to

Garnett: 'The certificates have now been printed, & they will be sent you today. I hope that you will find them satifactory' LB.

Each of the certificates was printed in the Golden type, with one decorated initial, on a single leaf of Flower paper approximately 180 × 225 mm, using the frames designed by Morris for the illustrations in the Chaucer (A4o). (However, two of the certificates  nos. 22 and 28 include frames intended for the Chaucer but not printed in it.) Altogether 9942 copies of the certificates were printed. The following list is based on a table supplied by Tomkinson (pp. 127-8), the details of which 'are taken from the set of Certificates in Mr. S. C. Cockerell's Collection'; an annotated set of certificates in the 'Specimens' book; an incomplete annotated set in NYPL; entries in Proctor's notebook; and an analysis of the certificates in Proctor's hand (cl 'Morrisiana 23').

## D4 Keats Memorial Leaflet

'AN AMERICAN MEMORIAL TO KEATS.' ("Specimens', no. 3o.) Golden type, three- and ten-line initials. 2 leaves (printed on outer side of first leaf only) of Flower paper, each 207 × 144 mm. 750 copies. MS in Sir Edmund Gosse's hand and corrected proof: cL 'Morrisiana 23'.

The occasion was the unveiling of a bust of Keats by Miss Anne Whitney of Boston at Hampstead Parish Church on 16 July 1894. On 18 June, Gosse, who was to participate in the ceremony, wrote to Morris: 'I have just received from Mr Day, of the American Keats Memorial Committee, a note from Lyons asking me to send you the enclosed form of invitation, which he tell me you are going to print at the Kelmscott Press. I have copied out his rough draft, & send you the form. I presume that Mr Day has communicated fully on the subject with you already' (cL 'Morrisiana 23'). In a cancelled version of the leaflet (Specimens', no. 44), it was announced that Bret Harte would represent the donors and that Dr Alexander C. MacKenzie would perform on the organ: 'Cancelled, as neither Bret Harte, nor Dr Mackenzie were able to attend' (Cockerell's note) (cl ´'Morrisiana 23').

An accompanying invitation ("Specimens', no. 29) to the unveiling is in Golden type, with six-line initial. 2 leaves (printed on outer side of first leaf only) of Flower paper, each 102 × 144 mm. 750 copies.

## D5 Ancoats Brotherhood Leaflet

'ANCOATS BROTHERHOOD. / March, 1894, to / March 1895.' (Specimens', no. 17.) Golden type, six- and ten-line initials, side and corner borders, illustration by Burne-Jones. Two leaves of Flower paper, each 204 × 143 mm. Note by Cockerell: '250 copies, 1894' (CL Morrisiana 3 Proofs:HPL;CL'red portolo

The frontispiece of John Ball (A6) appears on p. 1; an extract from Tennyson's 'The Golden Year' appears on p. 2; a passage from Ruskin appears on p. 3; Tennyson's 'Flower in the Crannied Wall' and printer's mark 1 appear on p. 4.

The cost of production was 10 13s. (Ledger).

Morris met Charles Rowley in 1883, and, at Walker's suggestion, Rowley persuaded Morris to lecture to the Ancoats Brotherhood, a socialist workers' organization in Manchester; Morris frequently attended the Ancoats meetings thereafter (reminiscences by Rowley [BL Add. MS. 45347, ff. 936]).

## D6 Address to Sir Lowthian Bell

'TO SIR LOWTHIAN BELL, BARONET, / F.R.S., D.C.L., &C., &c., THE FOUNDER OF / THE FIRM OF BELL BROTHERS IN THE YEAR 1844 [two ornaments 3]'. ('Specimens', no. 27.) Golden type, six-line initial. Four leaves of Flower paper, each 207 × 144 mm. Proofs: BL C.43.c.26 (ΣFF 2o). Note by Cockerell: '250 on paper, 2 on vellum 1894' (cL 'Morrisiana 23').

The Address, dated 30 June 1894, appears on pp. 12; names of the officials of Bell Brothers appear on pp. 3-5; names of 'the Representatives appointed by the Workmen to make the presentation on their balappear on p.;p. 8 is blank. The occasin was the fh anniniversary of the founding of Bell Brothers by Sir Isaac Lowthian Bell (18161904) and his two brothers, Thomas and John.

For the anniversary Walter Crane also designed a folio broadside, engraved by A. Leverett, with a portrait of Sir Lowthian, a border, and a congratulatory poem, printed by Walker & Boutall on Batchelor paper (reproduced in Saks sale, no. 152).

The cost of production, not including the binding of the two vellum copies, was 3 16s. 3d. (Ledger).

## D7 Deaconess Institution Associate's Card

The Deaconess Institution for / the Diocese of Rochester, 83 / North Side, Clapham Common. / Associates' Card.' ('Specimens', no. 137.) Chaucer and Troy types, five-line initial, frame. Two leaves of unusually thick Flower paper, each 127 × 79 mm. Design for frame: CL. Proofs: jc (corrected); cL 'red portfolio'. Trial proofs: Sotheby cat., 18 December 1944, no. 194; CL 'red portfolio'. 'April 2. 1897. 250 & 10 copies. Flower [paper]:  thicknesses.' (Specimens).

The heading and rules appear on p. 1; a prayer (within a frame) appears on pp. 23; a scriptural text appears on p. 4. The frame, not used elsewhere, 'was designed for the illustrations of a projected edition of The House of the Wolfngs [B24]' (List').

Cockerell wrote to Jane Morris on 19 March 1897: 'Mrs Gilmore [William Morris's sister Isabella, who was head of the Institution] was anxious to have the associate's card of her institution printed at the Kelmscott Press, and it is now being done. Please let me know whether you wish her to be charged for it. It is a small affair, and one that might be made an "unbirthday present"of, if you think well' (BL Add. MS. 52738, f. 11).

## D8 The Wayzegoose Menus

The annual printers'dinners of the k were held at the Royal Stag Hotel, Datchet, 5 September 1892; at the Crown Hotel, Broxbourne, 8 September 1893; and at the Dumb Bell Hotel, Taplow, 31 August 1894 and 13 September 1895. Only the 1894 menu is represented in the 'Specimens' book (no. 23); in a complete set of the menus (CL

Morrisina ; another t is in PL76941) there is this note by Cockerell: 'A very few copies of these four menus were printed. The arrangement was that of the compositors and was not shown to Morris.'In each case the kP printers have introduced as many of the Kelmscott types and oraments as possible, though May Morris uotes one of the printers as complaining, . . it isn't quite what we could ave dono T ar each two leaves the venue appears on p. 1; the menu on p. ; the programme on p. 3; and printer's mark I on p. 4. The 1892 and 1893 menus are on card stock (each leaf 178 × 114 mm and 234 × 178 mm, respectively); the 1894 and 1895 menus are on Batchelor paper (each leaf 215 × 142 mm in both). All are in two or more colours.

Inserted in the list of 2 July 1894 (see C23), on which date the change became effective. Sparling resigned the Secretaryship because his marriage with Morris's daughter May had broken up.

C. Cockerell, printed at the Kelmscott Press before the type was transferred'.

7. 'IN LOVING AND REV-/ERENT MEMORY OF / HÉNRIETTA JONES, / THE WIFE OF / WILLIAM JOHN JONES / (SYLVANUS DAUNCEY) / BORN JULY 12th, 1865, / DIED MAY 24th, 1896. / “BELOVED TILL LIFE / CAN CHARM NO MORE, AND MOURNED TILL/ PITY'S SELF BE DEAD." (Copy examined: CL.) Golden type on Batchelor paper.

8. 'IN MEMORY OF THOMAS SADLER, Ph.D., / BORN JULY 5, 1822. DIED SEPT 11, 1891 [ornament 2] MINISTER FROM 1846, OF THE OLD CHAPEL / & FROM 1862 OF THE CONGREGATION MEETING HERE [ornament 2] FIVE TIMES / DURING A PASTORATE OF 4S YEARS HIS FOLD

WAS ENLARGED FOR AN / INCREASING FLOCK, DRAWN TO HIM BY THE GRACES OF HIS APOSTOLIC / CHARACTER [ornament 2] BORN OF THE SPIRIT, HE SHARED THE BLESSING OF THE PURE / IN HEART [ornament 2] THE SECRET OF A LIFE HID WITH CHRIST IN GOD WAS FELT / IN THE SERVICES OF THIS HOUSE, & SEEN IN HIS PATIENCE OF FAITH, HOPE, & / LOVE [ornament 2] VERSED IN THE SCRIPTURES & THE RECORDS OF THE CHURCH, HE / LOOKED ON THEIR HUMAN TRAITS WITH HISTORIC SYMPATHY, & ON / THEIR LIGHTS OF DIVINE TRUTH AS INTO THE OPENED HEAVENS [ornament 2] / HE LOVED THE COMMUNION OF SAINTLY SOULS CHIEFLY OF THOSE / NEAREST TO THE SIMPLICITY OF CHRIST [twO ornaments 2] IN HIS OWN HOME, / SUDDENLY, SILENTLY, ALONE, HE PASSED WITHIN THE VEIL WHERE / ALL LIVE UNTO GOD.' (Specimens', no. 64.) Note by Cockerell: 'Nov. 1894. 450 cpis'Morrisiana . Golden type.One leaf of Flower pap, 123 × 253 mm. Corrected proof: BL C.43.f.24 (∆b12). 'A slip giving the text of a memorial to Dr. Thomas Sadler, for distribution at the unveiling of it in Rosslyn Hill Chapel, Hampstead' ('List'). On 17 September 1894, Cockerell wrote to Morris & Co.: 'We can print the inscription by Thursday next, if you give the order by return of post. The size of the paper will be determined by the length that the lines make, as we have only one size of Roman type. The cost of 45o copies will be two guineas' (LB). On 2o November he wrote again: 'As the time was so short, & it might be inconvenient to print the inscription later in the week, I thought it best to put it in hand at once. A few copies had been struck off when your telegram arrived, but the correction has now been made' (LB).

9. Morris supplied specimen passages in the Golden, Chaucer, and Troy types, printed on one side only of a single leaf of Flower paper ('Specimens', no. 75), 189 × 138 mm, to be included in Edward F. Strange's Alphabets: A Handbook of Lettering with Historical, Critical and Practical Descriptions, Ex-Libris Series (London: George Bell and Sons, 1895), following p. 206. Note by Cockerell: '2ooo like this and 6o on large paper. Dec. '94.' (cL 'Morrisiana 23'). On 7 December 1894 Strange wrote to Cockerell: 'Thanks for the proof which I like very much. I have sent it on to Messrs. Bell & Sons from whom you will receive a formal order, with precise details as to size of paper &c' (HEH HM 36674). To Gleeson White, the editor of the series, Cockerell

# WILLIAM MORRIS. Died October 3rd, 1896.

![](images/008e643f2420e7ec7aab4d47bcd4bca3a466354b21d0835551e9c2013f23194b.jpg)

HE idle singer of an empty day". Thy story, poet, Otherwise we read, for lo / thy day, too early passed away, Was full of lofty thought and knightly deed.

![](images/de2c39896e5e782f3f5fed0294c95e8b20f1ebc7b037eb7f25bdfa472de26e2d.jpg)

AST on an age of change, and stress, and strife, Thy aim was still, through manhood up from youth, To beautify the meanest things of life With that rich light that flows from Art and Truth.

![](images/0d3394625e6b7f4cccfb48c2606c700d2fbb46e298dc7f494c774f291ea08c33.jpg)

A rare piece of Kelmscott Press ephemera (D12.11  reduced): a poem written and printed by the Press workers on the occasion of Morris's death. Only one copy is reported on 10 January 1895: 'The price of 2000 copies of the leaflet for Mr. Stang' book, l mple, &cp  lar pae ill be  guineas. To prevent a mistake in the latter, will you kindly send in), when you give the order. Perhaps it would be well to let me have a similar pattern for the ordinary page as well, with edge required by binders marked, as the amount of this will affect the position of the type' (LB). White did not formally place the order until 21 February 1895 (HEH HM 36675), and Cockerell sent him the leaves on 12 March (LB).

I0. Kelmscott House stationery. (Copies examined: BL Add. Ms. 45340.) 'Kelmscott House, Upper Mall, / Hammersmith, W. / Golden typeTwleaves f thinaid paper watermarkedSive Linen' each0125 mm. Morris had earlier (until about 189)used Kel done at the KP.

11. 'WILLIAM MORRIS. [two ornaments 3] / Died October 3rd, 1898, [four ornaments 3]'. (Copy examined: wC.) A twelve-line poem in Chaucer type (first line of title in Troy type), with three- and seven-line initials and a verso octavo border, on one leaf of Batchelor paper, 244 × 184 mm. On the verso is this pencilled note: 'Printed at the Kelmscott Press on the death of William Morris by some of the workers there. Chas. MacLeish.'

12. Morris supplied a specimen leaf from The Golden Legend (A7), 283 × 215 mm on Batchelor paper, printed on the recto only, for the second edition of Joseph Pennell's Pen Drawing and Pen Draughtsmen: Their Work and Their Methods (London: Macmillan, 1894), following p. 4I4. The page is headed 'OF THADUENT OF OUR LORDE.', is set in the Golden type, and contains a border and six- and ten-line initials. Pennell noted that "at the moment of going to press [his preface is dated 4 April 1894] Mr. William Morris has lent me a page of his type and decorations' (p. 414). In the list of illustrations the page was described as 'printed at a hand press on Kelmscott Press paper' (p. xxxvi). A note by Cockerell indicates that the page was reset for the occasion (Saks sale, no. 181[15]).

13. In Theodore Low De Vinne's The Practice of Typography . . . Plain Printing Tγpes (New York: Century, 1899), p. 207, there is a brief passage (recto only of a leaf) on type-design which is set in the Golden type with a ten-line initial. A note by Cockerell (BL C.1o2.h.18) offers this explanation: 'Short account of the Press written by W.M. for one of De Vinne's books on Printing. An electro was set to Mr De Vinne.'

Out of o copies as printed on vellum I propose to sell you 6at £9.9.- per copy.

## E7 Memorandum from Cockerell to Way and Williams about Rossetti's Hand and Soul (A36), 6 June 1895 (LB).

## 'Hand & Soul'

I have now gone into the cost of producing the above in the style of Mr. Morris' lecture on Gothic Architecture, but bound in vellum with a title designed by Mr. Morris, & for say a dozen vellum copies, 15/- per copy. In addition to the above numbérs Mr. Morris would reserve the right of printing 25 paper copies & 4 vellum copies for his personal use. If you accept this estimate it will remain for you to fix the selling price, & we could then arrange terms for the sale of copies in England.

In writing will you kindly say how many copies you propose to leave for the English market (perhaps 2oo would be a suitable number, leaving 30oo for America) " & whether you will be prepared to pay for the whole issue within a month of delivery.

n   e Way n Wili 9 July 8 ), cerell wrote as follows:]

I beg to acknowledge, with thanks, your letter of the 6th inst. accepting Mr. Morris' proposals for the printing of 'Hand & Soul'. The price quoted does not include carriage to America, as we could only undertake to deliver the books to your agent in London. As to selling price I think that it should not be more than 1o/- for the paper copies & 3o/- for the vellum copies, seeing how very small the book will be. On the copies sold through our agency in London, Mr. Morris would expect a discount of 25% to cover discount to trade, printing of announcement etc., thus leaving you a profit of 2/6 on each paper copy sold, if you accept the selling prices suggested above. I do not think it will   Me El Ev now elapsed.

With regard to payment, I meant within a month after the issue of the book. If you will say how many vellum copies you are prepared to take, the number will thus depend n how many we an ee ur way to dispose of over here.

Until the book is actually in the press, it would be well not to make any definite announcement to the public as to date of issue, price, number etc. though I think you may rely on having the book by November.

P.. Mr. Morris is afraid that he cannot undertake to print a larger book for you at present, as he has so many projects of his own which will occupy the press for some time.

[Final arrangements were made in a third letter from Cockerell to Way nWill g 8

I beg to acnowlee yourfvours  the h & h inst.r r. Mor' ers o he pcn  'Han & S the number of vellum copies at I5, in addition to the copies for Mr. Morris' personal use. The price does not include the cost of shipment to America, or of announcements for America. The 2oo copies for England Mr. Morris will take off your hands, paying you a premium o , & I hall e plea  s yo a  ur Ame bes in order that you may deal with them direct.

The book will be printed in red & black, & the binding will be stiff t which you reer. I am sory that Mr. Morris does not ee his w o supplying you with a specimen for your booklet.

# INDEX OF COLLECTIONS

This index provides a record of the private and public collections drawn upon in compiling this bibliography. The following items, however, are not indexed here: the unpublished diaries of William Morris (BL), Sydney Cockerell (BL), and Wilfrid Scawen Blunt (FM[I]); and the letterbook (wMG), ledger (PML), and 'Specimens' book (BL) of the Kelmscott Press. Italic letters in parentheses refer tO entries in the RELATED MATERIALS sections.

In many instances abbreviations of collections are supplied parenthetically after entries. An alphabetical list of such abbreviations will be found on pp. xiii-xvi.

# INDEX OF ASSOCIATION COPIES OF KELMSCOTT BOOKS

These items are broken down into three categories: books presented to a person (ro); books presented by a person (from); and books owned by a person (owNED). Since most of the books were presented by Morris to his friends, the second category is not included under his name. On the other hand, Kelmscott books that were added to his library at Kelmscott House ater his death and in which his bookplate appears are listed as his copies (oWNed).

Italic letters in parentheses refer to entries in the RELATED MATERIALS sections.
